### [1] [Enhancing Safety of Large Language Models via Embedding Space Separation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20206)
*Xu Zhao,Xiting Wang,Weiran Shen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Embedding Space Separation (ES2), a fine-tuning method that increases the distance between harmful and safe query embeddings in LLMs while regularizing outputs to preserve general capabilities, resulting in safer models with minimal performance loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can follow instructions very well but are vulnerable to harmful prompts. Prior work shows harmful vs. safe inputs are often linearly separable in embedding space, and attackers can exploit this to bypass safety by pushing harmful queries toward the safe region. The authors aim to turn this vulnerability into a defense by structurally separating harmful and safe embeddings, improving safety without sacrificing overall model performance.

Method: They introduce Embedding Space Separation (ES2), a representation-level fine-tuning strategy. ES2 explicitly enlarges the distance between harmful and safe latent representations in the embedding space, encouraging clear geometric separation between them. To avoid hurting the model’s general abilities, they add a KL-divergence regularization term between the logits of the fine-tuned model and those of the original base model on harmless inputs, thereby preserving the original behavior where it is already safe.

Result: On several open-source LLMs and standard safety benchmarks, the method significantly increases safety metrics (e.g., reduced harmful responses, stronger robustness to adversarial prompts) while keeping general capability metrics (such as standard task performance) roughly unchanged compared to the base models.

Conclusion: Representation-level fine-tuning that enforces greater separation between harmful and safe embeddings, combined with KL-based regularization to preserve benign behavior, is an effective way to strengthen LLM safety with minimal impact on general capabilities. ES2 offers a practical defense that leverages known geometric properties of LLM representations.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have achieved impressive capabilities, yet ensuring their safety against harmful prompts remains a critical challenge. Recent work has revealed that the latent representations (embeddings) of harmful and safe queries in LLMs typically exhibit linear separability, a property that has been exploited to construct attacks by perturbing the embeddings of harmful queries towards the safe subspace. Motivated by this observation, we propose a representation-level fine-tuning approach, named Embedding Space Separation (ES2), which improves LLM safety by explicitly enlarging the distance between harmful and safe representations in the embedding space. To prevent degradation of model's general capabilities, we introduce a Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence regularization term into the loss function, which constrains the logits of the fine-tuned model to align with those of the original base model on harmless inputs. We evaluate our method on several open-source LLMs using standard safety benchmarks. Extensive experimental results demonstrate that our approach substantially improves model safety while maintaining comparable general capabilities.

</details>


### [2] [RedacBench: Can AI Erase Your Secrets?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20208)
*Hyunjun Jeon,Kyuyoung Kim,Jinwoo Shin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: RedacBench is a benchmark to evaluate how well models can redact sensitive information according to flexible security policies while preserving useful, non-sensitive content.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing redaction benchmarks are narrow: they focus mostly on fixed categories such as PII or on specific methods like masking. Real-world security needs are broader and policy-dependent, and we lack a systematic way to measure how well models follow arbitrary redaction policies while still keeping texts useful and coherent.

Method: The authors construct RedacBench from 514 human-written texts drawn from individual, corporate, and government contexts, and pair them with 187 distinct security policies. They annotate 8,053 propositions that represent all information that can be inferred from each text. For any given policy, propositions are labeled as sensitive or non-sensitive. Models are then evaluated on their ability to remove policy-violating (sensitive) propositions while retaining non-sensitive ones, across multiple redaction strategies and using several state-of-the-art language models. Metrics separately capture security (how well sensitive info is removed) and utility (how well non-sensitive info is preserved). They also provide a web playground for interactive dataset customization and evaluation.

Result: Experiments show that stronger language models generally do better at removing sensitive information, improving the security aspect of redaction. However, these models still struggle to preserve the utility of the text: they often over-redact or distort non-sensitive information, indicating a trade-off between security and usability that current models have difficulty balancing.

Conclusion: RedacBench provides a comprehensive, policy-conditioned benchmark and tooling to rigorously evaluate and compare redaction systems across domains and strategies. It reveals that even advanced language models are far from solving the challenge of maintaining both high security and high utility in redaction, highlighting the need for methods that can more precisely follow nuanced security policies. The released dataset and playground aim to catalyze further research on controllable, policy-aware redaction.

Abstract: Modern language models can readily extract sensitive information from unstructured text, making redaction -- the selective removal of such information -- critical for data security. However, existing benchmarks for redaction typically focus on predefined categories of data such as personally identifiable information (PII) or evaluate specific techniques like masking. To address this limitation, we introduce RedacBench, a comprehensive benchmark for evaluating policy-conditioned redaction across domains and strategies. Constructed from 514 human-authored texts spanning individual, corporate, and government sources, paired with 187 security policies, RedacBench measures a model's ability to selectively remove policy-violating information while preserving the original semantics. We quantify performance using 8,053 annotated propositions that capture all inferable information in each text. This enables assessment of both security -- the removal of sensitive propositions -- and utility -- the preservation of non-sensitive propositions. Experiments across multiple redaction strategies and state-of-the-art language models show that while more advanced models can improve security, preserving utility remains a challenge. To facilitate future research, we release RedacBench along with a web-based playground for dataset customization and evaluation. Available at https://hyunjunian.github.io/redaction-playground/.

</details>


### [3] [Children's Intelligence Tests Pose Challenges for MLLMs? KidGym: A 2D Grid-Based Reasoning Benchmark for MLLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20209)
*Hengwei Ye,Yuanting Guan,Yuxuan Ge,Tianying Zhu,Zhenhan Guan,Yijia Zhong,Yijing Zhang,Han Zhang,Yingna Wu,Zheng Tian*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: KidGym is a 2D grid-based, Wechsler-inspired benchmark to systematically test multimodal large language models on five core cognitive-like abilities using diverse, customizable tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal LLMs are expanding beyond text to vision and other modalities, but current evaluations do not thoroughly or interpretably assess their general, human-like cognitive abilities. Inspired by the Wechsler Intelligence Scales used in child assessment, the authors want a principled way to decompose and test key capabilities of MLLMs, particularly in settings that resemble stages of children’s cognitive development.

Method: The authors design KidGym, a 2D grid-world benchmark consisting of 12 distinct tasks. Each task is built to stress one or more of five target capabilities: Execution, Perception Reasoning, Learning, Memory, and Planning. The environments use varied scenarios, objects, and randomly generated layouts to avoid overfitting and to better probe generalization. The framework is user-customizable and extensible, enabling the community to add new tasks and tune difficulty levels. They then evaluate several state-of-the-art MLLMs on these tasks to analyze strengths and weaknesses.

Result: Experiments with current state-of-the-art multimodal LLMs on KidGym uncover nontrivial limitations across the five capabilities, even when models perform well on more conventional benchmarks. The results yield detailed insights into which cognitive-like skills are relatively strong or weak, and how performance changes across task types and difficulty levels.

Conclusion: KidGym serves as a comprehensive, developmentally inspired benchmark for multimodal LLMs, enabling fine-grained assessment of execution, perception reasoning, learning, memory, and planning within a controlled 2D grid environment. The benchmark’s diversity, randomization, and extensibility make it a robust tool for tracking progress and diagnosing shortcomings of MLLMs. The authors make KidGym publicly available to support standardized, interpretable evaluation for the growing MLLM community.

Abstract: Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs) combine the linguistic strengths of LLMs with the ability to process multimodal data, enbaling them to address a broader range of visual tasks. Because MLLMs aim at more general, human-like competence than language-only models, we take inspiration from the Wechsler Intelligence Scales - an established battery for evaluating children by decomposing intelligence into interpretable, testable abilities. We introduce KidGym, a comprehensive 2D grid-based benchmark for assessing five essential capabilities of MLLMs: Execution, Perception Reasoning, Learning, Memory and Planning. The benchmark comprises 12 unique tasks, each targeting at least one core capability, specifically designed to guage MLLMs' adaptability and developmental potential, mirroring the stages of children's cognitive growth. Additionally, our tasks encompass diverse scenarios and objects with randomly generated layouts, ensuring a more accurate and robust evluation of MLLM capabilities. KidGym is designed to be fully user-customizable and extensible, allowing researchers to create new evaluation scenarios and adjust difficuly levels to accommodate the rapidly growing MLLM community. Through the evaluation of state-of-the-art MLLMs using KidGym, we identified significant insights into model capabilities and revealed several limitations of current models. We release our benchmark at: https://kidgym.github.io/KidGym-Website/.

</details>


### [4] [CRoCoDiL: Continuous and Robust Conditioned Diffusion for Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20210)
*Roy Uziel,Omer Belhasin,Itay Levi,Akhiad Bercovich,Ran El-Yaniv,Ran Zilberstein,Michael Elad*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes CRoCoDiL, a new way to do diffusion-based text generation by operating in a continuous semantic space instead of only on discrete tokens, yielding better text quality and much faster sampling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Masked Diffusion Models for language can be efficient but often produce incoherent text because they operate on discrete token distributions and struggle to model long-range dependencies and semantics. The authors want a method that keeps the efficiency benefits of MDMs while improving semantic consistency and generation quality.

Method: They design CRoCoDiL, a fine-tuning framework that jointly trains an encoder and a demasker so that masking and denoising are grounded in a continuous sentence-level latent space. This effectively builds an autoencoder where the decoder is an MDM. On top of this, they define two unconditional generation schemes: (1) Continuous-Then-Discrete (ConThenDisc), which first performs diffusion in the continuous latent space and then decodes via an MDM; and (2) Continuous-Within-Discrete (ConWithinDisc), which interleaves refinement in the continuous space with the discrete token diffusion process. They implement and test these ideas using LLaDA as the base model.

Result: Their methods, when evaluated in an unconditional text generation setting with LLaDA, produce higher quality text than standard MDM-based approaches and achieve over 10x faster sampling speeds.

Conclusion: Grounding masked diffusion in a continuous semantic latent space via CRoCoDiL makes diffusion-based language generation both more coherent and more efficient. The proposed ConThenDisc and ConWithinDisc algorithms show that combining continuous and discrete diffusion can substantially improve unconditional text synthesis performance and speed.

Abstract: Masked Diffusion Models (MDMs) provide an efficient non-causal alternative to autoregressive generation but often struggle with token dependencies and semantic incoherence due to their reliance on discrete marginal distributions. We address these limitations by shifting the diffusion process into a continuous sentence-level semantic space. We propose CRoCoDiL (Continuous and Robust Conditioned Diffusion for Language), a unified fine-tuning approach that jointly trains an encoder-demasker architecture, grounding the MDM demasking in continuous latent representations. This leads to the formation of a novel autoencoder in which decoding is obtained by an MDM algorithm. Relying on the same framework, we introduce two unconditional text synthesis algorithms: Continuous-Then-Discrete (ConThenDisc), a hybrid-diffusion approach that first generates latent representations in continuous space and then decodes these to tokens via an MDM, and Continuous-Within-Discrete (ConWithinDisc), a multi-diffusion strategy that refines latent representations throughout the discrete sampling process. Experiments using LLaDA show that our methods achieve superior generation quality and more than 10x faster sampling speeds in an unconditional setting.

</details>


### [5] [Fast-Slow Thinking RM: Efficient Integration of Scalar and Generative Reward Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20212)
*Jiayun Wu,Peixu Hou,Shan Qu,Peng Zhang,Ning Gu,Tun Lu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a hybrid reward model for RLHF that combines fast scalar scoring and slower chain-of-thought reasoning to improve accuracy while cutting computation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing reward models for RLHF face a trade-off: Generative Reward Models that use chain-of-thought are accurate but expensive, whereas efficient Scalar Reward Models struggle with complex judgments and adaptability. The authors want to break this trade-off.

Method: They design a Fast-Slow Thinking Reward Model (F/S-RM) inspired by Dual Process Theory. A single model is trained to support two modes: (1) a fast "first-token" prediction that outputs a scalar reward, and (2) a slow chain-of-thought based judgment. A dual-confidence activation mechanism decides, per input, whether the slow CoT reasoning needs to be triggered or if the fast scalar prediction suffices.

Result: On benchmark alignment tasks, F/S-RM improves relative performance by 1.2% over previous state-of-the-art reward models, while reducing token usage by 20.8%, indicating better cost-accuracy trade-offs.

Conclusion: A unified fast-slow reward model with adaptive activation can surpass prior state-of-the-art in both performance and efficiency, suggesting that RLHF reward modeling benefits from integrating lightweight scalar evaluation with selectively invoked chain-of-thought reasoning.

Abstract: Reward models (RMs) are critical for aligning Large Language Models via Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF). While Generative Reward Models (GRMs) achieve superior accuracy through chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning, they incur substantial computational costs. Conversely, Scalar Reward Models (SRMs) offer efficiency but suffer from limited performance and adaptability in complex scenarios.
  We introduce Fast-Slow Thinking Reward Models (F/S-RM), a hybrid RM architecture inspired by Dual Process Theory. It trains a single model to integrate two distinct reward paradigms: first-token prediction as a scalar score (fast thinking) and CoT-based judgment (slow thinking), regulated by a dual-confidence activation mechanism that determines when to activate slow thinking.
  F/S-RM achieves a 1.2% relative performance improvement over state-of-the-art models while reducing token consumption by 20.8%. Code and data will be publicly available.

</details>


### [6] [Multi-Agent Debate with Memory Masking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20215)
*Hongduan Tian,Xiao Feng,Ziyuan Zhao,Xiangyu Zhu,Rolan Yan,Bo Han*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They show that standard multi-agent debate for LLM reasoning is fragile because agents rely on flawed shared memories, and propose a simple extension where agents can mask out suspected erroneous memories each round, improving robustness and benchmark performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent debate (MAD) has become a strong paradigm for boosting LLM reasoning by letting multiple agents argue over several rounds while reusing previous context as shared memory. However, despite its success, the framework still produces and then reuses incorrect intermediate statements, which can mislead agents and degrade final answers. There is little theoretical or empirical work on how the quality of these memories affects MAD, or on mechanisms to selectively remove bad content from the debate history. The authors want to understand this vulnerability and design a method that keeps the benefits of debate while reducing the impact of erroneous memories.

Method: 1) Analyze MAD from a theoretical perspective, modeling how the quality of intermediate memories influences overall reasoning performance and showing that erroneous memories can significantly harm outcomes. 2) Propose MAD-M^2 (multi-agent debate with memory masking), a modified debate framework where, at the beginning of each round, LLM agents are given the ability/instruction to examine the previous debate transcript and label or mask parts they deem erroneous or unhelpful. 3) Construct prompts or protocols that encourage agents to preserve informative and consistent statements while removing, down-weighting, or hiding suspicious ones from the working context for the next round. 4) Evaluate the approach on standard mathematical and logical reasoning benchmarks, comparing MAD-M^2 with vanilla MAD under similar computational budgets, and perform ablation analyses of masking behavior and its impact on performance.

Result: The experiments show that MAD-M^2 consistently outperforms the baseline MAD framework on mainstream mathematical and logical reasoning benchmarks. The agents can successfully identify and mask a substantial portion of erroneous or misleading memories from previous rounds, leading to improved accuracy of final answers. Analyses indicate that polishing the debate context via memory masking yields more coherent intermediate reasoning steps and reduces propagation of earlier mistakes, confirming the theoretical insight about the importance of memory quality in MAD.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the standard multi-agent debate framework is highly sensitive to the quality of shared debate memories, and that erroneous memories can notably degrade reasoning performance. By equipping LLM agents with a simple memory-masking mechanism, it is possible to make debate-based reasoning more robust: agents filter out suspected errors and focus on high-quality context, which empirically improves results on challenging reasoning benchmarks. The authors position MAD-M^2 as an effective and lightweight enhancement to existing MAD systems, and suggest future work on more sophisticated memory selection, verification, and interaction protocols between debating agents.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have recently demonstrated impressive capabilities in reasoning tasks. Currently, mainstream LLM reasoning frameworks predominantly focus on scaling up inference-time sampling to enhance performance. In particular, among all LLM reasoning frameworks, *multi-agent debate* (MAD), which employs multiple LLMs as agents to perform reasoning in the way of multi-round debate, has emerged as a powerful reasoning paradigm since it allows agents to access previous memories to alleviate fallacious content and refine their reasoning iteratively in each debate round. However, although MAD significantly improves the reasoning capabilities of LLMs, in this paper, we observe that there remain erroneous memories, and LLM agents are vulnerable to these erroneous memories. To explore this phenomenon, we provide a theoretical insight that the performance of MAD is highly dependent on the quality of memories derived from the previous debate, indicating that the existence of erroneous memories poses a threat to the performance of MAD. To address this problem, we introduce a simple yet effective multi-agent debate framework, *multi-agent debate with memory masking* (MAD-M$^2$), to improve the robustness of MAD by allowing LLM agents to mask erroneous memories from the previous debate round at the beginning of each debate round. In this way, MAD-M$^2$ can polish the contextual information before each debate round by preserving informative and meaningful memories while discarding the erroneous memories. Extensive experiments and analyses on mainstream mathematical and logical reasoning benchmarks demonstrate that MAD-M$^2$ can identify the erroneous memories and achieve better performance in reasoning than MAD.

</details>


### [7] [Locally Coherent Parallel Decoding in Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20216)
*Michael Hersche,Nicolas Menet,Ronan Tanios,Abbas Rahimi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces CoDiLA, a method that combines diffusion language models with a small local autoregressive model to enable parallel, bidirectional, and syntactically coherent code generation with sub-linear latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard diffusion language models promise sub-linear generation latency and bidirectional context use, which are attractive for code generation and editing. However, to be fast they must predict multiple tokens in parallel, and existing approaches sample these tokens independently from marginal distributions. This ignores joint dependencies between tokens generated at the same time, causing syntactic errors and breaking multi-token structures in code, hurting practical usefulness.

Method: Propose CoDiLA (Coherent Diffusion with Local Autoregression). The core idea is to preserve DLMs’ parallel and bidirectional strengths while fixing local syntactic coherence. Instead of asking the DLM to model fine-grained syntax directly, they add a small auxiliary autoregressive model that operates over the diffusion latents. Generation is organized into blocks of tokens: blocks are generated in parallel by the DLM, while the auxiliary AR model decodes within each block sequentially to enforce local consistency. Across blocks, the DLM retains its bidirectional modeling, but within each block, the AR model ensures valid token-level syntax and multi-token structure. The auxiliary model is intentionally compact (around 0.6B parameters) to keep latency low.

Result: Experiments on code generation benchmarks show that CoDiLA removes typical coherence issues found in vanilla DLMs, such as syntactic inconsistencies and broken multi-token constructs. The method achieves a better trade-off between generation accuracy and speed than prior approaches. Using a very compact auxiliary AR model (e.g., ~0.6B parameters) is sufficient to reach this new Pareto frontier, indicating that only lightweight local autoregression is needed to substantially improve coherence while retaining sub-linear latency.

Conclusion: CoDiLA demonstrates that combining diffusion language models with a small, local autoregressive decoder over latents can reconcile parallel, bidirectional generation with syntactic and structural coherence, especially in code. This hybrid design enables sub-linear latency without sacrificing correctness, establishing a new state-of-the-art accuracy–speed trade-off for code generation. It suggests a promising direction where DLMs handle global and bidirectional aspects, while lightweight AR components enforce local structure.

Abstract: Diffusion language models (DLMs) have emerged as a promising alternative to autoregressive (AR) models, offering sub-linear generation latency and bidirectional capabilities that are particularly appealing for code generation and editing. Achieving sub-linear latency in discrete DLMs requires predicting multiple tokens in parallel. However, standard DLMs sample tokens independently from conditional marginal distributions, failing to capture the joint dependencies among concurrently generated tokens. As a result, they often lead to syntactic inconsistencies and break multi-token structures. In this work, we introduce CoDiLA (Coherent Diffusion with Local Autoregression), a method that reconciles parallel sampling with local dependency modeling. Rather than forcing the DLM to resolve fine-grained syntax, CoDiLA delegates local decoding to a small, auxiliary AR model operating on the diffusion latents. This design allows for parallel block generation while ensuring sequential validity within each block and maintaining core DLM capabilities, including bidirectional modeling across blocks. We demonstrate that using a highly compact auxiliary AR model (e.g., 0.6B parameters) effectively eliminates coherence artifacts, establishing a new Pareto frontier for accuracy and speed in code generation benchmarks.

</details>


### [8] [Expected Reward Prediction, with Applications to Model Routing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20217)
*Kenan Hasanaliyev,Silas Alberti,Jenny Hamer,Dheeraj Rajagopal,Kevin Robinson,Jasper Snoek,Victor Veitch,Alexander Nicholas D'Amour*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They use reward models not just to rank responses, but to predict in advance which LLM will on average get the highest reward for a prompt, and use this for efficient model routing.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reward models are usually used at the response level for a single model and single prompt, e.g., choosing the best of several sampled completions. However, in practice we often have a pool of models with different capabilities and computational costs, and we would like to decide which model to use for a given prompt before generating responses. There is a need for a principled, automatic way to route prompts to the most suitable model while accounting for cost, ideally by leveraging existing reward models instead of building complex routing systems from scratch.

Method: They “lift” response-level reward models to the model level by predicting the expected reward that each candidate LLM would achieve for a given prompt when sampling multiple responses. They show how to estimate this expected reward from the reward model in a straightforward way, then build a routing protocol that, at inference time, takes a prompt, predicts expected rewards for each model in a pool, and selects the model that maximizes a reward–cost tradeoff. They evaluate this expected-reward-prediction-based routing (ERP) on the open-perfectblend dataset with a pool of Llama3.1-Instruct 8B/70B, Gemma2-IT 9B/27B, and Gemma1-IT 7B models, and compare against category-based routing baselines and more complex routing protocols.

Result: ERP can accurately predict the expected reward each model would earn and these predictions are discriminative enough to support effective routing. On the open-perfectblend dataset, ERP outperforms baselines that route by picking the model with best average performance in each prompt category. The analysis also shows that more complex routing methods work largely because they implicitly approximate the same expected reward quantities that ERP estimates explicitly.

Conclusion: Response-level reward models can be repurposed to predict model-level expected rewards for prompts, enabling a simple yet effective routing strategy across a pool of LLMs. This expected-reward-based routing improves performance relative to category-based baselines, explains the efficacy of more complex routers, and scales easily as new models are added, since incorporating them only requires estimating their expected rewards from the same reward model.

Abstract: Reward models are a standard tool to score responses from LLMs. Reward models are built to rank responses to a fixed prompt sampled from a single model, for example to choose the best of n sampled responses. In this paper, we study whether scores from response-level reward models lifted to score a model's suitability for a prompt, prior to seeing responses from that model. Specifically, we show that it is straightforward to predict the expected reward that an LLM would earn from the reward model under repeated sampling. Further, we show that these expected reward predictions are precise and discriminative enough to support an application to a model routing protocol that routes prompts to models at inference time to maximize reward while controlling computational cost. We demonstrate the performance of this routing procedure on the open-perfectblend dataset, using a model pool composed of Llama3.1-Instruct 8B/70B, Gemma2-IT 9B/27B, and Gemma1-IT 7B models. Our simple expected reward prediction--based routing (ERP) outperforms baselines that route prompts to models with the best average performance within each prompt's category, and explains the success of more complex routing protocols that implicitly estimate an expected reward. Our approach has the added advantage of being trivially extensible as new models are added to the pool.

</details>


### [9] [An experimental study of KV cache reuse strategies in chunk-level caching systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20218)
*Samuel Cestola,Tianxiang Xia,Zheng Weiyan,Zheng Pengfei,Diego Didona*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes limitations of existing chunk-level caching (CLC) for retrieval-augmented generation and proposes a new combined CLC design that improves accuracy by reconciling cross-chunk attention issues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Retrieval-augmented generation relies on retrieved text chunks, and CLC speeds up inference by reusing precomputed caches. However, naive CLC ignores cross-attention between chunks, harming answer quality. Existing fixes either lose accuracy, are not generally applicable, or only partially address the issue, motivating a systematic evaluation and a better CLC design.

Method: The authors (1) empirically evaluate multiple existing CLC systems and techniques across settings to reveal their fundamental tradeoffs and limitations, particularly regarding missed cross-chunk dependencies; and (2) design a new CLC scheme that combines complementary ideas from prior methods, presumably integrating selective recomputation and attention modeling to better capture inter-chunk dependencies while keeping speed benefits.

Result: Experiments show that prior CLC methods either suffer notable quality degradation or are limited in scope. The proposed combined CLC design outperforms individual existing techniques in accuracy while preserving the efficiency benefits of caching.

Conclusion: Chunk-level caching as previously designed cannot fully solve quality loss from ignored cross-chunk attention, but techniques addressing this are complementary. By carefully integrating them, the new CLC design yields a more accurate and still efficient retrieval-augmented generation system.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation improves large language models' accuracy by adding relevant retrieved text to the prompt. Chunk level caching (CLC) accelerates inference by precomputing KV caches for these retrieved chunks and reusing them. However, these caches miss cross-attention dependencies between chunks, which can reduce output quality. Several methods try to improve CLC accuracy using different techniques. We make two main contributions. First, we show that existing CLC approaches have fundamental limitations that limit their accuracy or their applicability. We back this conclusion with an extensive CLC system experimental evaluation. Second, we observe that existing CLC techniques are complementary. We leverage this insight to propose a new CLC design that carefully combines them and achieves better accuracy.

</details>


### [10] [Thinking into the Future: Latent Lookahead Training for Transformers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20219)
*Lorenzo Noci,Gregor Bachmann,Seyed-Mohsen Moosavi-Dezfooli,Moin Nabi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new training strategy, latent lookahead, that lets language models internally ‘think ahead’ for several steps in latent space before emitting a token, improving performance on planning-intensive tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard autoregressive language models must commit to each next token with a single forward pass, cannot easily explore multiple futures, and allocate equal compute to all tokens. This is limiting for tasks that need planning, foresight, or extra reasoning on difficult steps. The authors want a way for models to internally look ahead and spend more compute where it matters, without changing the discrete output sequence or relying on non-autoregressive generation.

Method: At selected positions, instead of immediately sampling the next token from a single forward pass, the model performs τ recursive ‘latent’ forward passes: its hidden state is fed back into the context multiple times, producing τ latent predictions without sampling discrete tokens. These τ latent states are supervised using the next τ ground-truth tokens, so the model learns to internally simulate and refine its prediction over several future steps. This training procedure, called latent lookahead, effectively gives the model extra compute and an internal planning horizon for particular tokens while preserving the autoregressive interface.

Result: Models trained with latent lookahead significantly outperform standard autoregressive and non-autoregressive baselines on tasks that require planning and foresight, including maze solving, Sudoku, and the ProsQA benchmark. The improvements suggest that enabling internal multi-step latent reasoning before committing to tokens yields better structured and long-horizon reasoning.

Conclusion: Allowing language models to perform multi-step latent rollouts before emitting certain tokens improves their ability to plan and reason about future consequences. Latent lookahead offers a scalable way to reallocate compute across tokens and introduce an internal planning phase while retaining the familiar autoregressive generation setup, leading to superior performance on planning-heavy benchmarks.

Abstract: Autoregressive language models trained with next-token prediction generate text by sampling one discrete token at a time. Although very scalable, this objective forces the model to commit at every step, preventing it from exploring or reflecting upon multiple plausible continuations. Furthermore, the compute allocation across tokens is uniform; every token is formed based on a single forward-pass, potentially limiting the model's expressiveness in cases where difficult tokens require inherently more compute. Towards addressing these limitations, we introduce latent lookahead, a training strategy that enables models to "think" before generating: at selected positions in the sequence, before committing to the next token, the model performs a multi-step lookahead in latent space. More precisely, instead of sampling future tokens, we leverage the network's latent space by recursively feeding its hidden states back into the context for $τ$ steps, investing more compute on predicting that token. This produces $τ$ latent predictions that are supervised against the next $τ$ ground-truth tokens, encouraging the model to "lookahead" and refine its prediction. We show that latent lookahead substantially outperforms both autoregressive and non-autoregressive baselines on planning tasks such as maze solving, Sudoku, and ProsQA, where foresight is essential.

</details>


### [11] [Linguistic Signatures for Enhanced Emotion Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20222)
*Florian Lecourt,Madalina Croitoru,Konstantin Todorov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They analyze whether explicit linguistic features improve transformer-based emotion detection, showing consistent gains across many datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While transformer models perform well on emotion detection benchmarks, we lack understanding of what linguistic patterns signal emotions and whether such interpretable features are useful across datasets and label sets.

Method: They collect 13 English emotion datasets, extract emotion-specific linguistic signatures (high-level linguistic features/explicit lexical cues), and integrate these features into RoBERTa-based models, then compare performance with and without these features, especially on GoEmotions.

Result: RoBERTa models augmented with high-level linguistic features show consistent improvements, with up to +2.4 macro F1 on the GoEmotions benchmark, indicating better robustness and accuracy in predicting emotion categories.

Conclusion: Interpretable linguistic features capture useful emotion-specific regularities that complement transformer representations, and adding them to RoBERTa improves emotion recognition performance and robustness across datasets.

Abstract: Emotion detection is a central problem in NLP, with recent progress driven by transformer-based models trained on established datasets. However, little is known about the linguistic regularities that characterize how emotions are expressed across different corpora and labels. This study examines whether linguistic features can serve as reliable interpretable signals for emotion recognition in text. We extract emotion-specific linguistic signatures from 13 English datasets and evaluate how incorporating these features into transformer models impacts performance. Our RoBERTa-based models enriched with high level linguistic features achieve consistent performance gains of up to +2.4 macro F1 on the GoEmotions benchmark, showing that explicit lexical cues can complement neural representations and improve robustness in predicting emotion categories.

### [12] [Beyond Test-Time Compute Strategies: Advocating Energy-per-Token in LLM Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20224)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They analyze test-time compute strategies (like Chain-of-Thought prompting and Majority Voting) on Small Language Models versus larger models using the MMLU benchmark. Their analysis reveals nonlinear energy behavior due to token dynamics, and they show that energy metrics can meaningfully complement accuracy when comparing models and inference strategies.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that AI evaluation should explicitly incorporate energy efficiency metrics alongside accuracy. By using operating curves and controlled reasoning depth in Chain-of-Thought generation, systems can dynamically adjust how much reasoning to use and when to route queries to different model sizes, achieving better balance between accuracy and energy for sustainable AI deployment.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) demonstrate exceptional performance across diverse tasks but come with substantial energy and computational costs, particularly in request-heavy scenarios. In many real-world applications, the full scale and capabilities of LLMs are often unnecessary, as Small Language Models (SLMs) can provide accurate responses for simpler text generation tasks. When enhanced with advanced reasoning strategies, such as Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting or Majority Voting, SLMs can approach the performance of larger models while reducing overall computational requirements. However, these strategies can also introduce additional energy costs, creating an energy-accuracy trade-off. Our analysis examines these trade-offs in test-time compute strategies for smaller models compared to larger ones, using the MMLU benchmark. Additionally, we explore the input-output token dynamics of transformer architectures, which result in nonlinear hardware energy operation curves for LLMs. To bridge AI research with its physical impact, we propose \textit{energy efficiency metrics}, including Energy-per-Token, as complements to traditional accuracy benchmarks. This vision integrates a energy-aware routing mechanism, ensuring that model selection and inference strategies balance accuracy for sustainable AI deployment.

*Michal Olak,Tommaso Boccato,Matteo Ferrante*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build a Transformer-based sequence-to-sequence decoder for intracortical attempted-speech BCIs that jointly predicts phonemes, words, and acoustic features, plus a new calibration module (NHS) for day-to-day variability, achieving state-of-the-art phoneme and strong word decoding with interpretable attention patterns.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speech BCIs need accurate, robust, and interpretable decoders that can map neural activity to language despite limited data and day-to-day signal changes. Prior work mostly used framewise phoneme classifiers plus language models, leaving unclear how much contextual sequence-to-sequence modeling helps with sublexical decoding, robustness to nonstationarity, and understanding how neural evidence unfolds over time.

Method: They use a multitask Transformer sequence-to-sequence architecture that takes intracortical recordings from area 6v during attempted speech and outputs (1) phoneme sequences, (2) word sequences, and (3) auxiliary acoustic features. To handle day-to-day nonstationarity, they introduce the Neural Hammer Scalpel (NHS) calibration module combining a global alignment transform with feature-wise modulation. They evaluate on the Willett et al. dataset, test both direct word decoding and candidate-generation-plus-rescoring, run held-out-day generalization experiments, and analyze encoder/decoder attention patterns.

Result: On the Willett et al. dataset, the model achieves a state-of-the-art phoneme error rate (PER) of 14.3%. For word-level decoding it reaches 25.6% word error rate (WER) with direct decoding and 19.4% WER when using candidate generation and rescoring. The NHS calibration significantly outperforms linear or no day-specific transforms for both phoneme and word decoding. Held-out-day tests show that performance degrades on unseen recording days, with worse degradation for days that are temporally more distant from training data. Attention visualizations show consistent temporal chunking in encoder representations and that phoneme and word decoders exploit these chunks differently.

Conclusion: Contextual sequence-to-sequence modeling improves neural-to-phoneme decoding fidelity from intracortical speech signals and yields competitive word decoding performance. The NHS calibration module effectively mitigates day-to-day nonstationarity. Attention-based analyses provide interpretable insights, suggesting that the model segments and accumulates neural speech evidence in recurring temporal chunks that are used differently for phoneme- versus word-level decoding.

Abstract: Speech brain--computer interfaces require decoders that translate intracortical activity into linguistic output while remaining robust to limited data and day-to-day variability. While prior high-performing systems have largely relied on framewise phoneme decoding combined with downstream language models, it remains unclear what contextual sequence-to-sequence decoding contributes to sublexical neural readout, robustness, and interpretability. We evaluated a multitask Transformer-based sequence-to-sequence model for attempted speech decoding from area 6v intracortical recordings. The model jointly predicts phoneme sequences, word sequences, and auxiliary acoustic features. To address day-to-day nonstationarity, we introduced the Neural Hammer Scalpel (NHS) calibration module, which combines global alignment with feature-wise modulation. We further analyzed held-out-day generalization and attention patterns in the encoder and decoders. On the Willett et al. dataset, the proposed model achieved a state-of-the-art phoneme error rate of 14.3%. Word decoding reached 25.6% WER with direct decoding and 19.4% WER with candidate generation and rescoring. NHS substantially improved both phoneme and word decoding relative to linear or no day-specific transform, while held-out-day experiments showed increasing degradation on unseen days with temporal distance. Attention visualizations revealed recurring temporal chunking in encoder representations and distinct use of these segments by phoneme and word decoders. These results indicate that contextual sequence-to-sequence modeling can improve the fidelity of neural-to-phoneme readout from intracortical speech signals and suggest that attention-based analyses can generate useful hypotheses about how neural speech evidence is segmented and accumulated over time.

*Mahesh Kumar,Bhaskarjit Sarmah,Stefano Pasquali*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces FinBench-QA-Hallucination, a benchmark to evaluate methods for detecting hallucinations in knowledge-graph-augmented financial QA systems built on SEC 10-K filings, and shows that current methods, especially LLM judges and embeddings, work well on clean data but are vulnerable to noisy KG triplets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI-powered QA systems are increasingly used in financial information systems for tasks like compliance, risk assessment, and decision support, but hallucinated (factually incorrect) answers can cause regulatory violations and poor decisions. Existing KG-augmented QA systems lack systematic, domain-specific benchmarks and methods to detect such hallucinations, especially in high-stakes finance contexts. The authors aim to fill this gap by providing a carefully annotated dataset and evaluation framework focused on groundedness to rigorously assess hallucination detection methods.

Method: The authors construct FinBench-QA-Hallucination from SEC 10-K filings, selecting 300 pages and creating 755 QA examples. Each example is annotated for groundedness using a conservative evidence-linkage protocol that requires support from both textual chunks and extracted KG relational triplets. They then benchmark six hallucination detection approaches—LLM judges, fine-tuned classifiers, NLI models, span detectors, and embedding-based methods—under two settings: one with clean KG triplets and one with noisy triplets. They perform statistical significance testing (Cochran's Q and McNemar tests) to compare methods.

Result: On clean data, LLM-based judges and embedding-based methods achieve the strongest performance, with F1 scores around 0.82–0.86. When noise is injected into the KG triplets, most approaches suffer large performance drops, with Matthews Correlation Coefficient declining by 44–84 percent, indicating strong sensitivity to KG noise. In contrast, embedding-based methods are relatively robust, with only about 9 percent MCC degradation. Statistical tests confirm that these performance differences among methods and conditions are significant (p < 0.001).

Conclusion: The study concludes that current hallucination detection methods in KG-augmented financial QA systems are vulnerable to noisy or imperfect KG data, undermining reliability in real-world deployments. LLM judges and embeddings are promising but still sensitive to KG quality, while embedding methods show comparatively better robustness. The FinBench-QA-Hallucination benchmark offers both a diagnostic tool and a design framework for integrating hallucination detection and AI reliability evaluation into financial information systems and can generalize to other high-stakes domains such as healthcare, legal, and public-sector information systems.

Abstract: As organizations increasingly integrate AI-powered question-answering systems into financial information systems for compliance, risk assessment, and decision support, ensuring the factual accuracy of AI-generated outputs becomes a critical engineering challenge. Current Knowledge Graph (KG)-augmented QA systems lack systematic mechanisms to detect hallucinations - factually incorrect outputs that undermine reliability and user trust. We introduce FinBench-QA-Hallucination, a benchmark for evaluating hallucination detection methods in KG-augmented financial QA over SEC 10-K filings. The dataset contains 755 annotated examples from 300 pages, each labeled for groundedness using a conservative evidence-linkage protocol requiring support from both textual chunks and extracted relational triplets. We evaluate six detection approaches - LLM judges, fine-tuned classifiers, Natural Language Inference (NLI) models, span detectors, and embedding-based methods under two conditions: with and without KG triplets. Results show that LLM-based judges and embedding approaches achieve the highest performance (F1: 0.82-0.86) under clean conditions. However, most methods degrade significantly when noisy triplets are introduced, with Matthews Correlation Coefficient (MCC) dropping 44-84 percent, while embedding methods remain relatively robust with only 9 percent degradation. Statistical tests (Cochran's Q and McNemar) confirm significant performance differences (p < 0.001). Our findings highlight vulnerabilities in current KG-augmented systems and provide insights for building reliable financial information systems, where hallucinations can lead to regulatory violations and flawed decisions. The benchmark also offers a framework for integrating AI reliability evaluation into information system design across other high-stakes domains such as healthcare, legal, and government.

The dataset consists of 46397 audio samples collected from children aged 3 - 12 years, covering 141 classes. All samples were recorded under controlled specifications to ensure consistency in duration, sampling rate, and format. To address high intra-class similarity among Arabic phonemes and the limited samples per class, we propose a hierarchical audio classification based on CNN-LSTM architectures. Our proposed methodology decomposes alphabet recognition into a two-stage process: an initial grouping classification model followed by specialized classifiers for each group. Both strategies: static linguistic-based grouping and dynamic clustering-based grouping, were evaluated. Experimental results demonstrate that static linguistic-based grouping achieves superior performance. Comparisons between traditional machine learning with deep learning approaches, highlight the effectiveness of CNN-LSTM models combined with data augmentation. Despite achieving promising results, most of our experiments indicate a challenge with overfitting, which is likely due to the limited number of samples, even after data augmentation and model regularization. Thus, future work may focus on collecting additional data to address this issue. Abjad-Kids will be publicly available. *Tianshu Zhang,Huan Sun*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes SciNav, a structured LLM-based science agent that uses relative judgments in a tree-search over code solutions to improve performance on scientific coding benchmarks under limited search budgets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based autonomous science agents are mostly applied to open-ended tasks with subjective, hard-to-evaluate outputs. In contrast, scientific coding benchmarks allow objective evaluation, but current methods for these tasks are ad-hoc, engineering-heavy pipelines without a principled, end-to-end agent framework. There is a need for a more systematic approach that can efficiently explore solution spaces under constrained computational budgets and improve over direct prompting and existing agents.

Method: Introduce SciNav (Scientific Navigator), an LLM-based agent framework tailored to scientific coding tasks. SciNav formulates solution exploration as a tree search, where nodes represent candidate code solutions or partial solutions. Instead of relying on absolute LLM scores, it uses pairwise relative judgments between candidate solutions to decide which branches are more promising. At each step, the agent selects the top-K branches to continue exploring and prunes low-potential ones, iteratively refining and narrowing down candidates using these comparative evaluations, all under a constrained search budget.

Result: On two scientific coding benchmarks spanning different task types and difficulty levels, SciNav consistently outperforms direct prompting and prior agent-based systems such as OpenHands and Self-Debug across multiple base LLMs. It also surpasses simpler comparators including random branch selection and methods relying on absolute LLM scoring for candidate evaluation, indicating that its relative judgment-guided search yields higher-quality code solutions.

Conclusion: Relative judgment-guided top-K tree search is an effective strategy for scientific coding agents, enabling better use of limited search budgets and yielding superior performance to existing methods. SciNav demonstrates that structured, end-to-end agent designs that exploit comparative evaluations can substantially advance the practicality and reliability of autonomous science agents for objectively testable tasks like scientific coding.

Abstract: Autonomous science agents built on large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used to generate hypotheses, design experiments, and produce reports. However, prior work mainly targets open-ended scientific problems with subjective outputs that are difficult to evaluate. Scientific coding benchmarks, by contrast, provide executable outputs for objective assessment. Existing approaches remain engineering-driven pipelines, revealing the need for structured, end-to-end science agent frameworks for scientific coding tasks. We address this gap by focusing on scientific coding tasks, where evaluation can be made rigorously, and introducing an agent framework SciNav (Scientific Navigator) that enables more effective solution exploration. Our framework is designed to operate under constrained search budgets, moving beyond reliance on pre-defined success metrics and prolonged search cycles. Inspired by findings that comparative judgments often reveal finer-grained quality differences and therefore provide greater discriminative power than absolute scoring, our framework leverages pairwise relative judgments within a tree search process to select top-K promising solution branches, prune low-potential ones, and progressively narrow down the solution candidates on the selected branches guided by relative comparisons. We demonstrate our agent's effectiveness across different types of tasks on two benchmarks. Experiments show that SciNav significantly outperforms direct prompting and prior agents like OpenHands and Self-Debug across different base models, task types, and difficulty levels, and exceeds different frontier comparators such as random selection and LLM absolute scoring. These results confirm the strength of our agent design and highlight the effectiveness of relative judgment-guided top-K search for high-quality scientific coding, marking a step toward more practical science agents.

### [17] [The production of meaning in the processing of natural language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20381)
*Christopher J. Agostino,Quan Le Thien,Nayan D'Souza,Louis van der Elst*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models exhibit quantum-like contextuality in semantic processing, using the CHSH |S| parameter, and shows that this property is largely independent of standard performance metrics while having implications for prompt injection defenses and large-scale manipulation via control of context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the mechanisms of meaning-making in language models and humans, and to investigate whether contextuality in semantic processing, previously observed in cognitive science, also appears systematically in large language models and how it relates to common benchmarks and safety concerns.

Method: The authors analyze violations of the Bell inequality via the CHSH |S| parameter in LLMs across a wide range of model scales (four orders of magnitude). They measure the distribution of |S| and its interquartile range, and compare these with external benchmarks such as MMLU scores, hallucination rates, and nonsense detection. They also examine how |S| depends on sampling parameters and word order, and interpret the results through an information-theoretic lens.

Result: They find that the interquartile range of the |S| distribution is the most discriminative statistic between models but is orthogonal to MMLU, hallucination rate, and nonsense detection benchmarks. The rate at which models violate the Bell inequality shows only a weak, statistically non-significant anticorrelation with these benchmarks. They further characterize how |S| changes with different sampling settings and word order manipulations.

Conclusion: Contextuality, as quantified by CHSH |S| in LLMs, appears to be a fundamental property of their semantic processing that is largely independent of conventional performance metrics. Genuine contextuality imposes information-theoretic constraints on defenses against prompt injection and suggests a powerful avenue for large-scale influence by shaping social and linguistic context, effectively manufacturing contextuality and thereby constraining possible interpretations before specific meanings are formed.

Abstract: Understanding the fundamental mechanisms governing the production of meaning in the processing of natural language is critical for designing safe, thoughtful, engaging, and empowering human-agent interactions. Experiments in cognitive science and social psychology have demonstrated that human semantic processing exhibits contextuality more consistent with quantum logical mechanisms than classical Boolean theories, and recent works have found similar results in large language models -- in particular, clear violations of the Bell inequality in experiments of contextuality during interpretation of ambiguous expressions. We explore the CHSH $|S|$ parameter -- the metric associated with the inequality -- across the inference parameter space of models spanning four orders of magnitude in scale, cross-referencing it with MMLU, hallucination rate, and nonsense detection benchmarks. We find that the interquartile range of the $|S|$ distribution -- the statistic that most sharply differentiates models from one another -- is completely orthogonal to all external benchmarks, while violation rate shows weak anticorrelation with all three benchmarks that does not reach significance. We investigate how $|S|$ varies with sampling parameters and word order, and discuss the information-theoretic constraints that genuine contextuality imposes on prompt injection defenses and its human analogue, whereby careful construction and maintenance of social contextuality can be carried out at scale -- manufacturing not consent but contextuality itself, a subtler and more fundamental form of manipulation that shapes the space of possible interpretations before any particular one is reached.

### [18] [Coding Agents are Effective Long-Context Processors](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20432)
*Weili Cao,Xunjian Yin,Bhuwan Dhingra,Shuyan Zhou*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They replace opaque long-context attention in LLMs with explicit code-driven interaction over a file system, using coding agents to manage and query massive text corpora, achieving strong gains on long-context benchmarks.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors want a more scalable and controllable way to process huge text corpora (up to trillions of tokens) without relying solely on bigger context windows or semantic search.

Method: Use off-the-shelf frontier coding agents as the main interface to text. The agent organizes documents into a file-system-like structure and uses native tools (code execution, terminal commands, file operations) to search, slice, and manipulate text. They apply this to several long-context tasks—long-context reasoning, retrieval-augmented generation, and open-domain QA—over extremely large corpora, and compare against existing long-context and retrieval baselines.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks, the coding-agent approach beats prior published state-of-the-art by an average of 17.3%. It successfully handles corpora as large as three trillion tokens, showing robust performance where standard long-context LLM approaches degrade.

Conclusion: Explicit, tool-based long-context handling via coding agents is a powerful alternative to scaling attention windows or relying only on semantic search. The key advantages are the agents’ proficiency with native tools and their ability to treat huge text collections as navigable file systems. This suggests future long-context LLM systems should offload much of the context management to such coding agents rather than embedding everything into a single model context.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) have demonstrated remarkable progress in scaling to access massive contexts. However, the access is via the latent and uninterpretable attention mechanisms, and LLMs fail to effective process long context, exhibiting significant performance degradation as context length increases. In this work, we study whether long-context processing can be externalized from latent attention into explicit, executable interactions, by allowing coding agents to organize text in file systems and manipulate it using its native tools. We evaluate off-the-shelf frontier coding agents as the general interface for tasks that require processing long contexts, including long-context reasoning, retrieval-augmented generation, and open-domain question answering with large-scale corpus contains up to three trillion tokens. Across multiple benchmarks, these agents outperform published state-of-the-art by 17.3% on average. We attribute this efficacy to two key factors: native tool proficiency, which enables agents to leverage executable code and terminal commands rather than passive semantic queries, and file system familiarity, which allows them to navigate massive text corpora as directory structures. These findings suggest that delegating long-context processing to coding agents offers an effective alternative to semantic search or context window scaling, opening new directions for long-context processing in LLMs.

### [19] [A Training-Free Regeneration Paradigm: Contrastive Reflection Memory Guided Self-Verification and Self-Improvement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20441)
*Yuran Li,Di Wu,Benoit Boulet*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a training-free, verification-guided regeneration method using an offline contrastive Reflection Memory to improve LLM reasoning accuracy efficiently.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing verification-guided self-improvement methods for LLMs face an efficiency–accuracy trade-off: iterative verification-rectification is computationally costly and can get stuck in incorrect reasoning loops, whereas best-of-N sampling needs many candidate generations without actually correcting the model’s internal reasoning flaws. There is a need for an approach that provides corrective guidance, avoids repeated or massive sampling, and breaks out of faulty reasoning with low computational overhead.

Method: The authors build an offline-curated contrastive Reflection Memory (RM) that stores examples of correct and incorrect reasoning. At inference, the model first performs RM-guided self-verification by comparing its initial reasoning against entries in RM to detect flaws and obtain corrective feedback. Then, instead of iteratively editing the original answer or sampling many candidates, the model performs a single regeneration from scratch, again guided by RM, using the retrieved reflections as constraints or hints. The process is training-free and uses only one verification and one regeneration step, eliminating iterative loops and large-N sampling.

Result: Across nine benchmarks covering algorithmic tasks, general reasoning, symbolic problems, and domain-specific tasks, and tested on both small and large LLMs, the proposed RM-guided verification-plus-single-regeneration consistently improves accuracy over prior verification-guided and best-of-N methods while using comparable or lower inference-time compute.

Conclusion: A training-free, RM-guided regeneration paradigm can effectively enhance LLM reasoning accuracy without expensive iterative correction or large-scale sampling. Leveraging an offline contrastive reflection memory for both verification and a single regeneration step yields better accuracy–efficiency trade-offs than prior verification-guided self-improvement methods, suggesting that structured external reflections are a powerful tool for correcting LLM reasoning at inference time.

Abstract: Verification-guided self-improvement has recently emerged as a promising approach to improving the accuracy of large language model (LLM) outputs. However, existing approaches face a trade-off between inference efficiency and accuracy: iterative verification-rectification is computationally expensive and prone to being trapped in faulty reasoning, while best-of-N selection requires extensive sampling without addressing internal model flaws. We propose a training-free regeneration paradigm that leverages an offline-curated contrastive Reflection Memory (RM) to provide corrective guidance, while regenerating from scratch helps break out of faulty reasoning. At inference time, the method performs RM-guided self-verification followed by a single RM-guided regeneration, avoiding both iterative correction and multi-sample selection. We evaluated our method on nine benchmarks that span algorithmic, reasoning, symbolic, and domain-specific tasks in both small- and large-scale LLMs. Experiment results show that our method outperforms prior methods while maintaining low computational cost.

Abstract: A number of scientific conferences and journals have recently enacted policies that prohibit LLM usage by peer reviewers, except for polishing, paraphrasing, and grammar correction of otherwise human-written reviews. But, are these policies enforceable? To answer this question, we assemble a dataset of peer reviews simulating multiple levels of human-AI collaboration, and evaluate five state-of-the-art detectors, including two commercial systems. Our analysis shows that all detectors misclassify a non-trivial fraction of LLM-polished reviews as AI-generated, thereby risking false accusations of academic misconduct. We further investigate whether peer-review-specific signals, including access to the paper manuscript and the constrained domain of scientific writing, can be leveraged to improve detection. While incorporating such signals yields measurable gains in some settings, we identify limitations in each approach and find that none meets the accuracy standards required for identifying AI use in peer reviews. Importantly, our results suggest that recent public estimates of AI use in peer reviews through the use of AI-text detectors should be interpreted with caution, as current detectors misclassify mixed reviews (collaborative human-AI outputs) as fully AI generated, potentially overstating the extent of policy violations.

### [21] [Diffutron: A Masked Diffusion Language Model for Turkish Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20466)
*Şuayp Talha Kocabay,Talha Rüzgar Akkuş*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Diffutron, a compact masked diffusion language model tailored for Turkish that achieves competitive results with much larger baselines through efficient training and multi-stage instruction tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Masked diffusion language models are promising non-autoregressive alternatives to standard LLMs, but they have been underexplored for morphologically rich languages like Turkish. There is a need for efficient, smaller models that can handle such languages while still performing competitively with very large multilingual models.

Method: The authors design Diffutron, a Turkish-focused masked diffusion language model. They first apply LoRA-based continual pre-training to a multilingual encoder using a large Turkish corpus, which is resource-efficient. Then they add generative capabilities through a progressive instruction-tuning pipeline: the model is tuned sequentially on general-purpose instructions and then on more task-specific instruction sets, yielding a non-autoregressive generative system for Turkish.

Result: On a broad set of benchmarks, Diffutron, despite being relatively small, matches or approaches the performance of existing multi-billion-parameter multilingual baselines, indicating strong efficiency and effectiveness for Turkish generation tasks.

Conclusion: The work shows that combining masked diffusion modeling with a staged tuning strategy (continual pre-training plus progressive instruction tuning) can yield a compact yet competitive non-autoregressive language model for a morphologically rich language like Turkish. This validates MDLMs plus multi-stage tuning as a viable path for efficient generation in such languages.

Abstract: Masked Diffusion Language Models (MDLMs) have emerged as a compelling non-autoregressive alternative to standard large language models; however, their application to morphologically rich languages remains limited. In this paper, we introduce $\textit{Diffutron}$, a masked diffusion language model specifically designed for Turkish. Our approach leverages a resource-efficient training pipeline, starting with LoRA-based continual pre-training of a multilingual encoder on a large-scale corpus. To enable generative capabilities, we employ a progressive instruction-tuning strategy, sequentially adapting the model on general and task-specific instruction sets. Experimental results across comprehensive benchmarks demonstrate that, despite its compact size, our model achieves competitive performance compared to existing multi-billion-parameter baselines. These findings validate the effectiveness of masked diffusion modeling combined with multi-stage tuning for non-autoregressive text generation in Turkish.

### [24] [Permutation-Consensus Listwise Judging for Robust Factuality Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20562)
*Tianyi Huang,Nathan Huang,Justin Tang,Wenqian Chen,Elsa Fan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes PCFJudge, a method that reduces order sensitivity when LLMs act as listwise factuality judges by averaging judgments across multiple permutations of candidate answers, significantly improving evaluation reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs used as judges for evaluating answer factuality are unstable: their decisions can change when the order of candidate answers is rearranged, even though this should be irrelevant. This order sensitivity is particularly problematic in listwise factuality settings where multiple polished answers may differ mainly in hallucination risk. There is a need for a practical method to reduce this nuisance variation and make LLM-based evaluation more reliable and robust.

Method: The authors introduce PCFJudge, an inference-time technique that repeatedly applies the same listwise factuality-judging prompt to multiple permutations of the same set of candidate answers. For each permutation, the LLM outputs scores, rankings, and uncertainty signals for all candidates. PCFJudge then aggregates these outputs across permutations into a single consensus decision, effectively averaging out order-induced variability. They perform ablations to separate the impact of permutation consensus from additional arbitration or aggregation layers.

Result: On the RewardBench 2 Factuality benchmark, PCFJudge yields up to a 7-point absolute improvement over standard, single-shot direct judging by an LLM. The ablation studies indicate that most of the performance gains come from the permutation consensus mechanism itself, while heavier arbitration components contribute relatively little beyond that.

Conclusion: The study concludes that a substantial portion of LLM factuality-judging error is due to instability caused by the order in which candidate answers are presented. By averaging judgments across multiple permutations, PCFJudge effectively mitigates this issue and provides a simple, inference-time-only way to make LLM-based factuality evaluation more accurate and reliable, without requiring retraining of the models.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are now widely used as judges, yet their decisions can change under presentation choices that should be irrelevant. We study one such source of instability: candidate-order sensitivity in listwise factuality evaluation, where several answers can look similarly polished while differing sharply in hallucination risk. We introduce PCFJudge, an inference-time method that reruns the same factuality-first listwise prompt over multiple orderings of the same candidate set and aggregates the resulting scores, ranks, and uncertainty signals into a single consensus decision. On RewardBench 2 Factuality, PCFJudge improves over direct judging by up to 7 absolute points. Development ablations show that the dominant gain comes from permutation consensus itself rather than from heavier arbitration layers. These results suggest that a meaningful share of factuality-judging error arises from order instability, and that averaging over this nuisance variation is a simple and effective way to make LLM evaluation more reliable.

In contrast, the same models perform better on adapted English benchmarks. JUBAKU is reliable, human-intuitive, and highlights the need for localized, culturally aware bias evaluation for LLMs beyond English.

Existing evaluations of social bias in large language models (LLMs) for non-English contexts, however, often rely on translations of English benchmarks. Such benchmarks fail to reflect local cultural norms, including those found in Japanese. For instance, Western benchmarks may overlook Japan-specific stereotypes related to hierarchical relationships, regional dialects, or traditional gender roles. To address this limitation, we introduce Japanese cUlture adversarial BiAs benchmarK Under handcrafted creation (JUBAKU), a benchmark tailored to Japanese cultural contexts. JUBAKU uses adversarial construction to expose latent biases across ten distinct cultural categories. Unlike existing benchmarks, JUBAKU features dialogue scenarios hand-crafted by native Japanese annotators, specifically designed to trigger and reveal latent social biases in Japanese LLMs. We evaluated nine Japanese LLMs on JUBAKU and three others adapted from English benchmarks. All models clearly exhibited biases on JUBAKU, performing below the random baseline of 50% with an average accuracy of 23% (ranging from 13% to 33%), despite higher accuracy on the other benchmarks. Human annotators achieved 91% accuracy in identifying unbiased responses, confirming JUBAKU's reliability and its adversarial nature to LLMs.
