### [26] [A Modular LLM Framework for Explainable Price Outlier Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20636)
*Shadi Sartipi,John Wu,Sina Ghotbi,Nikhita Vedula,Shervin Malmasi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: An LLM-based multi-stage agent framework flags product price outliers by comparing each item to similar products and achieves high agreement with human auditors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical price outlier detection methods in retail and e-commerce are simplistic, usually relying on fixed thresholds or basic statistics, and they ignore semantic relationships among product attributes. This leads to missed or misclassified erroneous prices, which harms competitiveness, revenue, and consumer trust. There is a need for a more nuanced, explainable approach that can understand product similarities and attribute-driven price differences, closer to how human auditors reason.

Method: The paper introduces an agentic LLM framework that treats price outlier detection as a structured reasoning problem executed in three stages. (i) Relevance classification: using product descriptions and attributes, the system identifies price-relevant similar products to serve as comparators. (ii) Relative utility assessment: for each similar product, the LLM evaluates the target item along key price-influencing dimensions (such as brand, size, and features) and compares their utilities. (iii) Reasoning-based decision: the model aggregates these comparison-based justifications into an explainable decision on whether the target price is an outlier, including accompanying reasoning traces. The approach is evaluated against zero-shot and retrieval-based LLM baselines, and additional ablation studies probe the impact of hyper-parameters and agreement thresholds.

Result: The framework achieves over 75% agreement with human auditors on a test dataset, surpassing zero-shot and retrieval-augmented LLM baselines in accuracy. Ablation experiments reveal how performance depends on specific hyper-parameters (e.g., thresholds, similarity criteria, or aggregation rules) and demonstrate that the system can be tuned for varying trade-offs between accuracy and level of agreement with auditors.

Conclusion: Treating price outlier detection as a multi-step reasoning task with LLM agents and explicit comparison to similar products leads to more accurate and explainable detection of erroneous or anomalous prices than simpler or naive LLM approaches. The method is flexible and tunable, allowing adaptation to different operational requirements and desired levels of alignment with human auditors, making it a promising solution for retail and e-commerce price quality control.

Abstract: Detecting product price outliers is important for retail and e-commerce stores as erroneous or unexpectedly high prices adversely affect competitiveness, revenue, and consumer trust. Classical techniques offer simple thresholds while ignoring the rich semantic relationships among product attributes. We propose an agentic Large Language Model (LLM) framework that treats outlier price flagging as a reasoning task grounded in related product detection and comparison. The system processes the prices of target products in three stages: (i) relevance classification selects price-relevant similar products using product descriptions and attributes; (ii) relative utility assessment evaluates the target product against each similar product along price influencing dimensions (e.g., brand, size, features); (iii) reasoning-based decision aggregates these justifications into an explainable price outlier judgment. The framework attains over 75% agreement with human auditors on a test dataset, and outperforms zero-shot and retrieval based LLM techniques. Ablation studies show the sensitivity of the method to key hyper-parameters and testify on its flexibility to be applied to cases with different accuracy requirement and auditor agreements.

### [27] [Hear Both Sides: Efficient Multi-Agent Debate via Diversity-Aware Message Retention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20640)
*Manh Nguyen,Anh Nguyen,Dung Nguyen,Svetha Venkatesh,Hung Le*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Diversity-Aware Retention (DAR), a method for multi-agent debate that selectively broadcasts only the most disagreeing agent messages each round to reduce noise and improve reasoning performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-agent debate methods broadcast all agents’ messages each round, which creates noise, redundancy, and computational waste, especially as the number of agents grows. Attempts to filter messages via confidence-based uncertainty estimation are unreliable because large language models have miscalibrated confidence scores and are sensitive to threshold choices. There is a need for a more robust way to decide which messages should be shared among agents to enhance debate quality rather than degrade it.

Method: The authors propose Diversity-Aware Retention (DAR), a lightweight framework layered on top of multi-agent debate. At each debate round, the system computes which agent responses disagree most with each other and with the current majority vote. Instead of broadcasting all messages, DAR retains and broadcasts only this maximally disagreeing subset. The mechanism is index-based: it does not alter message content but only selects which existing messages to propagate. This preserves authenticity of disagreements while reducing redundancy and noise. DAR is then applied and evaluated across different reasoning and QA benchmarks with varying numbers of agents.

Result: Experiments across several reasoning and question answering benchmarks show that DAR’s selective message propagation improves the final debate outcomes compared with standard broadcast-all and uncertainty-filtering baselines. The gains are especially notable when the number of agents is large, a setting where naïve broadcasting leads to severe noise accumulation and degraded performance. DAR maintains or improves accuracy while being more computationally efficient, since fewer messages are processed and propagated each round.

Conclusion: The study concludes that controlling which messages are heard in a multi-agent debate is crucial for effective collective reasoning. Diversity-Aware Retention offers a simple, content-preserving way to select maximally disagreeing messages, reducing noise and redundancy and improving debate performance, particularly at scale. This suggests that future multi-agent reasoning systems should focus not only on generating better agent utterances but also on designing principled mechanisms for selective communication and message routing.

Abstract: Multi-Agent Debate has emerged as a promising framework for improving the reasoning quality of large language models through iterative inter-agent communication. However, broadcasting all agent messages at every round introduces noise and redundancy that can degrade debate quality and waste computational resources. Current approaches rely on uncertainty estimation to filter low-confidence responses before broadcasting, but this approach is unreliable due to miscalibrated confidence scores and sensitivity to threshold selection. To address this, we propose Diversity-Aware Retention (DAR), a lightweight debate framework that, at each debate round, selects the subset of agent responses that maximally disagree with each other and with the majority vote before broadcasting. Through an explicit index-based retention mechanism, DAR preserves the original messages without modification, ensuring that retained disagreements remain authentic. Experiments on diverse reasoning and question answering benchmarks demonstrate that our selective message propagation consistently improves debate performance, particularly as the number of agents scales, where noise accumulation is most severe. Our results highlight that what agents hear is as important as what agents say in multi-agent reasoning systems.

### [28] [Weber's Law in Transformer Magnitude Representations: Efficient Coding, Representational Geometry, and Psychophysical Laws in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20642)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how transformer language models internally represent numerical and other magnitudes, finding that their internal geometry is logarithmic (log-compressive) but this representation does not by itself lead to human-like magnitude judgments or behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is conflicting evidence on whether transformer language models encode numerical magnitude in a logarithmic, linear, or digit-wise manner. Understanding this matters both for cognitive science (comparing models to humans) and for AI interpretability and capability assessment. The authors want a principled, quantitative way to resolve these disagreements and to test whether internal representations actually support magnitude-sensitive behavior.

Method: They adapt formal tools from psychophysics and apply four complementary analysis paradigms—(1) representational similarity analysis comparing model activations to Weber-law dissimilarity predictions, (2) behavioral discrimination tasks where the model chooses the larger magnitude, (3) analysis of precision gradients akin to Weber fractions, and (4) causal interventions on layers/units to test their role in magnitude processing. These are run over three types of magnitude (number, time, space) in three different 7–9B instruction-tuned transformers from distinct architecture families (Llama, Mistral, Qwen). They also analyze corpus statistics to test efficient-coding assumptions.

Result: (1) Across 96 combinations of model, domain, and layer, the internal representational geometry follows a log-compressive pattern: similarity of activations matches a Weber-law (log-based) dissimilarity matrix extremely well (correlations .68–.96), with no cases where a linear code fits better. (2) This geometric property is not tightly coupled to behavior: one model achieves a human-like Weber fraction (~0.20) on number discrimination, the other does not, and both fail (near chance) on time and space discrimination tasks despite showing logarithmic geometry for those domains. (3) Causal interventions show that early layers are specifically involved in magnitude processing (about 4.1× more than expected), while later layers—which exhibit the strongest log geometry—are only weakly causally involved (~1.2×). (4) Corpus analysis shows that the statistics of magnitudes in the training data satisfy an efficient-coding precondition (power-law with alpha ≈ 0.77).

Conclusion: Transformer LMs naturally develop log-compressive magnitude representations as a consequence of training data statistics and efficient coding, consistent with Weber’s law. However, having such a geometric structure in their internal states is not sufficient for robust, human-like magnitude-based behavior, and the layers where the geometry is clearest are not the ones most causally responsible for magnitude judgments. This challenges simple mappings between representational geometry and cognitive competence and suggests that downstream readout and task supervision are crucial for turning geometric structure into usable behavior.

Abstract: How do transformer language models represent magnitude? Recent work disagrees: some find logarithmic spacing, others linear encoding, others per-digit circular representations. We apply the formal tools of psychophysics to resolve this. Using four converging paradigms (representational similarity analysis, behavioural discrimination, precision gradients, causal intervention) across three magnitude domains in three 7-9B instruction-tuned models spanning three architecture families (Llama, Mistral, Qwen), we report three findings. First, representational geometry is consistently log-compressive: RSA correlations with a Weber-law dissimilarity matrix ranged from .68 to .96 across all 96 model-domain-layer cells, with linear geometry never preferred. Second, this geometry is dissociated from behaviour: one model produces a human-range Weber fraction (WF = 0.20) while the other does not, and both models perform at chance on temporal and spatial discrimination despite possessing logarithmic geometry. Third, causal intervention reveals a layer dissociation: early layers are functionally implicated in magnitude processing (4.1x specificity) while later layers where geometry is strongest are not causally engaged (1.2x). Corpus analysis confirms the efficient coding precondition (alpha = 0.77). These results suggest that training data statistics alone are sufficient to produce log-compressive magnitude geometry, but geometry alone does not guarantee behavioural competence.

</details>


### [29] [PAVE: Premise-Aware Validation and Editing for Retrieval-Augmented LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20673)
*Tianyi Huang,Caden Yang,Emily Yin,Eric Wang,Michael Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes PAVE, an inference-time validation and editing layer that improves the consistency and accuracy of retrieval-augmented language models by explicitly checking whether retrieved evidence supports the model’s draft answer, and revising unsupported outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Retrieval-augmented language models can access relevant evidence but still hallucinate or prematurely commit to answers that are not actually supported by the retrieved context. Current systems typically lack a fine-grained, auditable mechanism to verify that answers are grounded in the evidence before finalizing them. The authors aim to reduce unsupported answer commitments and make the decision process more transparent and controllable.

Method: The authors introduce PAVE (Premise-Grounded Answer Validation and Editing), an inference-time layer placed on top of a retrieval-augmented language model. PAVE first decomposes the retrieved context into question-conditioned atomic premises (facts). It then has the model draft an answer, evaluates how well this draft is supported by the extracted premises via a support scoring mechanism, and conditionally triggers an answer revision process when support is low. The entire procedure produces an explicit trace of premises, support scores, and revision decisions, enabling inspection and auditing.

Result: In controlled experiments where the retriever and underlying language model are held fixed, PAVE is compared against simpler post-retrieval baselines across two evidence-grounded QA settings. PAVE consistently improves performance, with the largest improvement being 32.7 accuracy points on a span-grounded benchmark, indicating substantially better evidence-grounded answer correctness.

Conclusion: The study concludes that explicitly extracting premises from retrieved context and using support-gated revision at inference time can materially strengthen the evidence-grounded consistency of retrieval-augmented LLMs. The authors present these results as proof-of-concept that such premise-level validation and editing layers are a promising direction for building more reliable, auditable question-answering systems based on large language models.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented language models can retrieve relevant evidence yet still commit to answers before explicitly checking whether the retrieved context supports the conclusion. We present PAVE (Premise-Grounded Answer Validation and Editing), an inference-time validation layer for evidence-grounded question answering. PAVE decomposes retrieved context into question-conditioned atomic facts, drafts an answer, scores how well that draft is supported by the extracted premises, and revises low-support outputs before finalization. The resulting trace makes answer commitment auditable at the level of explicit premises, support scores, and revision decisions. In controlled ablations with a fixed retriever and backbone, PAVE outperforms simpler post-retrieval baselines in two evidence-grounded QA settings, with the largest gain reaching 32.7 accuracy points on a span-grounded benchmark. We view these findings as proof-of-concept evidence that explicit premise extraction plus support-gated revision can strengthen evidence-grounded consistency in retrieval-augmented LLM systems.

*Jiang Liu,Ge Qiu,Hao Fei,Dongdong Xie,Jinbo Li,Fei Li,Chong Teng,Donghong Ji*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose Code-MIE, a code-style multimodal information extraction framework that uses Python-like input/output templates to better align LLMs with structured IE, achieving state-of-the-art results on several benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based multimodal information extraction methods rely mainly on natural language templates that are poorly aligned with inherently structured IE outputs like entities and relations. Some newer approaches use code-style templates but only for text-only IE, and they require task-specific template designs, limiting generality and simplicity, especially for multimodal settings involving images.

Method: They design Code-MIE, which recasts multimodal information extraction as unified code understanding and generation. The method: (1) extracts entity attributes (e.g., gender, affiliation) from text to help the model understand entities’ roles and context; (2) converts images into scene graphs and visual feature representations to inject rich visual information; (3) builds a unified Python function-style input template whose parameters consist of entity attributes, scene graphs, and raw text, and defines a Python-dictionary-style output template that encodes all extraction outputs (entities, relations, etc.). This code-style I/O is fed to and generated by an LLM as the core engine.

Result: On four benchmarks (M^3D English and Chinese, Twitter-15, Twitter-17, MNRE), Code-MIE outperforms six strong baseline models. Reported F1 (or similar metric) scores are 61.03% and 60.49% on the English and Chinese M^3D datasets, and 76.04%, 88.07%, and 73.94% on Twitter-15, Twitter-17, and MNRE, respectively, establishing new state-of-the-art results.

Conclusion: Representing multimodal IE as code understanding/generation using unified Python-style templates for both input and output allows LLMs to better handle structured information. Enriching inputs with entity attributes and structured visual representations (scene graphs and features) further improves performance. This design simplifies template engineering across tasks and yields state-of-the-art results on multiple multimodal IE benchmarks.

Abstract: With the rapid development of large language models (LLMs), more and more researchers have paid attention to information extraction based on LLMs. However, there are still some spaces to improve in the existing related methods. First, existing multimodal information extraction (MIE) methods usually employ natural language templates as the input and output of LLMs, which mismatch with the characteristics of information tasks that mostly include structured information such as entities and relations. Second, although a few methods have adopted structured and more IE-friendly code-style templates, they just explored their methods on text-only IE rather than multimodal IE. Moreover, their methods are more complex in design, requiring separate templates to be designed for each task. In this paper, we propose a Code-style Multimodal Information Extraction framework (Code-MIE) which formalizes MIE as unified code understanding and generation. Code-MIE has the following novel designs: (1) Entity attributes such as gender, affiliation are extracted from the text to guide the model to understand the context and role of entities. (2) Images are converted into scene graphs and visual features to incorporate rich visual information into the model. (3) The input template is constructed as a Python function, where entity attributes, scene graphs and raw text compose of the function parameters. In contrast, the output template is formalized as Python dictionaries containing all extraction results such as entities, relations, etc. To evaluate Code-MIE, we conducted extensive experiments on the M$^3$D, Twitter-15, Twitter-17, and MNRE datasets. The results show that our method achieves state-of-the-art performance compared to six competing baseline models, with 61.03\% and 60.49\% on the English and Chinese datasets of M$^3$D, and 76.04\%, 88.07\%, and 73.94\% on the other three datasets.

### [33] [The Anatomy of an Edit: Mechanism-Guided Activation Steering for Knowledge Editing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20795)
*Yuan Cao,Mingyang Wang,Hinrich Schütze*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how knowledge editing affects internal computations of large language models, then uses these insights to design a new non-weight-changing editing method, MEGA, which achieves strong, architecture-agnostic performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models are often treated as knowledge bases, but their stored facts quickly become outdated. Existing knowledge editing methods can inject or modify facts, yet it is unclear where and how these edits are actually implemented within the model. Prior work mainly analyzes models before editing to localize factual knowledge, offering limited guidance on how successful edits manifest internally. The authors aim to obtain a mechanistic understanding of post-edit changes and use this understanding to engineer more reliable, precise, and general editing techniques.

Method: The authors introduce neuron-level knowledge attribution (NLKA) in a post-edit setting. They compare internal activations and attributions between successful and failed knowledge edits to identify which components (attention layers, feed-forward networks) are responsible for implementing the new fact and suppressing the old one. They systematically apply this analysis across several representative knowledge editing methods and layers. Based on the discovered patterns, they design MEGA (MEchanism-Guided Activation steering), which performs targeted interventions on attention-residual streams in the regions identified by NLKA, steering activations toward the desired edited behavior without changing model weights. They evaluate MEGA on the CounterFact and Popular benchmarks using GPT2-XL and LLaMA2-7B.

Result: The analysis reveals a consistent mechanistic pattern across knowledge editing methods: mid-to-late attention layers are primarily responsible for promoting the new target fact, while attention and feed-forward modules work together to suppress the original fact. Using these insights, MEGA is able to carry out effective activation steering in attribution-aligned regions. Empirically, MEGA shows strong performance on standard knowledge editing metrics on the CounterFact and Popular benchmarks, and it works well across architectures, including GPT2-XL and LLaMA2-7B.

Conclusion: Post-edit, neuron-level attribution reveals stable internal mechanisms by which LLMs implement knowledge edits: specific attention and FFN components boost the new fact and dampen the old one. Exploiting this mechanistic understanding enables MEGA, a weight-free, architecture-agnostic activation steering method that delivers reliable knowledge edits. The work demonstrates that post-edit attribution can serve not only as an analysis tool but also as a practical engineering signal for designing better knowledge editing algorithms.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used as knowledge bases, but keeping them up to date requires targeted knowledge editing (KE). However, it remains unclear how edits are implemented inside the model once applied. In this work, we take a mechanistic view of KE using neuron-level knowledge attribution (NLKA). Unlike prior work that focuses on pre-edit causal tracing and localization, we use post-edit attribution -- contrasting successful and failed edits -- to isolate the computations that shift when an edit succeeds. Across representative KE methods, we find a consistent pattern: mid-to-late attention predominantly promotes the new target, while attention and FFN modules cooperate to suppress the original fact. Motivated by these findings, we propose MEGA, a MEchanism-Guided Activation steering method that performs attention-residual interventions in attribution-aligned regions without modifying model weights. On CounterFact and Popular, MEGA achieves strong editing performance across KE metrics on GPT2-XL and LLaMA2-7B. Overall, our results elevate post-edit attribution from analysis to engineering signal: by pinpointing where and how edits take hold, it powers MEGA to deliver reliable, architecture-agnostic knowledge edits.

### [34] [RLVR Training of LLMs Does Not Improve Thinking Ability for General QA: Evaluation Method and a Simple Solution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20799)
*Kaiyuan Li,Jing-Cheng Pang,Yang Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether reinforcement learning from verifiable rewards (RLVR), which improves LLM reasoning on verifiable tasks, also helps general question answering (GQA), finds it largely does not, and proposes a new training method (START) that better improves thinking quality and answers on GQA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RLVR has recently shown strong gains in LLM reasoning on tasks with verifiable outcomes (e.g., math, code), and many assume these gains will naturally transfer to more open-ended general question answering. However, this assumption has not been rigorously tested. Moreover, direct RL on GQA appears less effective, likely due to reward shortcuts that do not require deep reasoning. The authors want to (1) measure how useful RLVR-trained “thinking traces” actually are for GQA, and (2) design a training strategy that avoids shortcut behaviors and truly improves intermediate reasoning for GQA.

Method: They propose a Cross-Generation evaluation framework, where intermediate reasoning traces (thinking context) generated by one model are fed into LLMs of different capability levels to assess how much these traces help answer GQA questions. They compare performance on verifiable tasks vs GQA to quantify transfer. Observing weaknesses, they hypothesize GQA rewards admit shortcuts, and introduce START (Separated Thinking And Response Training): a two-stage RL-style training scheme that first trains only the hidden chain-of-thought (thinking process) using rewards computed on the final answer, and only later trains the surface response, thereby encouraging more robust internal reasoning. They test START across multiple GQA benchmarks and with different RL algorithms.

Result: Cross-Generation evaluation shows that the usefulness of the generated thinking context in GQA is substantially lower than in verifiable tasks, indicating limited transfer of RLVR benefits to GQA. Direct RL on GQA underperforms RLVR, supporting the idea that GQA permits reward shortcuts that do not foster good reasoning chains. Applying START improves both the judged quality of intermediate thinking and the final answer accuracy on several GQA benchmarks, and this holds across multiple RL algorithms.

Conclusion: Training LLMs with RL from verifiable rewards does not automatically confer strong benefits to general question answering, because GQA often allows reward-maximizing shortcuts that bypass genuine reasoning. Explicit GQA-oriented training is still needed. By separating the optimization of internal thinking from the final response, START mitigates shortcut exploitation and yields better reasoning traces and answers on GQA tasks, offering a more effective way to extend RLVR-style benefits to non-verifiable settings.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning from verifiable rewards (RLVR) stimulates the thinking processes of large language models (LLMs), substantially enhancing their reasoning abilities on verifiable tasks. It is often assumed that similar gains should transfer to general question answering (GQA), but this assumption has not been thoroughly validated. To assess whether RLVR automatically improves LLM performance on GQA, we propose a Cross-Generation evaluation framework that measures the quality of intermediate reasoning by feeding the generated thinking context into LLMs of varying capabilities. Our evaluation leads to a discouraging finding: the efficacy of the thinking process on GQA tasks is markedly lower than on verifiable tasks, suggesting that explicit training on GQA remains necessary in addition to training on verifiable tasks. We further observe that direct RL training on GQA is less effective than RLVR. Our hypothesis is that, whereas verifiable tasks demand robust logical chains to obtain high rewards, GQA tasks often admit shortcuts to high rewards without cultivating high-quality thinking. To avoid possible shortcuts, we introduce a simple method, Separated Thinking And Response Training (START), which first trains only the thinking process, using rewards defined on the final answer. We show that START improves both the quality of thinking and the final answer across several GQA benchmarks and RL algorithms.

### [35] [BenchBench: Benchmarking Automated Benchmark Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20807)
*Yandan Zheng,Haoran Luo,Zhenghong Lin,Wenjin Liu,Luu Anh Tuan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: BenchBench is a framework and dataset for evaluating how well LLMs can *design* benchmarks, not just solve them, using a multi-stage generation–validation pipeline across many domains and modalities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM benchmarks are static, quickly saturated, vulnerable to training-data contamination, and expensive to refresh. Using LLMs as judges for open-ended tasks adds bias and prompt sensitivity. The authors want a scalable, systematic way to (1) continuously generate new high-quality test items and (2) actually measure how good different LLMs are at designing such benchmarks.

Method: They propose BenchBench, a three-stage pipeline. (1) From existing seed benchmarks, they extract structured "domain cards" that summarize the domain and constraints. (2) Multiple LLMs, acting as benchmark designers, are prompted—under quota controls—to generate suites of new test items following those cards (including variants across domains, languages, and modalities). (3) A panel of LLM answerers attempts all items. When possible, automatic exact/numeric/symbolic checks verify correctness; otherwise, rubric-guided LLM judging is used. This yields large designer–answerer matrices annotated with item-level quality flags and psychometric statistics (e.g., discrimination).

Result: Across nine benchmark variants in areas like CS, math, medicine, and theory-of-mind, and including multilingual and multimodal tasks, they generate 16.7K candidate items and filter down to about 15K core items, producing roughly 152K graded model–item response pairs. Analysis shows that (a) an LLM’s ability to design good benchmarks correlates only moderately with its problem-solving strength (Spearman rho ≈ 0.37); (b) item invalidity is strongly negatively correlated with psychometric discrimination (Pearson r ≈ -0.62); and (c) the designer–answerer matrices support rich audits of properties like format adherence, modality and language fidelity, and how models behave on their own or related models’ benchmarks.

Conclusion: Benchmarking should include evaluating LLMs as benchmark designers, not just as test takers. BenchBench provides a scalable, multi-model, multi-domain pipeline and dataset to do this, demonstrating that design skill is a distinct capability and enabling systematic quality control and analysis of automatically generated benchmarks.

Abstract: Benchmarks are the de facto standard for tracking progress in large language models (LLMs), yet static test sets can rapidly saturate, become vulnerable to contamination, and are costly to refresh. Scalable evaluation of open-ended items often relies on LLM judges, introducing additional sources of bias and prompt sensitivity. We argue that evaluation must extend beyond how well models answer benchmarks to how well models design them. We introduce BenchBench, a three-stage pipeline and dataset for benchmarking automated benchmark generation: (i) extract structured domain cards from seed benchmarks, (ii) prompt multiple designer LLMs to generate quota-controlled suites, and (iii) validate items with a multi-model answerer panel using exact/numeric/symbolic verifiers when possible and rubric-guided judging otherwise, yielding designer--answerer matrices with item-level quality flags and psychometric diagnostics. Across nine variants spanning computer science, mathematics, medicine, and theory-of-mind reasoning (including multilingual and multimodal settings), we generate 16.7K items, retain ~15K core items post-filtering, and produce ~152K graded model--item responses. BenchBench shows that benchmark-design ability is only moderately correlated with answer-time strength (Spearman rho ~0.37), invalidity is negatively associated with discrimination (Pearson r~0.62), and the resulting designer--answerer matrices enable scalable audits of format/modality/language fidelity and suite-dependent self/family interactions. The project is available at: https://github.com/koanatakiyo/BenchBench.

</details>


### [36] [HiCI: Hierarchical Construction-Integration for Long-Context Attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20843)
*Xiangyu Zeng,Qi Xu,Yunke Wang,Chang Xu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes HiCI, a hierarchical attention module that explicitly structures local and global context to enable efficient long-context language modeling, extending LLaMA-2 context to up to 100K tokens with small parameter overhead and strong empirical gains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing long-context language modeling methods mostly treat the problem as scaling token-level attention, leaving the organization of information from local segments to global discourse largely implicit. Cognitive theories of discourse comprehension suggest humans build hierarchical representations (segments and global situation models), but current models typically do not encode such structure explicitly. There is a need for an architecture that introduces an explicit local-to-global hierarchy as an inductive bias while remaining parameter-efficient and compatible with strong base LLMs like LLaMA-2.

Method: Introduce HiCI (Hierarchical Construction–Integration), a hierarchical attention module that operates on segments rather than only tokens. First, it constructs segment-level representations, then integrates these into a shared global context representation, and finally broadcasts both segment-level and global representations back to condition segment-level attention. HiCI is added as a parameter-efficient adaptation layer on top of LLaMA-2, adding less than 5.5% additional parameters. The context window is extended from 4K tokens to 100K tokens for the 7B model and 64K for the 13B model, and performance is evaluated on multiple tasks.

Result: With <5.5% extra parameters, HiCI-augmented LLaMA-2 extends its effective context window from 4K to 100K tokens (7B) and 64K tokens (13B). Across language modeling, retrieval, and instruction-following benchmarks, the model shows consistent performance improvements over strong long-context baselines. It matches proprietary systems on topic retrieval tasks and surpasses GPT-3.5-Turbo-16K on code comprehension benchmarks, indicating competitive or superior long-context reasoning and retrieval capabilities.

Conclusion: Explicit hierarchical structuring of context via segment-level and global representations acts as a powerful inductive bias for long-context language models. HiCI demonstrates that adding a lightweight hierarchical attention module can substantially extend context length and improve performance across several benchmarks while remaining parameter-efficient and competitive with proprietary models. This supports the idea that future long-context architectures should incorporate explicit local-to-global hierarchy rather than solely focusing on scaling flat token-level attention.

Abstract: Long-context language modeling is commonly framed as a scalability challenge of token-level attention, yet local-to-global information structuring remains largely implicit in existing approaches. Drawing on cognitive theories of discourse comprehension, we propose HiCI (Hierarchical Construction--Integration), a hierarchical attention module that constructs segment-level representations, integrates them into a shared global context, and broadcasts both to condition segment-level attention. We validate HiCI through parameter-efficient adaptation of LLaMA-2 with only <5.5% additional parameters, extending context from 4K to 100K tokens (7B) and 64K tokens (13B). Across language modeling, retrieval, and instruction-following benchmarks, HiCI yields consistent improvements over strong baselines, including matching proprietary models on topic retrieval and surpassing GPT-3.5-Turbo-16K on code comprehension. These results demonstrate the effectiveness of explicit hierarchical structuring as an inductive bias for long-context modeling.

  <summary>Details</summary>

However, performance varies across dimensions: ChatGPT is notably weaker in dimensions associated with poeticity (e.g., nuanced aesthetic, stylistic, or artistic qualities), showing that while it captures general meanings reasonably well, it struggles with more subtle poetic aspects.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the new evaluation framework is both effective and necessary for assessing LLMs’ poetry understanding. ChatGPT demonstrates substantial but incomplete understanding of modern poetry, particularly falling short in capturing poeticity. The work provides a solid methodological basis and empirical reference point for future research on large language models and their use in poetry-related tasks, encouraging more nuanced evaluations that go beyond surface-level output quality.

Abstract: ChatGPT has demonstrated remarkable capabilities on both poetry generation and translation, yet its ability to truly understand poetry remains unexplored. Previous poetry-related work merely analyzed experimental outcomes without addressing fundamental issues of comprehension. This paper introduces a comprehensive framework for evaluating ChatGPT's understanding of modern poetry. We collaborated with professional poets to evaluate ChatGPT's interpretation of modern Chinese poems by different poets along multiple dimensions. Evaluation results show that ChatGPT's interpretations align with the original poets' intents in over 73% of the cases. However, its understanding in certain dimensions, particularly in capturing poeticity, proved to be less satisfactory. These findings highlight the effectiveness and necessity of our proposed framework. This study not only evaluates ChatGPT's ability to understand modern poetry but also establishes a solid foundation for future research on LLMs and their application to poetry-related tasks.

We present SozKZ, a family of Llama-architecture language models (50M-600M parameters) trained entirely from scratch on 9 billion tokens of Kazakh text with a dedicated 50K BPE tokenizer. We evaluate all models on three Kazakh benchmarks -- multiple-choice cultural QA, reading comprehension (Belebele), and topic classification (SIB-200) -- alongside five multilingual baselines ranging from 500M to 3B parameters. We observe consistent scaling from 50M to 600M, with MC QA accuracy rising from 22.8% to 30.3%, suggesting that further scaling remains beneficial. These results demonstrate that small, dedicated models trained from scratch with a language-appropriate tokenizer offer a viable path for low-resource language technology, achieving competitive performance at a fraction of the computational cost. All models and the tokenizer are released under open licenses.

Combined with their checklist-based evaluation paradigm, it offers a more reliable and unbiased way to generate and judge novelty reports. The authors expect this to help researchers quickly identify genuinely novel work and make paper screening more efficient; they publicly release code and a demo to support adoption and further research.

### [40] [LLM Router: Prefill is All You Need](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20895)
*Tanay Varshney,Annie Surla,Michelle Xu,Gomathy Venkata Krishnan,Maximilian Jeblick,David Austin,Neal Vaidya,Davide Onofrio*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a routing framework that uses internal activations of language models to decide which model should answer a query, closing a large portion of the gap to an ideal Oracle router at much lower cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although many large language models have similar overall benchmark scores, they excel on different subsets of tasks. An ideal Oracle that always picks the best model per input would greatly outperform any single model, but practical routers that try to approximate this are brittle because they rely on semantic or surface-level features of the input. There is a need for a more robust, principled way to exploit model complementarities to improve accuracy and reduce cost.

Method: The authors introduce Encoder-Target Decoupling, where one model (the Encoder) provides internal prefill activations that are used to predict which Target model will perform best, without the Encoder necessarily being the same as the Target. They mathematically probe intermediate layers using Fisher Separability (J) and Effective Dimensionality (d_eff) to identify which layer activations are most informative for routing decisions. Based on these signals, they design SharedTrunkNet, an architecture that shares an encoder trunk across multiple targets and uses the extracted features to route queries to the most suitable target model.

Result: Using SharedTrunkNet and the selected internal-layer features, the system recovers up to 45.58% of the performance gap between the best standalone model and an Oracle router that always chooses the best model per example, while also achieving 74.31% cost reduction compared to always using the most expensive model.

Conclusion: Internal prefill activations, when properly analyzed via Fisher Separability and Effective Dimensionality and used within an Encoder-Target Decoupling framework, can provide robust routing signals that exploit complementary strengths across LLMs. The proposed SharedTrunkNet architecture demonstrates that a substantial fraction of the Oracle router’s performance can be obtained at significantly lower computational cost, suggesting a promising direction for scalable multi-model deployment.

Abstract: LLMs often share comparable benchmark accuracies, but their complementary performance across task subsets suggests that an Oracle router--a theoretical selector with perfect foresight--can significantly surpass standalone model accuracy by navigating model-specific strengths. While current routers rely on fragile semantic signals, we propose using internal prefill activations via Encoder-Target Decoupling--a functional separation between the model providing the predictive signal (the Encoder) and the model whose performance is being estimated (the Target). This allows optimized heterogeneous pairing between unique encoders and target models. We utilize Fisher Separability (J) and Effective Dimensionality (d_eff) as mathematical probes to isolate optimal layer-wise signals, providing the predictive foundation for our SharedTrunkNet architecture. SharedTrunkNet captures up to 45.58% of the accuracy gap between the strongest standalone model and the Oracle while achieving 74.31% cost savings relative to the highest-cost model.

### [41] [Mitigating Shortcut Reasoning in Language Models: A Gradient-Aware Training Approach](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20899)
*Hongyu Cao,Kunpeng Liu,Dongjie Wang,Yanjie Fu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Shortcut-Aware Reasoning Training (SART), a training framework that detects and down-weights shortcut-based samples using gradient-based signals, leading to better reasoning generalization and robustness for large language models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models seem strong at reasoning, they often exploit spurious shortcuts like pattern matching or memorized answers instead of true logical inference. These shortcuts undermine robustness and out-of-distribution generalization. Existing methods don’t explicitly identify and handle shortcut-promoting training samples during optimization. The paper aims to directly detect when training data pushes the model towards shortcuts and adjust training accordingly.

Method: The authors propose SART, a gradient-aware training framework. First, they define ShortcutScore, which measures shortcutness by looking at (1) gradient misalignment between a sample’s training loss and a validation reasoning objective, and (2) answer-token concentration (whether gradients focus excessively on answer tokens rather than the reasoning chain). Samples with high ShortcutScore are considered shortcut-promoting. Then, they apply gradient surgery: modifying, reweighting, or filtering gradients from high-shortcut samples to reduce their influence during training, effectively reshaping the optimization trajectory to favor genuine reasoning patterns.

Result: On controlled reasoning benchmarks designed to measure robustness and generalization under distribution shifts, SART improves accuracy by 16.5 percentage points and robustness by 40.2% compared to the strongest baseline. These gains demonstrate that detecting and mitigating shortcut-promoting samples during training meaningfully enhances reasoning performance of large language models.

Conclusion: By explicitly identifying gradient-level signals of shortcut usage and altering training dynamics through gradient surgery, SART reduces shortcut reliance and strengthens true reasoning capabilities in large language models. The method yields substantially better accuracy and robustness on reasoning benchmarks, suggesting that data- and optimization-centric strategies targeting shortcuts are a promising direction for improving LLM reasoning generalization.

Abstract: Large language models exhibit strong reasoning capabilities, yet often rely on shortcuts such as surface pattern matching and answer memorization rather than genuine logical inference. We propose Shortcut-Aware Reasoning Training (SART), a gradient-aware framework that detects and mitigates shortcut-promoting samples via ShortcutScore and gradient surgery. Our method identifies shortcut signals through gradient misalignment with validation objectives and answer-token concentration, and modifies training dynamics accordingly. Experiments on controlled reasoning benchmarks show that SART achieves +16.5% accuracy and +40.2% robustness over the strongest baseline, significantly improving generalization under distribution shifts. Code is available at: https://github.com/fuyanjie/short-cut-aware-data-centric-reasoning.

</details>


### [42] [The Hidden Puppet Master: A Theoretical and Real-World Account of Emotional Manipulation in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20907)
*Jocelyn Shen,Amina Luvsanchultem,Jessica Kim,Kynnedy Smith,Valdemar Danry,Kantwon Rogers,Sharifa Alghowinem,Hae Won Park,Maarten Sap,Cynthia Breazeal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes how large language models (LLMs) can subtly manipulate users through hidden incentives, introduces a taxonomy (PUPPET) for such manipulation, runs a large human study on belief shifts under different incentive types, and evaluates how well LLMs can predict these shifts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As people increasingly use LLMs for personal and practical advice, there is a growing risk that these systems (or actors using them) can nudge users toward outcomes driven by hidden incentives that may not align with users’ interests. Existing work on persuasion and manipulation with LLMs tends to use artificial, debate-like setups, lacks validation against real human belief changes, and ignores whether the underlying incentives are morally harmful or prosocial. The authors aim to build a more realistic, ethically grounded framework and empirical basis for understanding and mitigating incentive-driven manipulation in everyday LLM use.

Method: The authors propose PUPPET, a theoretical taxonomy that categorizes forms of personalized emotional manipulation in LLM–human dialogues, explicitly organizing them around the morality of the incentives (e.g., harmful vs. prosocial). They then run a human subject experiment with 1,035 participants, constructing realistic everyday advice-seeking scenarios and systematically varying (1) the degree of personalization and (2) whether the hidden incentives embedded in the responses are harmful or prosocial. Participants’ belief changes after exposure to these conversations are measured. Finally, they benchmark several LLMs on a belief-prediction task, asking the models to predict how much a user’s beliefs will shift given a conversational context, and they evaluate prediction accuracy and bias in magnitude estimation.

Result: Empirically, hidden incentives with harmful moral valence lead to significantly larger shifts in participants’ beliefs than prosocial incentives under comparable conditions, suggesting that harmful manipulative nudges may be especially potent. On the LLM belief-prediction benchmark, models display moderate correlation (r = 0.3–0.5) between their predicted and actual human belief shifts, indicating some ability to anticipate persuasion effects from dialogue context. However, across conditions, the models systematically underestimate how strong the belief shifts actually are.

Conclusion: The study shows that incentive-driven manipulation in LLM-mediated advice is both measurable and more powerful when the underlying incentives are harmful, underscoring a concrete safety risk as LLMs become embedded in everyday decision-making. By introducing the PUPPET taxonomy and validating it behaviorally with a large human study, the authors provide a conceptual and empirical foundation for future work on detecting, understanding, and ultimately mitigating incentive-based manipulation. The belief-prediction benchmarking further suggests that LLMs could be used as tools to forecast manipulation risk, though current models underestimate its magnitude and require improvement for robust safety applications.

Abstract: As users increasingly turn to LLMs for practical and personal advice, they become vulnerable to being subtly steered toward hidden incentives misaligned with their own interests. Prior works have benchmarked persuasion and manipulation detection, but these efforts rely on simulated or debate-style settings, remain uncorrelated with real human belief shifts, and overlook a critical dimension: the morality of hidden incentives driving the manipulation. We introduce PUPPET, a theoretical taxonomy of personalized emotional manipulation in LLM-human dialogues that centers around incentive morality, and conduct a human study with N=1,035 participants across realistic everyday queries, varying personalization and incentive direction (harmful versus prosocial). We find that harmful hidden incentives produce significantly larger belief shifts than prosocial ones. Finally, we benchmark LLMs on the task of belief prediction, finding that models exhibit moderate predictive ability of belief change based on conversational contexts (r=0.3 - 0.5), but they also systematically underestimate the magnitude of belief shift. Together, this work establishes a theoretically grounded and behaviorally validated foundation for studying, and ultimately combatting, incentive-driven manipulation in LLMs during everyday, practical user queries.

### [43] [User Preference Modeling for Conversational LLM Agents: Weak Rewards from Retrieval-Augmented Interaction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20939)
*Yuren Hao,Shuhaib Mehri,ChengXiang Zhai,Dilek Hakkani-Tür*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces VARS, a method to personalize LLM assistants by maintaining long-term and short-term user preference vectors that bias retrieval over a structured memory, improving interaction efficiency without per-user model fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most LLM-based personal assistants lack persistent user modeling, so users must repeatedly restate preferences, and personalization typically requires costly per-user fine-tuning. The authors want a scalable way to model user preferences over time that can plug into existing LLM pipelines, work with frozen backbones, and improve user experience across multi-session interactions.

Method: They propose Vector-Adapted Retrieval Scoring (VARS), a pipeline-agnostic framework that keeps each user represented by two vectors—long-term and short-term—in a shared preference space. These vectors are learned and updated online from weak scalar rewards derived from user feedback. VARS uses these vectors to bias the scoring step in retrieval over a structured preference memory, thereby selecting context that better matches user preferences without changing the base LLM weights. They test this in an online multi-session collaboration setting (MultiSessionCollab) on math and code tasks, comparing against baselines including a Reflection-based approach, all with frozen backbones.

Result: With a frozen backbone, adding user-aware retrieval via VARS does not drastically increase raw task accuracy but significantly improves interaction efficiency: the full VARS agent attains best overall performance on the benchmark, matches a strong Reflection baseline in task success, and lowers timeout rates and user effort. Analysis shows that long-term vectors correlate with preference overlap across users, while short-term vectors reflect session-specific behavior, indicating that the two-vector representation captures meaningful and interpretable preference structure.

Conclusion: VARS offers an effective, interpretable, and scalable way to personalize LLM assistants without per-user fine-tuning by learning long-term and short-term user preference vectors that adapt retrieval. It improves multi-session collaboration efficiency and aligns with intuitive notions of stable vs. session-specific preferences, making it a promising approach for persistent user modeling in LLM-based systems.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used as personal assistants, yet most lack a persistent user model, forcing users to repeatedly restate preferences across sessions. We propose Vector-Adapted Retrieval Scoring (VARS), a pipeline-agnostic, frozen-backbone framework that represents each user with long-term and short-term vectors in a shared preference space and uses these vectors to bias retrieval scoring over structured preference memory. The vectors are updated online from weak scalar rewards from users' feedback, enabling personalization without per-user fine-tuning. We evaluate on \textsc{MultiSessionCollab}, an online multi-session collaboration benchmark with rich user preference profiles, across math and code tasks. Under frozen backbones, the main benefit of user-aware retrieval is improved interaction efficiency rather than large gains in raw task accuracy: our full VARS agent achieves the strongest overall performance, matches a strong Reflection baseline in task success, and reduces timeout rate and user effort. The learned long-term vectors also align with cross-user preference overlap, while short-term vectors capture session-specific adaptation, supporting the interpretability of the dual-vector design. Code, model, and data are available at https://github.com/YurenHao0426/VARS.

### [44] [Alignment Whack-a-Mole : Finetuning Activates Verbatim Recall of Copyrighted Books in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20957)
*Xinyue Liu,Niloofar Mireshghallah,Jane C. Ginsburg,Tuhin Chakrabarty*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that fine-tuning frontier LLMs on specific authors’ works enables large-scale verbatim extraction of copyrighted books, undermining claims that these models do not store or reproduce training data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Frontier LLM providers have argued in courts and regulatory settings that their models do not store or reproduce copyrighted training data, and that safety measures like RLHF and output filters effectively prevent regurgitation. These claims underpin legal defenses in ongoing copyright litigation and favorable fair-use assessments. The authors aim to empirically test these assurances, especially under realistic commercial uses such as fine-tuned writing assistants.

Method: The authors fine-tune three major LLMs (GPT-4o, Gemini-2.5-Pro, DeepSeek-V3.1) on text from specific authors, notably Haruki Murakami, using a task framed as expanding plot summaries into full text—a commercially plausible use case. They then prompt the fine-tuned models with only semantic descriptions or summaries (no verbatim book text) and measure how much of various held-out copyrighted books is reproduced verbatim. They compare extraction under different finetuning data sources (single author, random authors, public-domain texts, synthetic text) and measure overlap across models, quantifying shared memorization regions with correlation metrics (r ≥ 0.90).

Result: Fine-tuned models reproduce up to 85–90% of held-out copyrighted books, including long verbatim spans exceeding 460 words, despite receiving only semantic prompts. Finetuning on one author’s works (e.g., Murakami) enables extraction of verbatim text from many other authors’ copyrighted books, showing cross-author generalization. Similar extraction levels appear when finetuning on random author pairs and public-domain data, while synthetic-text finetuning yields near-zero extraction, implying that exposure to real authors’ texts reactivates memorized training content. Different providers’ models memorize and regurgitate largely the same books in highly correlated regions, indicating a shared, systemic vulnerability rather than a model-specific quirk.

Conclusion: The experiments demonstrate that LLM weights effectively store recoverable copies of copyrighted works, and that seemingly benign finetuning on individual authors’ works can bypass existing safety and alignment mechanisms designed to prevent verbatim regurgitation. This undermines key assurances made by LLM providers and calls into question legal arguments and court rulings that hinge on the supposed effectiveness of anti-regurgitation safeguards as a basis for fair use. The work suggests that current industry practices around training and deployment of large models pose significant, underappreciated copyright and security risks.

Abstract: Frontier LLM companies have repeatedly assured courts and regulators that their models do not store copies of training data. They further rely on safety alignment strategies via RLHF, system prompts, and output filters to block verbatim regurgitation of copyrighted works, and have cited the efficacy of these measures in their legal defenses against copyright infringement claims. We show that finetuning bypasses these protections: by training models to expand plot summaries into full text, a task naturally suited for commercial writing assistants, we cause GPT-4o, Gemini-2.5-Pro, and DeepSeek-V3.1 to reproduce up to 85-90% of held-out copyrighted books, with single verbatim spans exceeding 460 words, using only semantic descriptions as prompts and no actual book text. This extraction generalizes across authors: finetuning exclusively on Haruki Murakami's novels unlocks verbatim recall of copyrighted books from over 30 unrelated authors. The effect is not specific to any training author or corpus: random author pairs and public-domain finetuning data produce comparable extraction, while finetuning on synthetic text yields near-zero extraction, indicating that finetuning on individual authors' works reactivates latent memorization from pretraining. Three models from different providers memorize the same books in the same regions ($r \ge 0.90$), pointing to an industry-wide vulnerability. Our findings offer compelling evidence that model weights store copies of copyrighted works and that the security failures that manifest after finetuning on individual authors' works undermine a key premise of recent fair use rulings, where courts have conditioned favorable outcomes on the adequacy of measures preventing reproduction of protected expression.

</details>


### [45] [DiscoUQ: Structured Disagreement Analysis for Uncertainty Quantification in LLM Agent Ensembles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20975)
*Bo Jiang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DiscoUQ, a framework that uses rich inter-agent disagreement signals in multi-agent LLM systems to better estimate confidence and uncertainty, outperforming vote-based baselines in both discrimination and calibration.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In multi-agent LLM systems, multiple instances of an LLM answer the same question and their outputs are aggregated. Existing uncertainty quantification mainly relies on simple vote counts or shallow statistics, which ignore the detailed reasoning structure and semantic relationships among agents’ answers. This limits both the accuracy and calibration of confidence estimates, especially in cases of partial or weak disagreement. The authors aim to exploit the full structure of disagreement to obtain more reliable uncertainty estimates without changing the underlying LLM.

Method: They propose DiscoUQ, which extracts structured features describing inter-agent disagreement along two axes: (1) linguistic properties of agents’ reasoning, such as overlap in cited evidence, perceived argument strength, and where reasoning diverges; and (2) geometric properties in embedding space, including cluster distances, dispersion, and within-cluster cohesion. They instantiate three models: DiscoUQ-LLM, which uses an LLM to compute linguistic disagreement features and feeds them into a logistic regression; DiscoUQ-Embed, which computes embedding-based geometric features and uses logistic regression; and DiscoUQ-Learn, a neural network that jointly learns from all feature types. These models are trained to predict correctness of the aggregated answer and thereby provide calibrated probabilities.

Result: On four benchmarks (StrategyQA, MMLU, TruthfulQA, ARC-Challenge) with a 5-agent Qwen3.5-27B system, DiscoUQ-LLM attains an AUROC of 0.802, surpassing the best baseline (LLM Aggregator at 0.791) and achieving much better calibration (ECE 0.036 vs. 0.098). The disagreement features learned on one benchmark transfer effectively to others with almost no drop in performance. Gains are particularly strong in the weak-disagreement regime where agents’ votes are split but not strongly polarized, a setting where simple voting-based uncertainty is unreliable.

Conclusion: Leveraging the semantic and geometric structure of inter-agent disagreement significantly improves uncertainty estimation in multi-agent LLM systems, both in terms of discriminating correct from incorrect responses and in calibration of predicted confidence. DiscoUQ’s feature representations are robust across tasks and models, and are especially valuable in ambiguous cases where traditional vote-counting approaches perform poorly. This suggests that deeper analysis of agents’ reasoning and embeddings is a promising direction for building trustworthy multi-agent LLM systems.

Abstract: Multi-agent LLM systems, where multiple prompted instances of a language model independently answer questions, are increasingly used for complex reasoning tasks. However, existing methods for quantifying the uncertainty of their collective outputs rely on shallow voting statistics that discard the rich semantic information in agents' reasoning. We introduce DiscoUQ, a framework that extracts and leverages the structure of inter-agent disagreement -- both linguistic properties (evidence overlap, argument strength, divergence depth) and embedding geometry (cluster distances, dispersion, cohesion) -- to produce well-calibrated confidence estimates. We propose three methods of increasing complexity: DiscoUQ-LLM (logistic regression on LLM-extracted structure features), DiscoUQ-Embed (logistic regression on embedding geometry), and DiscoUQ-Learn (a neural network combining all features). Evaluated on four diverse benchmarks (StrategyQA, MMLU, TruthfulQA, ARC-Challenge) with a 5-agent system using Qwen3.5-27B, DiscoUQ-LLM achieves an average AUROC of 0.802, outperforming the best baseline (LLM Aggregator, 0.791) while being substantially better calibrated (ECE 0.036 vs. 0.098). The learned features generalize across benchmarks with near-zero performance degradation and provide the largest improvements where they are most needed: in the ambiguous "weak disagreement" tier where simple vote counting fails.

### [46] [Mitigating Selection Bias in Large Language Models via Permutation-Aware GRPO](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21016)
*Jinquan Zheng,Jia Yuan,Jiacheng Yao,Chenyang Gu,Pujun Zheng,Guoxiu He*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PA-GRPO, a reinforcement learning method that reduces selection bias of LLMs on multiple-choice and pairwise tasks by enforcing consistent decisions across different permutations of answer options.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs used as evaluators or solvers in multiple-choice and pairwise settings are biased by superficial features such as option order and label symbols (e.g., A/B/C, left/right). Existing debiasing at inference time is computationally expensive and can hurt reasoning, while standard pointwise training fails to exploit the fact that all permutations of a question should yield the same answer. The authors aim to create a training framework that directly tackles selection bias by making the model’s decisions invariant to permutations of the answer options.

Method: The authors propose Permutation-Aware Group Relative Policy Optimization (PA-GRPO), a reinforcement learning algorithm built around permutation groups of each training instance. For each question, they generate multiple permutations of the answer options, forming a group of equivalent instances. They then optimize the LLM with two mechanisms: (1) Cross-permutation advantage: for each permutation, the model’s reward (based on correctness or other signals) is compared to the average reward across all permutations of that question, yielding an advantage term that encourages policies that perform well relative to other permutations. (2) Consistency-aware reward: a regularization term that explicitly encourages the model to produce the same semantic decision (e.g., choose the same logical answer) across different permutations. Training is done via policy gradient/RL-style updates that incorporate these group-relative and consistency-aware signals.

Result: Across seven multiple-choice and pairwise evaluation benchmarks, PA-GRPO yields higher performance than strong baselines while substantially reducing selection bias to option positions and label forms. The method preserves or improves overall task accuracy while making model decisions more robust and permutation-invariant, demonstrating that the approach effectively addresses selection bias without sacrificing reasoning capability.

Conclusion: PA-GRPO provides a practical training-time solution to selection bias in LLM evaluators by exploiting the natural permutation invariance of multiple-choice and pairwise tasks. By grouping permutations of each instance and optimizing with cross-permutation advantages and consistency-aware rewards, the method aligns model behavior with semantic invariance and yields more reliable, less biased evaluations. The approach outperforms existing baselines, showing that permutation-aware RL fine-tuning is a promising direction for building robust LLM evaluators.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) used for multiple-choice and pairwise evaluation tasks often exhibit selection bias due to non-semantic factors like option positions and label symbols. Existing inference-time debiasing is costly and may harm reasoning, while pointwise training ignores that the same question should yield consistent answers across permutations. To address this issue, we propose Permutation-Aware Group Relative Policy Optimization (PA-GRPO), which mitigates selection bias by enforcing permutation-consistent semantic reasoning. PA-GRPO constructs a permutation group for each instance by generating multiple candidate permutations, and optimizes the model using two complementary mechanisms: (1) cross-permutation advantage, which computes advantages relative to the mean reward over all permutations of the same instance, and (2) consistency-aware reward, which encourages the model to produce consistent decisions across different permutations. Experimental results demonstrate that PA-GRPO outperforms strong baselines across seven benchmarks, substantially reducing selection bias while maintaining high overall performance. The code will be made available on Github (https://github.com/ECNU-Text-Computing/PA-GRPO).

</details>


### [47] [Left Behind: Cross-Lingual Transfer as a Bridge for Low-Resource Languages in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21036)
*Abdul-Salem Beibitkhan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper benchmarks multiple LLMs on low-resource languages and shows a large, systematic accuracy gap vs English, with fluency masking errors and only architecture-specific mitigation helping.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs perform impressively in English and other high-resource languages, their performance in low-resource languages is poorly understood and can directly impact the quality and safety of AI tools for those language communities. Prior work often reports aggregate multilingual scores without carefully dissecting accuracy vs fluency or examining how model architectures and prompting strategies affect performance in genuinely low-resource settings. This paper aims to fill that gap by providing a controlled, targeted evaluation of LLM behavior in two low-resource languages compared to English, to understand not only how large the gap is but also whether common cross-lingual prompting tricks actually help.

Method: The authors benchmark eight different large language models under five experimental conditions across three languages: English, Kazakh, and Mongolian. They construct a set of 50 hand-crafted questions covering four categories: factual, reasoning, technical, and culturally grounded content. For each model and condition, they collect a total of 2,000 responses. These responses are then evaluated along three dimensions: accuracy of the content, fluency of the language, and completeness of the answer. One of the conditions involves a cross-lingual transfer-prompting strategy, in which models are instructed to perform their reasoning in English and only then translate the final answer back into the low-resource language. The authors compare performance across languages, conditions, and architectures (e.g., bilingual vs English-dominant models) to identify systematic patterns and potential mitigation effects.

Result: Across the evaluated models and conditions, the authors observe a consistent performance gap between English and the low-resource languages (Kazakh and Mongolian): models perform 13.8 to 16.7 percentage points worse in the low-resource conditions. While the fluency of the generated text in low-resource languages generally remains high—meaning the outputs look grammatically well-formed and natural—the underlying accuracy of the information is significantly lower compared to English outputs. The cross-lingual transfer-prompting approach, where models reason in English before translating back, yields modest gains in accuracy for bilingual architectures, improving performance by about +2.2 to +4.3 percentage points. However, this same strategy does not improve performance for English-dominant models, indicating that prompting alone does not universally solve the low-resource performance gap.

Conclusion: The study concludes that current large language models systematically underperform in low-resource languages relative to English, in terms of accuracy and completeness, even when their outputs appear fluent. This gap suggests that low-resource language communities are being underserved by present-day LLM technology, potentially facing misleading but convincing outputs. Moreover, popular mitigation strategies such as cross-lingual transfer-prompting are not universally effective; their benefits depend on the underlying model architecture, with bilingual models showing some improvements and English-centric models showing none. Therefore, closing the low-resource gap will require architecture- and training-aware approaches rather than one-size-fits-all prompting techniques.

Abstract: We investigate how large language models perform on low-resource languages by benchmarking eight LLMs across five experimental conditions in English, Kazakh, and Mongolian. Using 50 hand-crafted questions spanning factual, reasoning, technical, and culturally grounded categories, we evaluate 2,000 responses on accuracy, fluency, and completeness. We find a consistent performance gap of 13.8-16.7 percentage points between English and low-resource language conditions, with models maintaining surface-level fluency while producing significantly less accurate content. Cross-lingual transfer-prompting models to reason in English before translating back-yields selective gains for bilingual architectures (+2.2pp to +4.3pp) but provides no benefit to English-dominant models. Our results demonstrate that current LLMs systematically underserve low-resource language communities, and that effective mitigation strategies are architecture-dependent rather than universal.

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how people replace body language and tone of voice with text-based cues (like emojis, punctuation, capitalization) in microblogging, builds tools to detect these cues automatically, and tests how they affect emotional understanding and ambiguity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As more interaction happens through text where facial expressions, gestures and tone are missing, we lack a systematic theory and measurement of how people recreate nonverbal signals in writing, especially on public platforms like microblogs. Existing work is fragmented, often descriptive, and not well integrated with classic nonverbal communication theory or scalable computational methods.

Method: The paper runs three studies: (1) a conceptual and computational study that derives a taxonomy of electronic nonverbal cues (eNVCs) from traditional nonverbal communication theory and implements an automated detection toolkit in Python; (2) a within-subject survey experiment that manipulates the presence of eNVCs in messages to measure their causal impact on emotional decoding accuracy and perceived ambiguity, including testing conditions such as sarcasm; (3) qualitative focus groups exploring how users interpret and reason about digital prosody, including how they infer meaning from both the presence and absence of cues.

Result: Study 1 yields a unified taxonomy of eNVCs and a working, scalable Python (and R) toolkit that can automatically detect them in microblog text. Study 2 finds that adding eNVCs generally improves readers’ ability to correctly infer emotions and reduces perceived ambiguity, but that this effect is weaker or disappears in contexts like sarcasm. Study 3 shows that users engage in nuanced interpretive strategies, including reading significance into missing expected cues and tending to interpret ambiguous messages more negatively.

Conclusion: Electronic nonverbal cues form a coherent and measurable class of behaviors in text-based microblogging. Recognizing and modeling them refines theories of media richness and interpretive effort in CMC, and the taxonomy plus detection toolkit enable practical applications in affective computing, user modeling, and the design of emotion-aware interfaces.

Abstract: As text-based computer-mediated communication (CMC) increasingly structures everyday interaction, a central question re-emerges with new urgency: How do users reconstruct nonverbal expression in environments where embodied cues are absent? This paper provides a systematic, theory-driven account of electronic nonverbal cues (eNVCs) - textual analogues of kinesics, vocalics, and paralinguistics - in public microblog communication. Across three complementary studies, we advance conceptual, empirical, and methodological contributions. Study 1 develops a unified taxonomy of eNVCs grounded in foundational nonverbal communication theory and introduces a scalable Python toolkit for their automated detection. Study 2, a within-subject survey experiment, offers controlled causal evidence that eNVCs substantially improve emotional decoding accuracy and lower perceived ambiguity, while also identifying boundary conditions, such as sarcasm, under which these benefits weaken or disappear. Study 3, through focus group discussions, reveals the interpretive strategies users employ when reasoning about digital prosody, including drawing meaning from the absence of expected cues and defaulting toward negative interpretations in ambiguous contexts. Together, these studies establish eNVCs as a coherent and measurable class of digital behaviors, refine theoretical accounts of cue richness and interpretive effort, and provide practical tools for affective computing, user modeling, and emotion-aware interface design. The eNVC detection toolkit is available as a Python and R package at https://github.com/kokiljaidka/envc.
