### [49] [Assessing the Ability of Neural TTS Systems to Model Consonant-Induced F0 Perturbation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21078)
*Tianle Yang,Chengzhe Sun,Phil Rose,Cassandra L. Jacobs,Siwei Lyu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a probing framework to test how well neural TTS systems reproduce subtle consonant-related pitch (f0) perturbations, revealing that current models mainly memorize prosody for frequent words and generalize poorly to rare ones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Neural TTS models sound natural overall, but it is unclear whether they truly learn fine-grained, linguistically grounded prosodic phenomena or just memorize patterns from the training data. Consonant-induced f0 perturbation is a well-studied, local articulatory-prosodic effect that can serve as a precise test of whether TTS systems encode such segmental-prosodic detail rather than relying on surface-level lexical memorization.

Method: The authors design a segmental-level prosodic probing framework that measures how well TTS systems reproduce consonant-induced f0 perturbations. They generate synthetic speech for thousands of words and compare it against natural speech realizations, controlling for lexical frequency and using two representative architectures—Tacotron 2 and FastSpeech 2—trained on the same LJ Speech corpus. After these controlled experiments, they extend the evaluation to a broader set of advanced TTS systems to examine how widespread the observed behaviors are.

Result: Across systems, synthetic speech closely matches natural f0 perturbation patterns for high-frequency words but performs poorly on low-frequency items. This indicates strong dependence on lexical frequency, with accurate reproduction largely limited to words that appear frequently in the training data, and degraded or absent consonant-induced f0 perturbations for rarer words.

Conclusion: The study concludes that the examined neural TTS systems tend to rely on lexical-level memorization rather than robust, abstract encoding of segmental-prosodic phenomena such as consonant-induced f0 perturbation. This limitation constrains their ability to generalize fine prosodic detail to unseen or low-frequency words. The proposed prosodic probing framework provides a linguistically grounded diagnostic tool that can guide future evaluation protocols and help assess interpretability and authenticity in synthetic speech generation.

Abstract: This study proposes a segmental-level prosodic probing framework to evaluate neural TTS models' ability to reproduce consonant-induced f0 perturbation, a fine-grained segmental-prosodic effect that reflects local articulatory mechanisms. We compare synthetic and natural speech realizations for thousands of words, stratified by lexical frequency, using Tacotron 2 and FastSpeech 2 trained on the same speech corpus (LJ Speech). These controlled analyses are then complemented by a large-scale evaluation spanning multiple advanced TTS systems. Results show accurate reproduction for high-frequency words but poor generalization to low-frequency items, suggesting that the examined TTS architectures rely more on lexical-level memorization than on abstract segmental-prosodic encoding. This finding highlights a limitation in such TTS systems' ability to generalize prosodic detail beyond seen data. The proposed probe offers a linguistically informed diagnostic framework that may inform future TTS evaluation methods, and has implications for interpretability and authenticity assessment in synthetic speech.

### [50] [ViCLSR: A Supervised Contrastive Learning Framework with Natural Language Inference for Natural Language Understanding Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21084)
*Tin Van Huynh,Kiet Van Nguyen,Ngan Luu-Thuy Nguyen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes ViCLSR, a supervised contrastive learning framework that optimizes Vietnamese sentence representations and significantly outperforms PhoBERT on multiple Vietnamese NLU benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Low-resource languages such as Vietnamese suffer from limited annotated data, which constrains the effectiveness of existing pre-trained language models (e.g., PhoBERT, CafeBERT) for NLU. There is a need for better sentence representations that can work well despite data scarcity and leverage available labeled resources more effectively.

Method: The authors design ViCLSR, a supervised contrastive learning framework tailored for Vietnamese sentence representation. They leverage existing Vietnamese NLI datasets and introduce a process to adapt these datasets for compatibility with supervised contrastive learning. The model is trained to pull semantically similar sentences closer and push dissimilar ones apart in embedding space, using NLI labels as supervision. They then evaluate the learned sentence embeddings on various downstream Vietnamese NLU tasks.

Result: ViCLSR substantially outperforms the strong monolingual baseline PhoBERT across five benchmark Vietnamese NLU datasets: +6.97% F1 on ViNLI, +4.97% F1 on ViWikiFC, +9.02% F1 on ViFactCheck, +5.36% F1 on UIT-ViCTSD, and +4.33% Accuracy on ViMMRC2.0. Additional analysis identifies factors that contribute to the superiority of contrastive learning-based sentence representations over conventional fine-tuning.

Conclusion: Supervised contrastive learning is highly effective for improving sentence representations in low-resource languages like Vietnamese. By leveraging and adapting existing NLI datasets, ViCLSR overcomes some limitations of data scarcity and achieves consistent gains over a strong pre-trained baseline on multiple NLU tasks. The released ViCLSR model and framework can help advance Vietnamese and other low-resource NLP research.

Abstract: High-quality text representations are crucial for natural language understanding (NLU), but low-resource languages like Vietnamese face challenges due to limited annotated data. While pre-trained models like PhoBERT and CafeBERT perform well, their effectiveness is constrained by data scarcity. Contrastive learning (CL) has recently emerged as a promising approach for improving sentence representations, enabling models to effectively distinguish between semantically similar and dissimilar sentences. We propose ViCLSR (Vietnamese Contrastive Learning for Sentence Representations), a novel supervised contrastive learning framework specifically designed to optimize sentence embeddings for Vietnamese, leveraging existing natural language inference (NLI) datasets. Additionally, we propose a process to adapt existing Vietnamese datasets for supervised learning, ensuring compatibility with CL methods. Our experiments demonstrate that ViCLSR significantly outperforms the powerful monolingual pre-trained model PhoBERT on five benchmark NLU datasets such as ViNLI (+6.97% F1), ViWikiFC (+4.97% F1), ViFactCheck (+9.02% F1), UIT-ViCTSD (+5.36% F1), and ViMMRC2.0 (+4.33% Accuracy). ViCLSR shows that supervised contrastive learning can effectively address resource limitations in Vietnamese NLU tasks and improve sentence representation learning for low-resource languages. Furthermore, we conduct an in-depth analysis of the experimental results to uncover the factors contributing to the superior performance of contrastive learning models. ViCLSR is released for research purposes in advancing natural language processing tasks.

</details>


### [51] [Evaluating Reasoning-Based Scaffolds for Human-AI Co-Annotation: The ReasonAlign Annotation Protocol](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21094)
*Smitha Muthya Sudheendra,Jaideep Srivastava*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how exposing human annotators to LLM-generated reasoning (without labels) affects their behavior and agreement in subjective NLP tasks, finding that such reasoning increases inter-annotator agreement with relatively few label revisions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human annotation for subjective NLP tasks often shows high variability across annotators, complicating evaluation. LLMs can generate explanations that might guide annotators, but their precise impact on human decision-making and annotation consistency is not well understood. The authors aim to systematically study how model-generated reasoning (without revealing labels) influences annotator behavior, rather than simply measuring accuracy.

Method: The authors propose ReasonAlign, a reasoning-based annotation scaffold where annotators label data in two passes. In the first pass, annotators independently label instances. In the second pass, they see only LLM-generated reasoning (explanations) for the instance, not the model’s predicted label, and can revise their decisions. They apply this protocol to sentiment classification and opinion detection tasks, and analyze changes in inter-annotator agreement and revision patterns. They introduce the Annotator Effort Proxy (AEP), which measures the proportion of labels revised after exposure to reasoning, to quantify how much effort or change the reasoning triggers.

Result: Across the evaluated tasks, exposing annotators to LLM-generated reasoning leads to higher inter-annotator agreement while requiring relatively few revisions, as captured by low AEP values. This suggests that the reasoning is most influential in resolving a subset of ambiguous or contentious cases rather than broadly altering annotator judgments.

Conclusion: Reasoning-based scaffolds like ReasonAlign can improve annotation consistency in subjective NLP tasks by presenting LLM explanations without labels. The approach enhances agreement with minimal annotator effort, indicating that structured reasoning chiefly helps clarify ambiguous instances. These insights support the use of reasoning-augmented workflows in human-AI annotation pipelines and motivate further study of how model explanations shape human judgments.

Abstract: Human annotation is central to NLP evaluation, yet subjective tasks often exhibit substantial variability across annotators. While large language models (LLMs) can provide structured reasoning to support annotation, their influence on human annotation behavior remains unclear.
  We introduce ReasonAlign, a reasoning-based annotation scaffold that exposes LLM-generated explanations while withholding predicted labels. We frame this as a controlled study of how reasoning affects human annotation behavior, rather than a full evaluation of annotation accuracy. Using a two-pass protocol inspired by Delphi-style revision, annotators first label instances independently and then revise their decisions after viewing model-generated reasoning.
  We evaluate the approach on sentiment classification and opinion detection tasks, analyzing changes in inter-annotator agreement and revision behavior. To quantify these effects, we introduce the Annotator Effort Proxy (AEP), a metric capturing the proportion of labels revised after exposure to reasoning. Our results show that exposure to reasoning is associated with increased agreement alongside minimal revision, suggesting that reasoning primarily helps resolve ambiguous cases without inducing widespread changes.
  These findings provide insight into how reasoning explanations shape annotation consistency and highlight reasoning-based scaffolds as a practical mechanism for supporting human-AI annotation workflows.

Across domains, the main bottleneck is missing cultural knowledge rather than visual grounding alone, with knowledge-intensive categories. These findings position BanglaVerse as a more realistic test bed for measuring culturally grounded multimodal understanding under linguistic variation.

### [53] [Entropy Alone is Insufficient for Safe Selective Prediction in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21172)
*Edward Phillips,Fredrik K. Gustafsson,Sean Wu,Anshul Thakur,David A. Clifton*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper improves selective prediction for language models by fixing failures of entropy-based uncertainty measures using a correctness probe, yielding better risk–coverage and calibration across QA tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Language models hallucinate, causing harmful errors. Selective prediction can reduce harm by abstaining in high-risk cases, but current uncertainty measures like entropy are not well evaluated for real-world deployment, especially at very low target error rates. Moreover, entropy-based measures can fail in model-specific ways, leading to unreliable abstention behavior. The authors aim to build uncertainty methods that are trustworthy at specified risk levels.

Method: The authors analyze a model-dependent failure mode of entropy-based uncertainty quantification in language models used for selective prediction. To address this, they propose combining the standard entropy-based uncertainty score with an additional correctness probe signal—an auxiliary predictor of whether the model’s answer is correct. They then evaluate this combined score as a selective prediction policy across multiple question answering benchmarks and model families, focusing on risk–coverage trade-offs and calibration at low error rates.

Result: Across three QA benchmarks (TriviaQA, BioASQ, MedicalQA) and four different model families, the combined entropy-plus-correctness-probe uncertainty score outperforms entropy-only baselines. It yields better risk–coverage trade-offs—meaning for a given coverage it achieves lower error—and better calibration, indicating that predicted risks more reliably match actual error rates, particularly in deployment-relevant low-risk regimes.

Conclusion: Entropy-only uncertainty measures can exhibit model-dependent failure modes that make abstention policies unreliable at low target error rates. Augmenting entropy with a correctness probe signal substantially improves both risk–coverage performance and calibration across diverse tasks and models. Effective deployment of selective prediction systems therefore requires evaluation protocols and metrics that directly measure whether a system can reliably operate at declared risk levels, rather than relying solely on generic uncertainty metrics.

Abstract: Selective prediction systems can mitigate harms resulting from language model hallucinations by abstaining from answering in high-risk cases. Uncertainty quantification techniques are often employed to identify such cases, but are rarely evaluated in the context of the wider selective prediction policy and its ability to operate at low target error rates. We identify a model-dependent failure mode of entropy-based uncertainty methods that leads to unreliable abstention behaviour, and address it by combining entropy scores with a correctness probe signal. We find that across three QA benchmarks (TriviaQA, BioASQ, MedicalQA) and four model families, the combined score generally improves both the risk--coverage trade-off and calibration performance relative to entropy-only baselines. Our results highlight the importance of deployment-facing evaluation of uncertainty methods, using metrics that directly reflect whether a system can be trusted to operate at a stated risk level.

Method: They define a new task called Dissimilar Span Detection (DSD), where given two texts the system must detect which spans differ semantically. Conclusion: Dissimilar Span Detection is a challenging but valuable extension of traditional STS: it makes similarity judgments more interpretable by localizing semantic mismatches and can tangibly benefit downstream tasks like paraphrase detection. The released Span Similarity Dataset and initial baselines provide a foundation for future research on fine-grained semantic comparison between texts.

Abstract: Semantic Textual Similarity (STS) is a crucial component of many Natural Language Processing (NLP) applications. However, existing approaches typically reduce semantic nuances to a single score, limiting interpretability. To address this, we introduce the task of Dissimilar Span Detection (DSD), which aims to identify semantically differing spans between pairs of texts. This can help users understand which particular words or tokens negatively affect the similarity score, or be used to improve performance in STS-dependent downstream tasks. Furthermore, we release a new dataset suitable for the task, the Span Similarity Dataset (SSD), developed through a semi-automated pipeline combining large language models (LLMs) with human verification. We propose and evaluate different baseline methods for DSD, both unsupervised, based on LIME, SHAP, LLMs, and our own method, as well as an additional supervised approach. While LLMs and supervised models achieve the highest performance, overall results remain low, highlighting the complexity of the task. Finally, we set up an additional experiment that shows how DSD can lead to increased performance in the specific task of paraphrase detection.

*Sai Koneru,Jian Wu,Sarah Rajtmajer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how to better extract hypotheses and their supporting statistical evidence from full scientific articles by optimizing retrieval of relevant paragraphs for large language model extractors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Synthesizing empirical findings across many papers requires identifying key hypotheses and the statistical evidence supporting them. However, this is difficult because scientific arguments are spread across long documents and multiple sections, and many paragraphs are topically related but play different rhetorical roles. The authors aim to understand and improve how systems retrieve the most relevant parts of a paper for downstream information extraction.

Method: The authors cast the task as a sequential full-text extraction problem: link a primary finding stated in the abstract to (i) the corresponding hypothesis in the body and (ii) its statistical evidence. They use a two-stage retrieve-and-extract framework and run a controlled set of experiments. They systematically vary: (a) how much context is retrieved, (b) the quality of retrieval (standard retrieval-augmented generation, reranking, and a fine-tuned retriever + reranker), and (c) an oracle paragraph condition where the correct paragraph is given. They test these retrieval configurations across four different LLM-based extractors to disentangle retrieval errors from extraction limitations.

Result: Targeted, high-quality context selection substantially improves hypothesis extraction compared with simply prompting on the full text. The best gains occur when retrieval is both accurate and yields "clean" context with fewer off-target but topically related paragraphs. However, extracting the supporting statistical evidence is still difficult. Even in the oracle setting where the right paragraph is provided, performance on evidence extraction is only moderate, suggesting that current LLM extractors struggle with complex, mixed numeric-textual statements rather than being limited solely by retrieval quality.

Conclusion: Optimizing retrieval—particularly using fine-tuned retrievers and reranking to provide focused, clean context—meaningfully improves hypothesis extraction from full-text scientific articles, but is insufficient for robust extraction of statistical evidence. The persistent difficulty in evidence extraction, even with oracle context, indicates that advances are needed in extractor models themselves to better handle hybrid numeric-textual reasoning, not just in retrieval strategies.

Abstract: Extracting hypotheses and their supporting statistical evidence from full-text scientific articles is central to the synthesis of empirical findings, but remains difficult due to document length and the distribution of scientific arguments across sections of the paper. The work studies a sequential full-text extraction setting, where the statement of a primary finding in an article's abstract is linked to (i) a corresponding hypothesis statement in the paper body and (ii) the statistical evidence that supports or refutes that hypothesis. This formulation induces a challenging within-document retrieval setting in which many candidate paragraphs are topically related to the finding but differ in rhetorical role, creating hard negatives for retrieval and extraction. Using a two-stage retrieve-and-extract framework, we conduct a controlled study of retrieval design choices, varying context quantity, context quality (standard Retrieval Augmented Generation, reranking, and a fine-tuned retriever paired with reranking), as well as an oracle paragraph setting to separate retrieval failures from extraction limits across four Large Language Model extractors. We find that targeted context selection consistently improves hypothesis extraction relative to full-text prompting, with gains concentrated in configurations that optimize retrieval quality and context cleanliness. In contrast, statistical evidence extraction remains substantially harder. Even with oracle paragraphs, performance remains moderate, indicating persistent extractor limitations in handling hybrid numeric-textual statements rather than retrieval failures alone.

### [56] [Graph Fusion Across Languages using Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21248)
*Kaung Myat Kyaw,Khush Agarwal,Jonathan Chan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a framework that uses large language models (LLMs) to fuse multiple multilingual knowledge graphs by turning graph triples into natural language and letting the LLM align entities and relations across languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Combining knowledge graphs that come from different languages is hard because of semantic mismatches and complex graph structures. Existing cross-lingual alignment methods can be brittle, model-specific, or hard to scale as new graphs arrive. The authors aim to explore whether LLMs, with their multilingual understanding and in-context reasoning abilities, can act as a general, flexible mechanism to integrate heterogeneous multilingual KGs over time.

Method: They propose a cross-lingual graph fusion framework that linearizes KG structure into text. Each triple is expressed as a natural language sequence of the form [head] [relation] [tail]. An LLM is then prompted with triples from a current fused graph (Gc^(t−1)) and a new candidate graph (Gt) and asked to map relations and reconcile entities across them, effectively deciding alignments and merges. The process is applied iteratively to sequentially add more graphs to the fused KG. The approach is evaluated using the multilingual DBP15K dataset.

Result: On DBP15K, the approach shows that LLMs can successfully align and merge entities and relations across different-language KGs, enabling sequential fusion of multiple heterogeneous graphs. The experiments indicate that an LLM can function as a cross-lingual semantic bridge, handling semantic heterogeneity sufficiently well to build a growing unified graph from multiple sources.

Conclusion: The study suggests that LLMs, when given a linearized textual view of KGs, can provide a scalable and modular solution for cross-lingual KG fusion. By serving as a universal semantic bridge, they allow sequential, continuous synthesis of knowledge from multiple multilingual graphs, reducing reliance on specialized alignment models and offering a flexible way to integrate heterogeneous KGs over time.

Abstract: Combining multiple knowledge graphs (KGs) across linguistic boundaries is a persistent challenge due to semantic heterogeneity and the complexity of graph environments. We propose a framework for cross-lingual graph fusion, leveraging the in-context reasoning and multilingual semantic priors of Large Language Models (LLMs). The framework implements structural linearization by mapping triplets directly into natural language sequences (e.g., [head] [relation] [tail]), enabling the LLM to map relations and reconcile entities between an evolving fused graph ($G_{c}^{(t-1)}$) and a new candidate graph ($G_{t}$). Evaluated on the DBP15K dataset, this exploratory study demonstrates that LLMs can serve as a universal semantic bridge to resolve cross-lingual discrepancies. Results show the successful sequential agglomeration of multiple heterogeneous graphs, offering a scalable, modular solution for continuous knowledge synthesis in multi-source, multilingual environments.

### [57] [Conversation Tree Architecture: A Structured Framework for Context-Aware Multi-Branch LLM Conversations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21278)
*Pranav Hemanth,Sampriti Saha*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a tree-structured conversation architecture for LLMs to prevent different topics from interfering and degrading answer quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard chat interfaces keep a single, ever-growing context where all prior messages are appended. As a conversation becomes long and multi-topic, unrelated threads share the same context. This causes responses to be influenced by irrelevant prior content, which the authors call logical context poisoning. There is a need for a principled way to partition and manage context so that LLMs can sustain long, multi-topic, and possibly multi-agent conversations without quality degradation.

Method: The authors design the Conversation Tree Architecture (CTA), which represents a dialogue as a tree of nodes, where each node corresponds to a discrete conversational context. Each node has its own local context window instead of relying on a single global history. They define rules for context flow: (1) downstream, when new branches (child nodes) are created, some subset of the parent’s context is passed down; (2) upstream, when branches are deleted or merged, selected information from child nodes can be merged back into the parent. They also introduce volatile nodes—temporary branches whose content is not automatically preserved and must either be selectively merged upward or discarded before they are purged. The paper formalizes these primitives, outlines design choices for context selection and flow, connects the framework to existing ideas in LLM memory and context management, and presents a prototype implementation to demonstrate feasibility.

Result: They obtain a working prototype implementation of CTA that demonstrates how conversations can be structured as context-isolated nodes organized in a tree, with explicit rules for propagating and merging context. The architecture successfully decouples different conversational threads, allowing the system to avoid the logical context poisoning that arises from flat, append-only histories. They also clarify and systematize the design space of context flow policies (what to propagate, merge, or discard) and show that their framework can naturally generalize to multi-agent scenarios.

Conclusion: The Conversation Tree Architecture offers a principled way to structure and manage conversational context for LLMs, replacing a single unbounded history with a hierarchy of local context windows. This tree-based approach mitigates logical context poisoning by isolating topics into separate nodes, while still supporting controlled information sharing via well-defined context flow mechanisms and volatile nodes. The framework unifies and extends prior memory-management ideas and is applicable not only to single LLM chats but also to more complex multi-agent interactions.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed for extended, multi-topic conversations, yet the flat, append-only structure of current conversation interfaces introduces a fundamental limitation: all context accumulates in a single unbounded window, causing topically distinct threads to bleed into one another and progressively degrade response quality. We term this failure mode logical context poisoning. In this paper, we introduce the Conversation Tree Architecture (CTA), a hierarchical framework that organizes LLM conversations as trees of discrete, context-isolated nodes. Each node maintains its own local context window; structured mechanisms govern how context flows between parent and child nodes, downstream on branch creation and upstream on branch deletion. We additionally introduce volatile nodes, transient branches whose local context must be selectively merged upward or permanently discarded before purging. We formalize the architecture's primitives, characterize the open design problems in context flow, relate our framework to prior work in LLM memory management, and describe a working prototype implementation. The CTA provides a principled foundation for structured conversational context management and extends naturally to multi-agent settings.

</details>


### [58] [More Than Sum of Its Parts: Deciphering Intent Shifts in Multimodal Hate Speech Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21298)
*Runze Sun,Yu Zheng,Zexuan Xiong,Zhongjin Qu,Lei Chen,Jiwen Lu,Jie Zhou*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

These agents perform asymmetric reasoning over multimodal inputs and debate before the system produces a final verdict.

Result: On the new H-VLI benchmark, ARCADE substantially outperforms prior state-of-the-art models, especially on difficult implicit hate cases that require nuanced multimodal understanding. Conclusion: Modeling the interaction between image and text and focusing on semantic intent shifts is crucial for handling modern, multimodal hate speech. A courtroom-style, multi-agent debate framework like ARCADE can significantly enhance the detection of implicit and subtle hate while remaining competitive on conventional tasks. H-VLI provides a challenging, realistic benchmark to drive future research in multimodal hate-speech understanding.

Abstract: Combating hate speech on social media is critical for securing cyberspace, yet relies heavily on the efficacy of automated detection systems. As content formats evolve, hate speech is transitioning from solely plain text to complex multimodal expressions, making implicit attacks harder to spot. Current systems, however, often falter on these subtle cases, as they struggle with multimodal content where the emergent meaning transcends the aggregation of individual modalities. To bridge this gap, we move beyond binary classification to characterize semantic intent shifts where modalities interact to construct implicit hate from benign cues or neutralize toxicity through semantic inversion. Guided by this fine-grained formulation, we curate the Hate via Vision-Language Interplay (H-VLI) benchmark where the true intent hinges on the intricate interplay of modalities rather than overt visual or textual slurs. To effectively decipher these complex cues, we further propose the Asymmetric Reasoning via Courtroom Agent DEbate (ARCADE) framework. By simulating a judicial process where agents actively argue for accusation and defense, ARCADE forces the model to scrutinize deep semantic cues before reaching a verdict. Extensive experiments demonstrate that ARCADE significantly outperforms state-of-the-art baselines on H-VLI, particularly for challenging implicit cases, while maintaining competitive performance on established benchmarks. Our code and data are available at: https://github.com/Sayur1n/H-VLI

### [59] [enhancing reasoning accuracy in large language models during inference time](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21301)
*Vinay Sharma,Manish Jain*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares three inference-time strategies—self-consistency, dual-model agreement, and self-reflection—for improving LLM reasoning, finding self-consistency with controlled sampling works best for smaller non-reasoning models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are good at language but unreliable at multi-step reasoning without extra training. The authors want methods that improve reasoning accuracy using only inference-time techniques, avoiding costly fine-tuning or architectural changes, and they want a controlled comparison of the leading approaches.

Method: They use Chain-of-Thought prompting for all methods and systematically test three strategies: (i) self-consistency via multiple stochastic CoT samples with nucleus sampling and tuned temperature, selecting the most common final answer; (ii) dual-model agreement, where two independent models generate CoTs and only consistent answers/reasoning are accepted; and (iii) self-reflection, where a model critiques and revises its own initial CoT. All are evaluated under matched prompting and verification settings on LLM benchmarks.

Result: Self-consistency with nucleus sampling and controlled temperature gives 9–15 percentage points higher accuracy than standard greedy single-pass decoding, with relatively low computational overhead. Dual-model agreement further increases reliability at the cost of more compute, making it suitable for higher-stakes settings. Self-reflection yields only marginal gains for the smaller non-reasoning models studied.

Conclusion: Inference-time strategies can substantially boost LLM reasoning without retraining, but their effectiveness varies. Self-consistency is the most cost-effective and broadly useful, dual-model agreement is preferable when extra compute is acceptable for higher reliability, and self-reflection appears weak for smaller models, indicating that not all inference-time reasoning augmentations are equally beneficial.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) often exhibit strong linguistic abilities while remaining unreliable on multi-step reasoning tasks, particularly when deployed without additional training or fine-tuning. In this work, we study inference-time techniques to improve the reasoning accuracy of LLMs. We systematically evaluate three classes of inference-time strategies: (i) self-consistency via stochastic decoding, where the model is sampled multiple times using controlled temperature and nucleus sampling and the most frequent final answer is selected; (ii) dual-model reasoning agreement, where outputs from two independent models are compared and only consistent reasoning traces are trusted; and (iii) self-reflection, where the model critiques and revises its own reasoning. Across all evaluated methods, we employ Chain-of-Thought (CoT) [1] prompting to elicit explicit intermediate reasoning steps before generating final answers. In this work, we provide a controlled comparative evaluation across three inference-time strategies under identical prompting and verification settings. Our experiments on LLM [2] show that self-consistency with nucleus sampling and controlled temperature value yields the substantial gains, achieving a 9% to 15% absolute improvement in accuracy over greedy single-pass decoding, well-suited for low-risk domains, offering meaningful gains with minimal overhead. The dual-model approach provides additional confirmation for model reasoning steps thus more appropriate for moderate-risk domains, where higher reliability justifies additional compute. Self-reflection offers only marginal improvements, suggesting limited effectiveness for smaller non-reasoning models at inference time.

</details>


### [60] [TimeTox: An LLM-Based Pipeline for Automated Extraction of Time Toxicity from Clinical Trial Protocols](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21335)
*Saketh Vinjamuri,Marielle Fis Loperena,Marie C. Spezia,Ramez Kouzy*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents TimeTox, an LLM-based pipeline that automatically measures “time toxicity” (healthcare contact days) from clinical trial protocols, showing that extraction stability on real-world data is more critical for deployment than synthetic-benchmark accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Measuring time toxicity—how much time patients spend in healthcare settings due to trial participation—is clinically important but currently requires manual, labor-intensive extraction from protocol documents, especially Schedule of Assessments tables. Existing methods do not scale to large numbers of protocols or treatment arms. The authors aim to automate and standardize this process so that time toxicity can be routinely quantified and compared across regimens and disease sites.

Method: The authors design TimeTox, a multi-stage pipeline using Google’s Gemini LLMs. Stage 1 extracts and summarizes the Schedule of Assessments from full protocol PDFs. Stage 2 quantifies time toxicity by estimating cumulative healthcare contact days at six predefined timepoints for each treatment arm. Stage 3 performs multiple independent LLM runs and reconciles them with position-based matching of arms to generate a consensus output. They test two architectures: (1) a single-pass “vanilla” approach where the LLM directly outputs cumulative time toxicity and (2) a two-stage “structure-then-count” approach where the model first structures the schedule and then computes days. They evaluate on 20 synthetic schedules (240 arm–timepoint comparisons) and examine run-to-run reproducibility on 644 real-world oncology protocols across three runs.

Result: On synthetic data, the two-stage structure-then-count approach vastly outperforms the vanilla pipeline, reaching 100% clinically acceptable accuracy within ±3 days and a mean absolute error of 0.81 days vs. 41.5% and 9.0 days MAE, respectively. On the larger set of real-world oncology protocols, however, the vanilla pipeline exhibits better reproducibility across runs: 95.3% of arm–timepoint estimates are clinically acceptable with interquartile range ≤3 days, and 82.0% are perfectly stable (IQR = 0) over three runs on 644 protocols. Using the chosen production configuration, the system successfully extracted time toxicity metrics for 1,288 treatment arms spanning multiple disease sites.

Conclusion: While more complex multi-step LLM pipelines may excel on synthetic benchmarks, their performance can be less stable on heterogeneous, real-world clinical protocols. For safe and scalable deployment of LLMs in clinical research workflows, extraction stability on real-world data is more important than peak accuracy on synthetic test sets. Accordingly, the authors select the more reproducible vanilla TimeTox architecture for production use, enabling large-scale, automated quantification of time toxicity across many oncology treatment arms and trial protocols.

Abstract: Time toxicity, the cumulative healthcare contact days from clinical trial participation, is an important but labor-intensive metric to extract from protocol documents. We developed TimeTox, an LLM-based pipeline for automated extraction of time toxicity from Schedule of Assessments tables. TimeTox uses Google's Gemini models in three stages: summary extraction from full-length protocol PDFs, time toxicity quantification at six cumulative timepoints for each treatment arm, and multi-run consensus via position-based arm matching. We validated against 20 synthetic schedules (240 comparisons) and assessed reproducibility on 644 real-world oncology protocols. Two architectures were compared: single-pass (vanilla) and two-stage (structure-then-count). The two-stage pipeline achieved 100% clinically acceptable accuracy ($\pm$3 days) on synthetic data (MAE 0.81 days) versus 41.5% for vanilla (MAE 9.0 days). However, on real-world protocols, the vanilla pipeline showed superior reproducibility: 95.3% clinically acceptable accuracy (IQR $\leq$ 3 days) across 3 runs on 644 protocols, with 82.0% perfect stability (IQR = 0). The production pipeline extracted time toxicity for 1,288 treatment arms across multiple disease sites. Extraction stability on real-world data, rather than accuracy on synthetic benchmarks, is the decisive factor for production LLM deployment.

</details>


### [61] [Beyond Memorization: Distinguishing between Reductive and Epistemic Reasoning in LLMs using Classic Logic Puzzles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21350)
*Adi Gabay,Gabriel Stanovsky,Liat Peterfreund*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models (LLMs) solve epistemic reasoning puzzles, arguing that apparent “memorization” is actually a kind of problem reduction, and shows that models struggle when true epistemic reasoning is needed.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Epistemic reasoning—reasoning about what agents know or believe—is central to many tasks, but current LLM evaluations often treat success as either genuine reasoning or shallow memorization of canonical puzzles. This binary view is inadequate for modern models, which may instead map new inputs onto familiar templates. The authors aim to more precisely characterize how LLMs handle epistemic puzzles and where their abilities break down.

Method: The authors conceptualize memorization as a form of reduction: mapping a new instance of a problem to a previously seen canonical epistemic puzzle. They construct a “reduction ladder,” a sequence of systematically modified puzzle instances that gradually diverge from canonical forms while preserving the underlying logical structure. They then test multiple large language models along this ladder to see how performance changes as reduction becomes harder and genuine epistemic reasoning is required.

Result: The experiments show that some large models can correctly solve puzzles that are close variants of canonical forms, indicating successful reduction. However, other models fail even on relatively small deviations from known instances, suggesting limited reduction abilities. Across all evaluated models, performance degrades substantially once the instances are modified enough that straightforward reduction is no longer possible, revealing a consistent weakness in genuine epistemic reasoning.

Conclusion: Memorization in modern LLMs is better viewed as a specific kind of reduction from new tasks to stored canonical problems rather than a separate, brittle behavior. The proposed reduction ladder exposes distinct regimes: easy cases solvable by reduction and harder cases that require true epistemic reasoning, where current models consistently falter. This framework offers a more nuanced understanding of LLM behavior on epistemic tasks and a more fine-grained way to probe and benchmark their reasoning abilities.

Abstract: Epistemic reasoning requires agents to infer the state of the world from partial observations and information about other agents' knowledge. Prior work evaluating LLMs on canonical epistemic puzzles interpreted their behavior through a dichotomy between epistemic reasoning and brittle memorization. We argue that this framing is incomplete: in recent models, memorization is better understood as a special case of reduction, where a new instance is mapped onto a known problem. Instead, we introduce a reduction ladder, a sequence of modifications that progressively move instances away from a canonical epistemic puzzle, making reduction increasingly difficult while preserving the underlying logic. We find that while some large models succeed via reduction, other models fail early, and all models struggle once epistemic reasoning is required.

Moreover, simply scaling model size does not reliably reduce these dialectal biases. The LLM-as-a-judge translation evaluation aligns better with human assessment than traditional automatic metrics, and the authors release a rigorously validated benchmark and measurements across 19 LLMs.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that current LLMs exhibit marked dialectal bias across Bengali varieties, and that these disparities persist even in larger models, raising concerns for equitable and safe deployment in multilingual, dialect-rich settings. The proposed framework—including an LLM-judged translation fidelity method, a dialectal QA benchmark, and the Critical Bias Sensitivity metric—offers practical tools for diagnosing and quantifying such bias, particularly in safety-critical applications, and can inform future model training and evaluation strategies aimed at improving dialectal robustness.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) frequently exhibit performance biases against regional dialects of low-resource languages. However, frameworks to quantify these disparities remain scarce. We propose a two-phase framework to evaluate dialectal bias in LLM question-answering across nine Bengali dialects. First, we translate and gold-label standard Bengali questions into dialectal variants adopting a retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) pipeline to prepare 4,000 question sets. Since traditional translation quality evaluation metrics fail on unstandardized dialects, we evaluate fidelity using an LLM-as-a-judge, which human correlation confirms outperforms legacy metrics. Second, we benchmark 19 LLMs across these gold-labeled sets, running 68,395 RLAIF evaluations validated through multi-judge agreement and human fallback. Our findings reveal severe performance drops linked to linguistic divergence. For instance, responses to the highly divergent Chittagong dialect score 5.44/10, compared to 7.68/10 for Tangail. Furthermore, increased model scale does not consistently mitigate this bias. We contribute a validated translation quality evaluation method, a rigorous benchmark dataset, and a Critical Bias Sensitivity (CBS) metric for safety-critical applications.

Conclusion: The study shows that small language models can be more suitable than larger ones for production scenarios where inference efficiency is critical and marginal accuracy gains from scaling up are not worth the extra cost. Abstract: Large Language Models achieve remarkable performance but incur substantial computational costs unsuitable for resource-constrained deployments. We introduce the Performance-Efficiency Ratio (PER), a novel metric integrating accuracy, throughput, memory, and latency through geometric mean normalization. Our systematic evaluation reveals that small models (0.5--3B parameters) achieve superior PER scores across all given tasks. These findings establish quantitative foundations for deploying small models in production environments prioritizing inference efficiency over marginal accuracy gains.

### [65] [Multi-Perspective LLM Annotations for Valid Analyses in Subjective Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21404)
*Navya Mehrotra,Adam Visokay,Kristina Gligorić*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Perspective-Driven Inference, a method to better estimate how different demographic groups would label subjective text tasks by combining LLM-based annotations with a small, carefully allocated pool of human annotations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM-based text annotation often assumes a single ground truth label, which breaks down for inherently subjective tasks (e.g., politeness, offensiveness) where different demographic groups have legitimately different perspectives. Existing error-correction methods for LLM annotations typically try to get closer to one true label, ignoring meaningful cross-group disagreement, and they also lack efficient strategies for spending limited human annotation budgets when some groups are harder for LLMs to model than others.

Method: The authors introduce Perspective-Driven Inference, which explicitly models the full distribution of labels across demographic groups instead of a single label. They use LLM predictions as noisy proxies for each group’s perspective, then design an adaptive sampling strategy for human annotations that focuses more effort on those demographic groups where the LLM proxy is least accurate or most uncertain, while using fewer human labels where the LLM is already reliable. This way, a small but strategically allocated human budget can correct group-specific biases in the LLM-based estimates.

Result: On politeness and offensiveness rating datasets, the method achieves better estimation quality for demographic groups that are harder for LLMs to model (e.g., underrepresented or systematically mispredicted groups) compared to baselines that spread human annotation effort uniformly across groups. At the same time, it maintains overall coverage of perspectives and does not sacrifice performance on easier or better-modeled groups.

Conclusion: Perspective-Driven Inference allows practitioners to treat cross-group disagreement as the target signal rather than as noise, combining LLM predictions with adaptively allocated human annotations to recover more accurate, group-specific label distributions under a limited budget. This leads to more faithful modeling of diverse perspectives on subjective language tasks such as politeness and offensiveness, and demonstrates that targeted human annotation can systematically improve fairness and representational coverage relative to uniform sampling schemes.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used to annotate texts, but their outputs reflect some human perspectives better than others. Existing methods for correcting LLM annotation error assume a single ground truth. However, this assumption fails in subjective tasks where disagreement across demographic groups is meaningful. Here we introduce Perspective-Driven Inference, a method that treats the distribution of annotations across groups as the quantity of interest, and estimates it using a small human annotation budget. We contribute an adaptive sampling strategy that concentrates human annotation effort on groups where LLM proxies are least accurate. We evaluate on politeness and offensiveness rating tasks, showing targeted improvements for harder-to-model demographic groups relative to uniform sampling baselines, while maintaining coverage.

Method: Use BERTimbau (Base and Large) on the SQuAD-BR benchmark and systematically combine four parameter-efficient fine-tuning approaches (LoRA, DoRA, QLoRA, QDoRA) with quantization. Overall, smaller encoder-based architectures offer a more sustainable, resource-efficient path for this QA task while aligning with Green AI goals.

Abstract: Although large language models have transformed natural language processing, their computational costs create accessibility barriers for low-resource languages such as Brazilian Portuguese. This work presents a systematic evaluation of Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) and quantization techniques applied to BERTimbau for Question Answering on SQuAD-BR, the Brazilian Portuguese translation of SQuAD v1. We evaluate 40 configurations combining four PEFT methods (LoRA, DoRA, QLoRA, QDoRA) across two model sizes (Base: 110M, Large: 335M parameters). Our findings reveal three critical insights: (1) LoRA achieves 95.8\% of baseline performance on BERTimbau-Large while reducing training time by 73.5\% (F1=81.32 vs 84.86); (2) higher learning rates (2e-4) substantially improve PEFT performance, with F1 gains of up to +19.71 points over standard rates; and (3) larger models show twice the quantization resilience (loss of 4.83 vs 9.56 F1 points). These results demonstrate that encoder-based models can be efficiently fine-tuned for extractive Brazilian Portuguese QA with substantially lower computational cost than large generative LLMs, promoting more sustainable approaches aligned with \textit{Green AI} principles. An exploratory evaluation of Tucano and Sabiá on the same extractive QA benchmark shows that while generative models can reach competitive F1 scores with LoRA fine-tuning, they require up to 4.2$\times$ more GPU memory and 3$\times$ more training time than BERTimbau-Base, reinforcing the efficiency advantage of smaller encoder-based architectures for this task.

### [67] [Semantic Shift: the Fundamental Challenge in Text Embedding and Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21437)
*Hang Gao,Dimitris N. Metaxas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why and when pooling-based Transformer embeddings suffer from geometric pathologies in retrieval, arguing that semantic shift—how meaning evolves and disperses within a text—is the key causal factor.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer embedding models use pooling to get fixed-size vectors for variable-length text, which is essential for efficient similarity search. However, this induces anisotropy and length-related embedding collapse. Prior work describes these geometric issues but does not clearly explain under what semantic conditions they actually hurt retrieval performance. The authors aim to identify a semantic, causal explanation rather than relying on superficial features like text length.

Method: The authors theoretically analyze how pooling leads to semantic smoothing: as a text’s internal semantic diversity increases, the pooled embedding is forced to move away from each constituent sentence embedding, becoming less discriminative. They then define a quantitative measure of semantic shift that combines local semantic evolution (how meaning changes from one part to the next) and global semantic dispersion (overall spread of meanings in the text). They run controlled experiments across multiple corpora and embedding models, correlating semantic shift, embedding concentration, and retrieval performance.

Result: They find that semantic shift strongly correlates with the degree of embedding concentration and accurately predicts when retrieval performance degrades. In contrast, simple text length does not reliably predict these failures. The experiments show that higher semantic shift leads to more severe pooling-induced smoothing and worse retrieval, across different models and datasets.

Conclusion: Semantic shift is proposed as a unified explanatory and diagnostic concept for understanding when pooling-based Transformer embeddings suffer harmful anisotropy and embedding collapse. Rather than treating geometric pathologies as purely structural issues or attributing them to length alone, practitioners can use semantic shift to anticipate and diagnose retrieval failures, guiding the design and use of embedding models.

Abstract: Transformer-based embedding models rely on pooling to map variable-length text into a single vector, enabling efficient similarity search but also inducing well-known geometric pathologies such as anisotropy and length-induced embedding collapse. Existing accounts largely describe \emph{what} these pathologies look like, yet provide limited insight into \emph{when} and \emph{why} they harm downstream retrieval. In this work, we argue that the missing causal factor is \emph{semantic shift}: the intrinsic, structured evolution and dispersion of semantics within a text.
  We first present a theoretical analysis of \emph{semantic smoothing} in Transformer embeddings: as the semantic diversity among constituent sentences increases, the pooled representation necessarily shifts away from every individual sentence embedding, yielding a smoothed and less discriminative vector. Building on this foundation, we formalize semantic shift as a computable measure integrating local semantic evolution and global semantic dispersion. Through controlled experiments across corpora and multiple embedding models, we show that semantic shift aligns closely with the severity of embedding concentration and predicts retrieval degradation, whereas text length alone does not. Overall, semantic shift offers a unified and actionable lens for understanding embedding collapse and for diagnosing when anisotropy becomes harmful.

### [68] [PROMPT2BOX: Uncovering Entailment Structure among LLM Prompts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21438)
*Neeladri Bhuiya,Shib Sankar Dasgupta,Andrew McCallum,Haw-Shiuan Chang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces PROMPT2BOX, a box-embedding method that models both semantic similarity and specificity relations between prompts, enabling finer-grained analysis of LLM weaknesses than standard vector embeddings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing analyses of LLM weaknesses rely on vector embeddings and clustering of prompts, but these embeddings mainly capture topical similarity. Prompts that differ in specificity and difficulty but share a topic are often mapped nearby, hindering precise identification of where LLMs struggle. There is a need for a representation that encodes not just similarity but also hierarchical specificity among prompts to better analyze and visualize LLM performance and weaknesses.

Method: The authors propose PROMPT2BOX, which uses a trained encoder to map prompts into a box embedding space instead of a vector space. Box embeddings allow modeling inclusion relations, so that more specific prompts can be represented as boxes contained within more general ones (e.g., "writing an adventure story" inside "writing a story"). The encoder is trained on existing and synthesized datasets to learn both semantic similarity and specificity relations. Additionally, they introduce a new dimensionality-reduction technique tailored for box embeddings to support visualization and dataset comparison. PROMPT2BOX is then applied to build hierarchical clustering trees of prompts for 17 LLMs using the UltraFeedback dataset.

Result: Empirical evaluations show that box embeddings capture prompt specificity relations more effectively than vector-based baselines. In the downstream task of creating hierarchical clustering trees for 17 LLMs, PROMPT2BOX identifies 8.9% more LLM weaknesses compared to vector baselines and yields about a 33% higher correlation between the hierarchical depth in the clustering trees and the true instruction specificity of prompts.

Conclusion: PROMPT2BOX provides a richer representation of prompts by encoding both semantic similarity and specificity through box embeddings. This leads to more accurate, fine-grained identification and visualization of LLM weaknesses than traditional vector embeddings. The improved capture of hierarchical specificity and better alignment between clustering depth and instruction specificity suggest that box embeddings are a promising direction for analyzing and comparing LLM behavior across datasets and models.

Abstract: To discover the weaknesses of LLMs, researchers often embed prompts into a vector space and cluster them to extract insightful patterns. However, vector embeddings primarily capture topical similarity. As a result, prompts that share a topic but differ in specificity, and consequently in difficulty, are often represented similarly, making fine-grained weakness analysis difficult. To address this limitation, we propose PROMPT2BOX, which embeds prompts into a box embedding space using a trained encoder. The encoder, trained on existing and synthesized datasets, outputs box embeddings that capture not only semantic similarity but also specificity relations between prompts (e.g., "writing an adventure story" is more specific than "writing a story"). We further develop a novel dimension reduction technique for box embeddings to facilitate dataset visualization and comparison. Our experiments demonstrate that box embeddings consistently capture prompt specificity better than vector baselines. On the downstream task of creating hierarchical clustering trees for 17 LLMs from the UltraFeedback dataset, PROMPT2BOX can identify 8.9\% more LLM weaknesses than vector baselines and achieves an approximately 33\% stronger correlation between hierarchical depth and instruction specificity.

### [69] [KG-Hopper: Empowering Compact Open LLMs with Knowledge Graph Reasoning via Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21440)
*Shuai Wang,Yinan Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: KG-Hopper is a reinforcement learning framework that enables a compact 7B-parameter LLM to perform integrated multi-hop reasoning over knowledge graphs in a single inference, outperforming larger multi-step systems and approaching proprietary model performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are strong at natural language but weak at knowledge-intensive, multi-hop reasoning, especially in Knowledge Base Question Answering where accurate traversal of large knowledge graphs is required. Existing methods rely on rigid, step-by-step pipelines that treat each reasoning step in isolation, which limits flexibility, prevents global optimization across steps, and causes error propagation when early steps are mistaken. A new approach is needed that lets smaller, open LLMs perform holistic, flexible KG reasoning efficiently.

Method: The authors introduce KG-Hopper, a reinforcement learning framework that trains a dedicated Reasoning LLM to internalize the entire knowledge graph traversal process into a single, unified “thinking” phase. Instead of explicit sequential hops, the model plans and executes multi-hop KG reasoning implicitly, allowing it to explore paths, handle cross-step dependencies, and even backtrack within one inference. The system is built around a compact 7B-parameter LLM and uses RL to optimize its traversal and decision strategies over KGs for KBQA tasks.

Result: Across eight knowledge-graph reasoning benchmarks, KG-Hopper with a 7B-parameter backbone consistently surpasses larger multi-step systems using models up to 70B parameters. Its performance approaches that of proprietary closed-source systems like GPT-3.5-Turbo and GPT-4o-mini, while using fewer parameters and remaining open and data-efficient.

Conclusion: Embedding multi-hop KG reasoning into a single RL-trained reasoning phase allows a compact open LLM to perform flexible, globally optimized KG traversal. This unified reasoning strategy mitigates error cascades inherent in step-wise pipelines and delivers strong KBQA performance that exceeds larger open models and is competitive with commercial LLMs, demonstrating an efficient path toward powerful, open, knowledge-intensive reasoning systems.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) demonstrate impressive natural language capabilities but often struggle with knowledge-intensive reasoning tasks. Knowledge Base Question Answering (KBQA), which leverages structured Knowledge Graphs (KGs) exemplifies this challenge due to the need for accurate multi-hop reasoning. Existing approaches typically perform sequential reasoning steps guided by predefined pipelines, restricting flexibility and causing error cascades due to isolated reasoning at each step. To address these limitations, we propose KG-Hopper, a novel Reinforcement Learning (RL) framework that empowers compact open LLMs with the ability to perform integrated multi-hop KG reasoning within a single inference round. Rather than reasoning step-by-step, we train a Reasoning LLM that embeds the entire KG traversal and decision process into a unified ``thinking'' stage, enabling global reasoning over cross-step dependencies and dynamic path exploration with backtracking. Experimental results on eight KG reasoning benchmarks show that KG-Hopper, based on a 7B-parameter LLM, consistently outperforms larger multi-step systems (up to 70B) and achieves competitive performance with proprietary models such as GPT-3.5-Turbo and GPT-4o-mini, while remaining compact, open, and data-efficient. The code is publicly available at: https://github.com/Wangshuaiia/KG-Hopper.

</details>


### [70] [Cross-Context Verification: Hierarchical Detection of Benchmark Contamination through Session-Isolated Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21454)
*Tae-Eun Song*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that current LLM coding benchmarks, especially SWE-bench Verified, are compromised by data leakage and poor test design, and introduces a new multi-run, multi-agent verification method that cleanly distinguishes genuine reasoning from memorized solutions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods for detecting benchmark contamination and verifying LLM coding performance mostly rely on surface signals (paraphrase checks, n-gram overlap, perplexity) and repeated verification. These approaches neither directly test whether a model is reasoning versus recalling, nor avoid human/agent confirmation bias, and in practice they generate many false positives and degrade accuracy. There is a need for a principled, black-box way to distinguish genuine problem solving from solution recall and to structure multi-agent verification so that it improves, rather than harms, reliability.

Method: The authors propose Cross-Context Verification (CCV) and the Hierarchical Cross-Context Architecture (HCCA). CCV runs the same benchmark problem across N independent model sessions and quantifies the diversity of resulting solutions; low diversity indicates memorized recall, while high diversity indicates genuine reasoning. HCCA is a multi-agent framework in which different analytical roles work under strict information restrictions, limiting cross-contamination of hypotheses and thus reducing confirmation bias when judging whether solutions are contaminated. They test these methods on nine SWE-bench Verified problems using Claude Opus 4.6 at temperature 0, conduct 45 trials, and analyze diversity metrics and contamination labels statistically (e.g., Mann-Whitney U). They also run a pilot with a multi-stage Worker–Verifier–Director pipeline without information restriction to test whether structural complexity alone helps.

Result: On the evaluated SWE-bench Verified subset, CCV perfectly separates contaminated cases (memorized solutions) from genuine reasoning, achieving Mann-Whitney U=0 with p≈0.012 and effect size r=1.0 between the two groups. The experiments reveal that contamination behaves in a binary fashion: the model either fully recalls the solution or not at all; absence of genuine reasoning acts as a perfect discriminator of contamination; and about one-third of prior contamination labels are actually false positives. The HCCA framework, through independent, restricted analyses, uncovers complex cases where contamination and genuine flaws coexist that single-analyst methods miss. The Worker–Verifier–Director pilot without strong information restriction fails, yielding fully sycophantic confirmation and no additional reliability gains.

Conclusion: Cross-Context Verification, combined with a carefully designed Hierarchical Cross-Context Architecture, offers a powerful black-box approach for distinguishing memorized from reasoned solutions in LLM coding benchmarks and for reducing false contamination labels. The results indicate that the key to reliable multi-agent verification is not more structural stages but strict information restriction that curbs confirmation bias. The work implies that a significant portion of current benchmark contamination assessments may be wrong and that future benchmark evaluation should incorporate cross-context diversity measures and independence-preserving analysis protocols. All accompanying code and data are made available to facilitate adoption and further study.

Abstract: LLM coding benchmarks face a credibility crisis: widespread solution leakage and test quality issues undermine SWE-bench Verified, while existing detection methods--paraphrase consistency, n-gram overlap, perplexity analysis--never directly observe whether a model reasons or recalls. Meanwhile, simply repeating verification degrades accuracy: multi-turn review generates false positives faster than it discovers true errors, suggesting that structural approaches are needed.
  We introduce Cross-Context Verification (CCV), a black-box method that solves the same benchmark problem in N independent sessions and measures solution diversity, combined with the Hierarchical Cross-Context Architecture (HCCA), a multi-agent analysis framework that prevents confirmation bias through intentional information restriction across specialized analytical roles.
  On 9 SWE-bench Verified problems (45 trials, Claude Opus 4.6, temperature 0), CCV achieves perfect separation between contaminated and genuine reasoning (Mann-Whitney U=0, p approx 0.012, r = 1.0). Key findings: (1) contamination is binary--models either recall perfectly or not at all; (2) reasoning absence is a perfect discriminator; (3) 33% of prior contamination labels are false positives; (4) HCCA's independent analysis structure discovers contamination-flaw composite cases that single-analyst approaches miss. A pilot experiment extending HCCA to multi-stage verification (Worker to Verifier to Director) yields a negative result--100% sycophantic confirmation--providing further evidence that information restriction, not structural complexity, is the key mechanism. We release all code and data.

</details>


### [71] [DRTriton: Large-Scale Synthetic Data Reinforcement Learning for Triton Kernel Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21465)
*Siqi Guo,Ming Lin,Tianbao Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces DRTriton, a framework that trains LLMs to automatically convert PyTorch code to highly optimized Triton/CUDA kernels using synthetic data, curriculum reinforcement learning, and test-time search, achieving strong speedups over existing LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Writing efficient CUDA kernels for generative AI workloads is difficult and time-consuming, and even strong LLMs like GPT-5.2 and Claude-Sonnet-4.5 perform poorly at reliably generating fast kernels from PyTorch code. There is a need for a scalable way to train models that can automatically produce high-performance GPU kernels with minimal human effort.

Method: The authors propose DRTriton, which has three main components: (1) CSP-DAG, a synthetic data generation algorithm that produces PyTorch-operator graphs with full coverage of the operator space, uniform sampling, and controllable difficulty; (2) a curriculum reinforcement learning scheme with decoupled rewards that separately optimizes for correctness (conversion success) and runtime performance (inference speed), allowing the model to learn both reliably; and (3) a test-time search algorithm that explores multiple Triton kernel candidates and selects the fastest one, further improving runtime efficiency.

Result: DRTriton-7B, a 7-billion-parameter model trained only on synthetic data, is evaluated on KernelBench Level 2. It achieves runtime speedups on 92% of the benchmarks, substantially outperforming GPT-5.2 (23%) and Claude-Sonnet-4.5 (19%), and is able to generalize to challenging real-world CUDA kernels that human experts find difficult to optimize.

Conclusion: Synthetic data plus curriculum RL and test-time search can effectively train an LLM to convert PyTorch code into highly optimized Triton/CUDA kernels. DRTriton significantly improves both success rate and speed over existing state-of-the-art LLMs and generalizes from synthetic training distributions to complex real-world GPU kernels.

Abstract: Developing efficient CUDA kernels is a fundamental yet challenging task in the generative AI industry. Recent researches leverage Large Language Models (LLMs) to automatically convert PyTorch reference implementations to CUDA kernels, significantly reducing the engineering efforts. State-of-the-art LLMs, such as GPT-5.2 and Claude-Sonnet-4.5, still struggle in this specific task. To address this challenge, we propose DRTriton, a scalable learning framework for training LLMs to convert PyTorch codes into highly optimized Triton kernels, which are then compiled to CUDA kernels at runtime. DRTriton consists of three key components: (i) a data synthetic algorithm CSP-DAG that guarantees full coverage and unbiased uniform sampling over the operator space with controlled difficulty; (ii) a curriculum reinforcement learning with decoupled reward efficiently optimizes conversion success rate and inference speed simultaneously; and (iii) a test-time search algorithm that further improves the inference speed of the generated Triton kernels. Notably, despite being trained exclusively on synthetic data, DRTriton generalizes effectively to real-world CUDA kernels that are challenging even for human experts. Experimental results show that DRTriton-7B achieves speedup on 92% of the KernelBench Level 2, compared to 23% for GPT-5.2 and 19% for Claude-Sonnet-4.5.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Naive multi-agent collaboration leads to issues like conflicting edits, unsynchronized dependencies, and difficulty merging partial work. Human developers already use mature collaboration tools to handle similar challenges, suggesting these primitives could inspire better AI coordination mechanisms.

Method: The authors design Centralized Asynchronous Isolated Delegation (CAID), a coordination paradigm built around three software engineering primitives: centralized task delegation, asynchronous execution, and isolated workspaces. A central manager creates dependency-aware task plans and delegates subtasks to multiple agents. Agents work concurrently in isolated workspaces (e.g., via git worktree), and their outputs are later integrated through structured branch-and-merge workflows using tools like git commit and git merge. Executable tests are used to verify and consolidate progress. The system is empirically evaluated on paper reproduction tasks (PaperBench) and Python library development tasks (Commit0).

Result: CAID significantly outperforms single-agent baselines, improving absolute accuracy by 26.7% on PaperBench (paper reproduction tasks) and 14.3% on Commit0 (Python library development tasks). The analysis highlights that branch-and-merge is the key coordination mechanism enabling robust asynchronous multi-agent collaboration.

Conclusion: Structured, SWE-inspired coordination via CAID makes asynchronous multi-agent systems more effective for long-horizon software engineering tasks. By leveraging core development primitives—centralized task planning, isolated workspaces, and branch-and-merge with test-based verification—multi-agent AI systems can achieve higher accuracy and more reliable integration than single-agent approaches. The work underscores the importance of explicit collaboration infrastructure for scaling agent-based software development.

Abstract: AI agents have become increasingly capable at isolated software engineering (SWE) tasks such as resolving issues on Github. Yet long-horizon tasks involving multiple interdependent subtasks still pose challenges both with respect to accuracy, and with respect to timely completion. A natural approach to solving these long-horizon tasks in a timely manner is asynchronous multi-agent collaboration, where multiple agents work on different parts of the task at the same time. But effective application of multi-agent systems has proven surprisingly difficult: concurrent edits by multiple agents interfere with each other, dependencies are difficult to synchronize, and combining partial progress into a coherent whole is challenging. On the other hand, human developers have long relied on mature collaboration infrastructure to manage these challenges in large software projects. Inspired by these collaboration primitives, we introduce Centralized Asynchronous Isolated Delegation (CAID), a structured multi-agent coordination paradigm grounded in three core SWE primitives: centralized task delegation, asynchronous execution, and isolated workspaces. CAID constructs dependency-aware task plans through a central manager, executes subtasks concurrently in isolated workspaces, and consolidates progress via structured integration with executable test-based verification. In empirical evaluation, we find that CAID improves accuracy over single-agent baselines by 26.7% absolute on paper reproduction tasks (PaperBench) and 14.3% on Python library development tasks (Commit0). Through systematic analysis, we find that branch-and-merge is a central coordination mechanism for multi-agent collaboration, and that SWE primitives such as git worktree, git commit, and git merge enable it to be realized in a reliable and executable manner.

Main category: cs.CL

  <summary>Details</summary>
