Method: The authors collected and processed over 1.7 million online news articles from Swiss digital outlets in French, German, and Italian. They apply a triangulated methodology: (1) quantitative text analysis using lexical metrics; (2) entity-centered analysis via named entity recognition and Wikidata-based entity linking to compare coverage across languages; (3) targeted sentiment analysis to assess evaluative tone around selected entities or topics; and (4) consensus-based change-point detection to identify moments of significant shifts in coverage over time. They further construct domestication profiles (describing how strongly news is oriented toward domestic vs foreign topics) and define a proximity salience ratio to measure the prominence of culturally or geographically proximate content, enabling principled cross-language comparison connected to theories of domestication and cultural proximity. Quantitative findings are then interpreted through qualitative examination of selected thematic, recurrent, and singular events.

Result: The study uncovers clear, distinct temporal patterns of news coverage across the three language regions. It shows that French-, German-, and Italian-language media differ in how they domesticate events, what levels of geographical and cultural proximity they emphasize, and how strongly they cover domestic versus foreign topics. The domestication profiles and proximity salience ratio reveal systematic differences in attention and framing linked to linguistic and cultural contexts. Change-point detection identifies key moments when coverage patterns shift, and sentiment analyses highlight divergences in evaluative tone across languages around comparable events or entities. The combination of quantitative indicators with qualitative case readings validates these patterns and shows how specific events or narratives play out differently across linguistic regions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that linguistic and cultural contexts significantly shape the temporal dynamics and framing of news coverage within a single national media ecosystem. Triangulating large-scale quantitative methods with qualitative interpretation provides a richer and more reliable understanding of these dynamics than any single method alone. The proposed domestication profiles, proximity salience ratio, and triangulated analytic framework offer a transferable toolkit for studying other multilingual or culturally diverse media environments. Overall, the work advances both empirical knowledge about Swiss digital media and methodological practice in media studies, demonstrating how internal linguistic diversity must be considered to fully grasp the formation of public discourse and collective narratives.

Abstract: Analyzing news coverage in multilingual societies can offer valuable insights into the dynamics of public discourse and the development of collective narratives, yet comprehensive studies that account for linguistic and cultural diversity within national media ecosystems remain limited, particularly in complex contexts such as Switzerland. This paper studies temporal trends in Swiss digital media across the country's three main linguistic regions, French, German, and Italian, using a triangulated methodology that combines quantitative analyses with qualitative insights. We collected and processed over 1.7 million news articles, applying lexical metrics, named entity recognition and Wikidata-based linking, targeted sentiment analysis, and consensus-based change-point detection. To enable principled cross-language comparisons and to connect to theories of domestication and cultural proximity, we derive domestication profiles together with a proximity salience ratio. Our analysis spans thematic, recurrent, and singular events. By integrating quantitative data with qualitative interpretation, we provide new insights into the dynamics of Swiss digital media and demonstrate the usefulness of triangulation in media studies. The findings reveal distinct temporal patterns and highlight how linguistic and cultural contexts influence reporting. Our approach offers a framework applicable to other multilingual or culturally diverse media environments, contributing to a deeper understanding of how news is shaped by linguistic and cultural factors.

### [75] [Generalizable Self-Evolving Memory for Automatic Prompt Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21520)
*Guanbao Liang,Yuanchen Bei,Sheng Zhou,Yuheng Qin,Huan Zhou,Bingxin Jia,Bin Li,Jiajun Bu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MemAPO is a memory-driven framework that turns prompt optimization into a reusable, continually improving experience system for LLMs, outperforming existing methods while being more efficient.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing automatic prompt optimization methods usually find a single specialized prompt per task, which fails to generalize across diverse queries and does not accumulate knowledge over time. There is a need for a framework that allows LLMs to reuse successful prompting strategies and learn from recurring errors across tasks, enabling better generalization and continual improvement.

Method: MemAPO introduces a dual-memory mechanism: one memory bank stores distilled strategy templates derived from successful reasoning trajectories, and another stores structured error patterns extracted from incorrect generations that represent recurring failure modes. For a new prompt, MemAPO retrieves relevant strategies and failure patterns from memory, then composes an optimized prompt that encourages effective reasoning and explicitly guards against known mistakes. The system performs iterative self-reflection and memory editing so that both strategy templates and error patterns are continuously updated as more prompts are processed.

Result: On a variety of benchmarks, MemAPO achieves consistently better performance than strong prompt optimization baselines. At the same time, it reduces the optimization cost, indicating greater efficiency in leveraging past experience for new queries.

Conclusion: Treating prompt optimization as experience accumulation via a dual-memory system enables LLMs to generalize better across heterogeneous tasks and improve over time. MemAPO provides a practical way to maintain and refine reusable prompting strategies and known failure patterns, yielding higher accuracy and lower cost than conventional, task-specific prompt optimization approaches.

Abstract: Automatic prompt optimization is a promising approach for adapting large language models (LLMs) to downstream tasks, yet existing methods typically search for a specific prompt specialized to a fixed task. This paradigm limits generalization across heterogeneous queries and prevents models from accumulating reusable prompting knowledge over time. In this paper, we propose MemAPO, a memory-driven framework that reconceptualizes prompt optimization as generalizable and self-evolving experience accumulation. MemAPO maintains a dual-memory mechanism that distills successful reasoning trajectories into reusable strategy templates while organizing incorrect generations into structured error patterns that capture recurrent failure modes. Given a new prompt, the framework retrieves both relevant strategies and failure patterns to compose prompts that promote effective reasoning while discouraging known mistakes. Through iterative self-reflection and memory editing, MemAPO continuously updates its memory, enabling prompt optimization to improve over time rather than restarting from scratch for each task. Experiments on diverse benchmarks show that MemAPO consistently outperforms representative prompt optimization baselines while substantially reducing optimization cost.

### [76] [CatRAG: Functor-Guided Structural Debiasing with Retrieval Augmentation for Fair LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21524)
*Ravi Ranjan,Utkarsh Grover,Mayur Akewar,Xiaomin Lin,Agoritsa Polyzou*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are being used in high-stakes applications but exhibit strong demographic, gender, and geographic biases that harm fairness and trust. Existing debiasing methods typically operate at only one point in the model pipeline and thus fail to fully mitigate bias or remain robust under distribution shifts, often leading to brittle trade-offs between fairness and utility. The paper aims to design a principled, more complete, and robust debiasing framework.

Method: The paper introduces CatRAG Debiasing, which has two main components: (1) a functor-based module grounded in category theory that defines a structure-preserving projection in the embedding space to suppress directions associated with bias while preserving task-relevant semantics; and (2) a structural debiasing mechanism integrated into Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG), which uses retrieved, debiased or bias-aware information to further steer the model’s generation toward less biased outputs. The debiasing is thus applied both at the representation level and at the retrieval/generation stage, forming a dual-pronged pipeline.

Result: On the Bias Benchmark for Question Answering (BBQ) evaluated over three open-source LLMs (Meta Llama-3, OpenAI GPT-OSS, and Google Gemma-3), CatRAG Debiasing achieves state-of-the-art performance. It improves accuracy by up to 40 percentage points over the base models and by more than 10 points compared to prior debiasing techniques, while driving measured bias scores from around 60% for base models to nearly zero across categories such as gender, nationality, race, and intersectional subgroups.

Conclusion: CatRAG Debiasing provides a principled and effective debiasing framework that simultaneously targets embedding-level and RAG-level bias, achieving both significantly reduced bias and improved task performance on a standard benchmark. The results suggest that category-theoretic projections combined with structural RAG interventions can offer more robust and comprehensive debiasing than previous single-stage approaches.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are deployed in high-stakes settings but can show demographic, gender, and geographic biases that undermine fairness and trust. Prior debiasing methods, including embedding-space projections, prompt-based steering, and causal interventions, often act at a single stage of the pipeline, resulting in incomplete mitigation and brittle utility trade-offs under distribution shifts. We propose CatRAG Debiasing, a dual-pronged framework that integrates functor with Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) guided structural debiasing. The functor component leverages category-theoretic structure to induce a principled, structure-preserving projection that suppresses bias-associated directions in the embedding space while retaining task-relevant semantics. On the Bias Benchmark for Question Answering (BBQ) across three open-source LLMs (Meta Llama-3, OpenAI GPT-OSS, and Google Gemma-3), CatRAG achieves state-of-the-art results, improving accuracy by up to 40% over the corresponding base models and by more than 10% over prior debiasing methods, while reducing bias scores to near zero (from 60% for the base models) across gender, nationality, race, and intersectional subgroups.

### [79] [A Comparative Analysis of LLM Memorization at Statistical and Internal Levels: Cross-Model Commonalities and Model-Specific Signatures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21658)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work on LLM memorization is fragmented because researchers typically analyze only one model series whose pre-training data is partially known. This makes it hard to know which behaviors are universal properties of LLMs and which are artifacts of a particular model or dataset. The authors want a cross-family, comparative understanding of memorization that can reveal common laws, mechanisms, and differences across models, providing a more fundamental picture of how LLMs store and retrieve training data.

Method: They gather several independent open LLM families (Pythia, OpenLLaMa, StarCoder, OLMo1/2/3) and run a common battery of analyses. At the statistical level, they measure memorization rates vs. model size, compressibility of memorized sequences, and how memorized content distributes by frequency and domain. At the internal level, they inject perturbations into inputs to test robustness, decode representations from middle layers to track the formation of outputs, and ablate attention heads to see which heads are crucial for reproducing memorized sequences and how these heads are distributed across architectures and families.

Result: (1) Memorization rate follows a roughly log-linear scaling with model size across families. (2) Memorized sequences tend to be more compressible than raw text and share a similar frequency and domain distribution pattern across models, though each family retains some idiosyncratic traits. (3) LLMs can correct or ignore certain injected perturbations on non-memorized content, but memorized sequences are more fragile, showing distinct sensitivity. (4) Intermediate-layer decoding and head ablations expose a broadly shared decoding pipeline and a set of attention heads that are consistently important for memorization; yet, the layer-wise and structural distribution of these crucial heads differs between families.

Conclusion: Memorization in LLMs exhibits both universal regularities—such as scaling behavior, compressibility properties, and common internal mechanisms—and model-family-specific signatures, especially in how important attention heads for memorization are organized. By aligning and extending observations across multiple model series, the work offers a more unified framework for understanding memorization in LLMs and lays groundwork for future theory and control of memorization behavior.

Abstract: Memorization is a fundamental component of intelligence for both humans and LLMs. However, while LLM performance scales rapidly, our understanding of memorization lags. Due to limited access to the pre-training data of LLMs, most previous studies focus on a single model series, leading to isolated observations among series, making it unclear which findings are general or specific. In this study, we collect multiple model series (Pythia, OpenLLaMa, StarCoder, OLMo1/2/3) and analyze their shared or unique memorization behavior at both the statistical and internal levels, connecting individual observations while showing new findings. At the statistical level, we reveal that the memorization rate scales log-linearly with model size, and memorized sequences can be further compressed. Further analysis demonstrated a shared frequency and domain distribution pattern for memorized sequences. However, different models also show individual features under the above observations. At the internal level, we find that LLMs can remove certain injected perturbations, while memorized sequences are more sensitive. By decoding middle layers and attention head ablation, we revealed the general decoding process and shared important heads for memorization. However, the distribution of those important heads differs between families, showing a unique family-level feature. Through bridging various experiments and revealing new findings, this study paves the way for a universal and fundamental understanding of memorization in LLM.

### [80] [TAMTRL: Teacher-Aligned Reward Reshaping for Multi-Turn Reinforcement Learning in Long-Context Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21663)
*Li Wang,Yandong Wang,Xin Yu,Kui Zhang,Tianhao Peng,Wenjun Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose TAMTRL, a reinforcement-learning method that reshapes rewards for each turn of multi-turn long-context processing, improving how LLMs update memory over long documents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs struggle with long documents that exceed their context window because they must process text in chunks across multiple turns, updating an internal memory. Training typically only supervises the final answer, not intermediate memory updates, causing a temporal credit assignment problem and inefficient learning. Existing evaluation methods for intermediate steps are noisy and computationally expensive, motivating a more efficient, fine-grained, and reliable way to assign credit at every turn.

Method: TAMTRL (Teacher-Aligned Reward Reshaping for Multi-Turn Reinforcement Learning) uses relevant documents as teacher signals. For each turn in multi-turn processing, it aligns the current model input with these teacher documents and computes normalized probabilities that serve as self-supervised rewards. These per-turn rewards reshape the RL objective, providing detailed credit for each memory update step, rather than only using the final outcome. This reduces reliance on external judges or process reward models, aiming for lower overhead and less noise.

Result: Across seven long-context benchmarks and for multiple LLM scales, TAMTRL yields consistent performance gains over strong baseline methods for long-context processing, indicating better memory updating and task performance.

Conclusion: By reshaping rewards using teacher-aligned, self-supervised signals at each turn, TAMTRL effectively addresses temporal credit assignment in multi-turn memory training for long-context tasks. This leads to more accurate and efficient long-context processing than existing baselines, showing that fine-grained per-turn supervision can significantly improve LLMs’ ability to handle inputs beyond their context window.

Abstract: The rapid progress of large language models (LLMs) has led to remarkable performance gains across a wide range of tasks. However, when handling long documents that exceed the model's context window limit, the entire context cannot be processed in a single pass, making chunk-wise processing necessary. This requires multiple turns to read different chunks and update memory. However, supervision is typically provided only by the final outcome, which makes it difficult to evaluate the quality of memory updates at each turn in the multi-turn training setting. This introduces a temporal credit assignment challenge. Existing approaches, such as LLM-as-a-judge or process reward models, incur substantial computational overhead and suffer from estimation noise. To better address the credit assignment problem in multi-turn memory training, we propose Teacher-Aligned Reward Reshaping for Multi-Turn Reinforcement Learning (TAMTRL). TAMTRL leverages relevant documents as teacher signals by aligning them with each turn of model input and assigns rewards through normalized probabilities in a self-supervised manner. This provides fine-grained learning signals for each memory update and improves long-context processing. Experiments with multiple models of varying scales across seven long-context benchmarks show that TAMTRL consistently outperforms strong baselines, demonstrating its effectiveness. Our code is available at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/TAMTRL-F1F8.

</details>


### [81] [Optimizing Multi-Agent Weather Captioning via Text Gradient Descent: A Training-Free Approach with Consensus-Aware Gradient Fusion](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21673)
*Shixu Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents WeatherTGD, a training-free multi-agent LLM framework that generates interpretable, domain-specific weather captions from time series data via an iterative Text Gradient Descent process.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Numerical weather forecasts and current LLM-based methods either lack human-understandable explanations or produce shallow, generic descriptions that ignore meteorological domain knowledge. There is a need for a system that can produce rich, interpretable, and domain-faithful natural language captions directly from weather time series.

Method: WeatherTGD uses three specialized LLM agents—a Statistical Analyst, a Physics Interpreter, and a Meteorology Expert—to produce domain-specific textual "gradients" (feedback) from weather time series. These textual gradients are combined using a Consensus-Aware Gradient Fusion mechanism that preserves both shared and unique insights. The fused feedback is then used in an iterative refinement loop, analogous to gradient descent, to progressively improve a weather caption without additional model training.

Result: On real-world meteorological datasets, WeatherTGD yields substantially better captions according to both LLM-based automatic metrics and evaluations by human meteorology experts, and it outperforms other multi-agent LLM baselines while keeping computation efficient via parallel agent execution.

Conclusion: A training-free, multi-agent Text Gradient Descent framework can effectively generate high-quality, interpretable, and domain-informed natural language weather captions from time series data, bridging a key gap between numerical forecasting and human-understandable meteorological communication.

Abstract: Generating interpretable natural language captions from weather time series data remains a significant challenge at the intersection of meteorological science and natural language processing. While recent advances in Large Language Models (LLMs) have demonstrated remarkable capabilities in time series forecasting and analysis, existing approaches either produce numerical predictions without human-accessible explanations or generate generic descriptions lacking domain-specific depth. We introduce WeatherTGD, a training-free multi-agent framework that reinterprets collaborative caption refinement through the lens of Text Gradient Descent (TGD). Our system deploys three specialized LLM agents including a Statistical Analyst, a Physics Interpreter, and a Meteorology Expert that generate domain-specific textual gradients from weather time series observations. These gradients are aggregated through a novel Consensus-Aware Gradient Fusion mechanism that extracts common signals while preserving unique domain perspectives. The fused gradients then guide an iterative refinement process analogous to gradient descent, where each LLM-generated feedback signal updates the caption toward an optimal solution. Experiments on real-world meteorological datasets demonstrate that WeatherTGD achieves significant improvements in both LLM-based evaluation and human expert evaluation, substantially outperforming existing multi-agent baselines while maintaining computational efficiency through parallel agent execution.

### [82] [Probing How Scalable Table Data Enhances General Long-Context Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21719)

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that synthetic structured table data with periodic dependencies is highly effective for improving LLM long-context reasoning, and introduces a scalable RL-based pipeline (TableLong) to generate such data, achieving strong gains on long-context and even out-of-domain benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context reasoning is increasingly important for LLMs, but we lack understanding of what training data best develops this ability and why. Existing work often uses unstructured or ad-hoc long-context data without principled analysis. The authors aim to identify data types that are intrinsically suited to long-context reasoning, understand their properties mathematically, and exploit them to systematically improve LLM performance on long sequences.

Method: 1) Empirically observe that structured table data with periodic patterns seems promising for long-context tasks. 2) Formally analyze dependency structures in tabular data using mutual information, showing that such data contains periodic non-vanishing dependencies over long ranges. 3) Run scaling experiments to probe how this data type trains and improves LLM long-context abilities, and analyze underlying mechanisms. 4) Design TableLong, a simple, scalable pipeline that synthesizes large amounts of high-quality, diverse, and verifiable table data and uses reinforcement learning to train LLMs on it for long-context reasoning. 5) Evaluate the resulting models on multiple long-context and out-of-domain benchmarks.

Result: Models trained with the proposed TableLong pipeline demonstrate substantial improvements on multiple long-context reasoning benchmarks, with an average gain of about 8.24%. Additionally, they show robust gains on out-of-domain benchmarks by roughly 8.06% on average, indicating that the benefits of table-based long-context training generalize beyond the synthetic domain. The analysis confirms the presence and utility of periodic, non-decaying dependencies in table data for teaching long-range reasoning.

Conclusion: Structured table data with periodic dependency structures is a particularly effective and principled training signal for long-context reasoning in LLMs. By mathematically characterizing these dependencies and using them to design a scalable RL-based data synthesis and training pipeline (TableLong), the authors significantly boost long-context capabilities and even out-of-domain performance. Their findings suggest practical guidance for constructing post-training datasets that target long-context reasoning in a more systematic and theoretically grounded way.

Abstract: As real-world tasks grow increasingly complex, long-context reasoning has become a core capability for Large Language Models (LLMs). However, few studies explore which data types are effective for long-context reasoning and why. We find that structured table data with periodic structures shows strong potential for long-context reasoning. Motivated by this observation, we mathematically analyze tabular dependency structures using mutual information, revealing periodic non-vanishing dependencies in table data. Furthermore, we systematically analyze the capabilities of structured table data, conduct relevant scaling experiments, and validate its underlying mechanisms for enhancing long-context reasoning, yielding several meaningful insights. Leveraging these insights, we propose a simple yet scalable pipeline(TableLong) for synthesizing high-quality, diverse, and verifiable structured table data to boost long-context reasoning via RL. Extensive experimental results demonstrate that table data significantly enhances the long-context reasoning capability of LLMs across multiple long-context benchmarks (+8.24\% on average), and even improves performance on out-of-domain benchmarks (+8.06\% on average). We hope that our insights provide practical guidance for effective post-training data to enhance long-context reasoning in LLMs.

We formulate AER as an evidence-grounded multiple-choice benchmark that captures key challenges of real-world causal reasoning, including distributed evidence, indirect background factors, and semantically related but non-causal distractors. The shared task attracted 122 participants and received 518 submissions. This paper presents the task formulation, dataset construction pipeline, evaluation setup, and system results. ### [84] [Politics of Questions in News: A Mixed-Methods Study of Interrogative Stances as Markers of Voice and Power](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21823)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing research on interrogatives in news has focused on small, mostly English, broadcast interview data, while large-scale computational news studies usually ignore question forms and their functions. There is a gap in understanding how questions operate pragmatically and sociologically across large corpora of written digital news, especially in other languages. This paper aims to fill that gap for French-language news and to integrate computational, pragmatic, and sociological approaches.

Method: The authors compile a corpus of over one million French-language digital news articles from January 2023 to June 2024. They automatically detect interrogative stances, approximate functional types of questions (e.g., issue-introducing, information-seeking, echo, leading, tag), and algorithmically locate answer-like textual spans when they occur, distinguishing between journalist narrative voice and quoted speech. They link these quantitative measures to a smaller, qualitatively annotated subcorpus informed by semantic and pragmatic theories of questions, using a mixed-methods framework.

Result: Interrogatives are relatively rare but exhibit systematic patterns: most are used to introduce or organize issues; smaller proportions are information-seeking or echo-like; explicitly leading and tag questions are uncommon. Density and functional mix vary by outlet and topic. Their heuristic indicates that the vast majority of questions are followed by an answer-like span in the same article, typically voiced by the journalist rather than sources. Interrogative contexts show high concentrations of named persons, organizations, and locations, while publics and broad social groups are mentioned less, revealing a strong personalization of interrogative discourse.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that interrogative stance, uptake, and voice can be reliably operationalized for corpus-scale analysis. It concludes that questions in French digital news primarily serve to frame and structure issues, are usually resolved within the same article, and tend to foreground prominent actors and places rather than diffuse publics. The authors argue that combining computational techniques with pragmatic and sociological theory provides a powerful way to explain how questioning practices shape contemporary news discourse and its patterns of personalization.

Abstract: Interrogatives in news discourse have been examined in linguistics and conversation analysis, but mostly in broadcast interviews and relatively small, often English-language corpora, while large-scale computational studies of news rarely distinguish interrogatives from declaratives or differentiate their functions. This paper brings these strands together through a mixed-methods study of the "Politics of Questions" in contemporary French-language digital news. Using over one million articles published between January 2023 and June 2024, we automatically detect interrogative stances, approximate their functional types, and locate textual answers when present, linking these quantitative measures to a qualitatively annotated subcorpus grounded in semantic and pragmatic theories of questions. Interrogatives are sparse but systematically patterned: they mainly introduce or organize issues, with most remaining cases being information-seeking or echo-like, while explicitly leading or tag questions are rare. Although their density and mix vary across outlets and topics, our heuristic suggests that questions are overwhelmingly taken up within the same article and usually linked to a subsequent answer-like span, most often in the journalist's narrative voice and less often through quoted speech. Interrogative contexts are densely populated with named individuals, organizations, and places, whereas publics and broad social groups are mentioned much less frequently, suggesting that interrogative discourse tends to foreground already prominent actors and places and thus exhibits strong personalization. We show how interrogative stance, textual uptake, and voice can be operationalized at corpus scale, and argue that combining computational methods with pragmatic and sociological perspectives can help account for how questioning practices structure contemporary news discourse.

### [85] [Instruction Set and Language for Symbolic Regression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21836)
*Ezequiel Lopez-Rubio,Mario Pascual-Gonzalez*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces IsalSR, a new representation framework that canonically encodes symbolic regression expressions to remove structural redundancy in the search space.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Symbolic regression search spaces suffer from structural redundancy because many different node-numbering schemes represent the same expression DAG, wasting fitness evaluations and hindering search efficiency. The paper aims to eliminate this redundancy by collapsing equivalent representations.

Method: The authors design IsalSR, which represents expression DAGs as strings over a compact two-tier alphabet, and define an algorithm to compute a pruned canonical string that is a labeled-DAG isomorphism invariant, so all equivalent graphs share the same encoding.

Result: Using the canonical string representation, all structurally equivalent expression DAGs map to a single point in the search space, removing redundancy due to node-numbering variations and reducing wasted evaluations while preserving all unique model structures.

Conclusion: IsalSR provides a canonical, redundancy-free representation for symbolic regression expressions that collapses structurally equivalent DAGs into one form, potentially improving search efficiency and diversity management in SR algorithms.

Abstract: A fundamental but largely unaddressed obstacle in Symbolic regression (SR) is structural redundancy: every expression DAG with admits many distinct node-numbering schemes that all encode the same expression, each occupying a separate point in the search space and consuming fitness evaluations without adding diversity. We present IsalSR (Instruction Set and Language for Symbolic Regression), a representation framework that encodes expression DAGs as strings over a compact two-tier alphabet and computes a pruned canonical string -- a complete labeled-DAG isomorphism invariant -- that collapses all the equivalent representations into a single canonical form.

</details>


### [86] [Select, Label, Evaluate: Active Testing in NLP](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21840)
*Antonio Purificato,Maria Sofia Bucarelli,Andrea Bacciu,Amin Mantrach,Fabrizio Silvestri*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to greatly reduce human test-set annotation in NLP while still accurately estimating model performance, using an Active Testing framework.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Human annotation for test sets in NLP is costly and slow, yet high-quality labels are essential for trustworthy model evaluation. Traditional practice labels every test example, which does not scale as datasets and models grow. There is a need for principled methods that select only the most informative test samples so that we can estimate model performance reliably with much less annotation effort.

Method: The authors formalize the Active Testing problem in NLP: given a labeling budget, select a subset of test instances whose labels will let us best estimate model performance. They benchmark multiple existing Active Testing / sample selection strategies across 18 datasets, 4 embedding strategies, and 4 NLP task types. They also design an adaptive stopping criterion that automatically decides when enough samples have been labeled, instead of requiring a fixed budget beforehand.

Result: Across the benchmark, using Active Testing can reduce the amount of annotated test data by up to 95% while keeping the estimated performance within about 1% of what would be obtained with a fully labeled test set. However, the effectiveness of particular selection methods varies with dataset characteristics and task type; no single strategy works best everywhere.

Conclusion: Active Testing can dramatically cut test annotation costs in NLP without sacrificing evaluation reliability, but method choice should be data- and task-dependent. The proposed adaptive stopping criterion further increases practicality by removing the need to predefine a labeling budget and letting the system automatically decide when additional annotations no longer improve performance estimates meaningfully.

Abstract: Human annotation cost and time remain significant bottlenecks in Natural Language Processing (NLP), with test data annotation being particularly expensive due to the stringent requirement for low-error and high-quality labels necessary for reliable model evaluation. Traditional approaches require annotating entire test sets, leading to substantial resource requirements. Active Testing is a framework that selects the most informative test samples for annotation. Given a labeling budget, it aims to choose the subset that best estimates model performance while minimizing cost and human effort. In this work, we formalize Active Testing in NLP and we conduct an extensive benchmarking of existing approaches across 18 datasets and 4 embedding strategies spanning 4 different NLP tasks. The experiments show annotation reductions of up to 95%, with performance estimation accuracy difference from the full test set within 1%. Our analysis reveals variations in method effectiveness across different data characteristics and task types, with no single approach emerging as universally superior. Lastly, to address the limitation of requiring a predefined annotation budget in existing sample selection strategies, we introduce an adaptive stopping criterion that automatically determines the optimal number of samples.

### [87] [Riding Brainwaves in LLM Space: Understanding Activation Patterns Using Individual Neural Signatures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21847)
*Ajan Subramanian,Sumukh Bettadapura,Rohan Sathish*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that frozen large language models (LLMs) contain person-specific neural representations that can predict an individual’s EEG responses during reading, and these representations are stable, distinct from shared population signals, and concentrated in deeper model layers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As consumer-grade EEG becomes common in everyday devices, there is a need to understand whether and how large language models can interface with and adapt to individual users’ neural responses. Specifically, the authors want to know if LLM internal representations already encode directions that correspond to an individual person’s EEG patterns, which would enable personalization without retraining the entire model.

Method: Using the ZuCo corpus with word-level EEG recordings from 30 participants reading naturalistic sentences, the authors feed the text into a frozen Qwen 2.5 7B LLM and extract hidden states. They then train simple linear probes (separate for each individual) from these hidden states to predict that individual’s EEG power features (e.g., high-gamma) at the word level. They compare these person-specific probes against a single population-level probe trained across participants, use negative controls (fixation count), analyze temporal stability via split-half reliability, test cross-person transfer, examine the effect of removing population components, inspect layer-wise localization of signal, and replicate with a different architecture (LLaMA 3.1 8B) while controlling for word-level confounds.

Result: Person-specific linear probes substantially outperform a single population probe on all EEG features; for example, for high-gamma power, the person-specific probe reaches a correlation of rho = 0.183, roughly nine times the population probe’s rho = 0.020 (p < 10^-4). The negative control (fixation count) shows no such person-specific advantage, suggesting the effect is not due to trivial lexical properties. The person-specific neural directions are highly temporally stable (split-half cosine = 0.824), do not transfer well between individuals (self-prediction rho = 0.369 vs other-person prediction rho = 0.143, p < 10^-19), and retain predictive power even after removing the shared population signal. The strongest person-specific signal resides in deeper layers, peaking around layer 24 of 28. These findings are consistent when tested on another LLM architecture, LLaMA 3.1 8B, and hold after controlling for word-level confounds.

Conclusion: Frozen LLMs naturally contain robust, temporally stable, person-specific ‘neural directions’ in their deep representations that align with individual EEG responses during reading. These directions are distinct from shared population signals, do not trivially reflect lexical features, and generalize across different LLM architectures. This provides a geometric and practical foundation for EEG-driven personalization and suggests that adapting lightweight probes on top of frozen LLMs may be sufficient to capture individual neural signatures for brain–computer interaction applications.

Abstract: Consumer-grade EEG is entering everyday devices, from earbuds to headbands, raising the question of whether language models can be adapted to individual neural responses. We test this by asking whether frozen LLM representations encode person-specific EEG signals, directions in activation space that predict one person's brain activity but not another's. Using word-level EEG from 30 participants reading naturalistic sentences (ZuCo corpus), we train a separate linear probe for each person, mapping hidden states from a frozen Qwen 2.5 7B to that individual's EEG power. Person-specific probes outperform a single population probe on every EEG feature tested; for high-gamma power, the person-specific probe achieves rho = 0.183, a ninefold improvement over the population probe (rho = 0.020, p < 10^-4). A negative control, fixation count, shows no person-specific advantage (p = 0.360); fixation count reflects word length and frequency rather than individual cognition. The individual directions are temporally stable (split-half cosine = 0.824), non-transferable across people (self rho = 0.369 vs. other rho = 0.143, p < 10^-19), and distinct from the shared population signal: person-specific probes retain predictive power after the population component is removed. The person-specific signal concentrates in the model's deep layers, rising consistently with depth and peaking at Layer 24 of 28. The results are consistent across architectures (LLaMA 3.1 8B) and survive word-level confound controls. Frozen language models contain stable, person-specific neural directions in their deep layers, providing a geometric foundation for EEG-driven personalization.

### [90] [Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning for Medical Text Summarization: A Comparative Study of Lora, Prompt Tuning, and Full Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21970)
*Ulugbek Shernazarov,Rostislav Svitsov,Bin Shi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates efficient ways to adapt large language models for medical text summarization and finds that LoRA outperforms full fine-tuning while updating less than 1% of parameters.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full fine-tuning of large language models for specialized domains like medical summarization is computationally expensive and resource-intensive. There is a need to determine whether parameter-efficient fine-tuning methods can match or surpass full fine-tuning performance while reducing computational and storage costs, especially for practical and clinical applications where resources are limited.

Method: The authors compare three adaptation strategies—Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA), Prompt Tuning, and standard Full Fine-Tuning—on the Flan-T5 model family using the PubMed medical summarization dataset. They run experiments with multiple random seeds to ensure statistical reliability, measure summarization quality mainly with ROUGE-1, and perform sensitivity analyses on LoRA rank and the number of prompt tokens to understand how these hyperparameters affect performance and efficiency.

Result: On Flan-T5-Large, LoRA with only 0.6% of the parameters trainable achieves 43.52 ± 0.18 ROUGE-1, outperforming full fine-tuning which achieves 40.67 ± 0.21 ROUGE-1 while training all parameters. LoRA consistently outperforms full fine-tuning across seeds, while also being more parameter-efficient. Sensitivity studies show the influence of LoRA rank and prompt token count but the core result is that low-rank adaptation is both more effective and cheaper than full fine-tuning in this setting.

Conclusion: The study concludes that Low-Rank Adaptation provides a highly effective and computationally efficient way to adapt large language models for domain-specific tasks like medical summarization. The low-rank constraint seems to introduce a useful form of regularization that can yield better generalization than full fine-tuning, challenging the assumption that updating all model parameters is necessary for top performance. This supports broader adoption of PEFT methods such as LoRA in resource-constrained, domain-specific NLP applications.

Abstract: Fine-tuning large language models for domain-specific tasks such as medical text summarization demands substantial computational resources. Parameter-efficient fine-tuning (PEFT) methods offer promising alternatives by updating only a small fraction of parameters. This paper compares three adaptation approaches-Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA), Prompt Tuning, and Full Fine-Tuning-across the Flan-T5 model family on the PubMed medical summarization dataset. Through experiments with multiple random seeds, we demonstrate that LoRA consistently outperforms full fine-tuning, achieving 43.52 +/- 0.18 ROUGE-1 on Flan-T5-Large with only 0.6% trainable parameters compared to 40.67 +/- 0.21 for full fine-tuning. Sensitivity analyses examine the impact of LoRA rank and prompt token count. Our findings suggest the low-rank constraint provides beneficial regularization, challenging assumptions about the necessity of full parameter updates. Code is available at https://github.com/eracoding/llm-medical-summarization

### [91] [Retrieving Climate Change Disinformation by Narrative](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.22015)

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors reframe narrative detection as a retrieval problem: given a narrative’s core message (a query), rank corpus documents by alignment. The system is evaluated using MAP and compared against standard retrieval methods like BM25. The SpecFi framework, which uses generated hypothetical documents conditioned on community-based summaries, improves retrieval performance and robustness, especially for difficult, high-variance narratives. Moreover, unsupervised graph-based community detection can recover narrative structures similar to expert taxonomies, suggesting a scalable path to surfacing narrative structures from unlabeled text.

Abstract: Detecting climate disinformation narratives typically relies on fixed taxonomies, which do not accommodate emerging narratives. Thus, we re-frame narrative detection as a retrieval task: given a narrative's core message as a query, rank texts from a corpus by alignment with that narrative. This formulation requires no predefined label set and can accommodate emerging narratives. We repurpose three climate disinformation datasets (CARDS, Climate Obstruction, climate change subset of PolyNarrative) for retrieval evaluation and propose SpecFi, a framework that generates hypothetical documents to bridge the gap between abstract narrative descriptions and their concrete textual instantiations. SpecFi uses community summaries from graph-based community detection as few-shot examples for generation, achieving a MAP of 0.505 on CARDS without access to narrative labels. We further introduce narrative variance, an embedding-based difficulty metric, and show via partial correlation analysis that standard retrieval degrades on high-variance narratives (BM25 loses 63.4% of MAP), while SpecFi-CS remains robust (32.7% loss). Our analysis also reveals that unsupervised community summaries converge on descriptions close to expert-crafted taxonomies, suggesting that graph-based methods can surface narrative structure from unlabeled text.

*Stella Eva Tsiapali,Cong-Thanh Do,Kate Knill*

TL;DR: They study and improve a state-of-the-art distillation method (DSKD-CMA) for compressing LLMs with different tokenizers, and propose an adversarial variant that slightly but consistently boosts generation quality, especially out-of-distribution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are powerful but expensive to deploy, so we need better knowledge distillation techniques to train smaller models without sacrificing much performance. Existing SOTA cross-tokenizer KD method DSKD-CMA works well but its attention behavior and limitations are poorly understood, especially the effects of mismatched tokenizations and attention distributions between teacher and student.

Method: 1) Empirically analyze DSKD-CMA’s cross-model attention with manual token alignment and heatmap visualization to understand how teacher and student attend to each other’s representations. 2) Identify distribution mismatches in keys and queries arising from distinct tokenizers. 3) Propose DSKD-CMA-GA, which introduces a generative adversarial learning component to align the distributions of keys and queries produced by teacher and student models. 4) Evaluate the method via ROUGE-L on text generation tasks, including out-of-distribution settings, and compare to baseline KD setups (cross- vs same-tokenizer).

Result: Their analysis exposes both beneficial and problematic patterns in DSKD-CMA’s attention when teacher and student have different tokenizers, specifically highlighting mismatches in key/query distributions. The proposed DSKD-CMA-GA reduces these mismatches and yields modest but consistent improvements in ROUGE-L scores for text generation, with an average gain of +0.37, and shows particular advantages on out-of-distribution test data. This narrows the performance gap between cross-tokenizer KD and same-tokenizer KD baselines.

Conclusion: Understanding attention behavior in cross-tokenizer KD is important for further improving LLM compression. By explicitly addressing key/query distribution mismatches through a generative adversarial framework, DSKD-CMA-GA slightly but reliably improves distilled student performance and brings cross-tokenizer KD closer to same-tokenizer KD quality, especially in more challenging out-of-distribution scenarios.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve state-of-the-art (SOTA) performance across language tasks, but are costly to deploy due to their size and resource demands. Knowledge Distillation (KD) addresses this by training smaller Student models to mimic larger Teacher models, improving efficiency without significant performance loss. Dual-Space Knowledge Distillation with Cross-Model Attention (DSKD-CMA) has emerged as a SOTA method for KD between LLMs with distinct tokenizers, yet its internal workings remain largely opaque. In this work, we systematically analyse the attention mechanism of DSKD-CMA through manual token alignment probing and heatmap visualisations, revealing both strengths and limitations. Building on this, we introduce a novel method, DSKD-CMA-GA, based on Generative Adversarial (GA) learning, to address the mismatched distributions between the keys and queries computed from distinct models. Experiments show modest but consistent ROUGE-L gains in text generation quality, particularly on out-of-distribution data (+0.37 on average), narrowing the gap between cross- and same-tokenizer KD.

### [93] [Autoregressive vs. Masked Diffusion Language Models: A Controlled Comparison](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.22075)
*Caio Vicentino*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically compares autoregressive and masked-diffusion language models under strictly matched training conditions, finding similar throughput, different convergence/overfitting patterns, and a trade-off between fluency and structural diversity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is growing interest in diffusion-style language models, but most comparisons with traditional autoregressive models confound multiple variables (data, scale, optimization, hardware). The authors want a clean, controlled comparison that isolates only the generation paradigm, to understand how AR and masked diffusion differ in efficiency, training dynamics, and output properties such as diversity and fluency.

Method: Train an autoregressive language model and a masked diffusion language model on exactly the same dataset (50M TinyStories tokens), with strictly matched compute budget (20,000 steps, batch size 32, sequence length 512) and hardware (NVIDIA H100 80GB). Measure and compare: (1) training throughput and wall-clock time; (2) convergence and overfitting behavior over training steps; and (3) quantitative diversity vs. fluency metrics on 1,000 generated samples, including opening-phrase uniqueness, Distinct-n, and Self-BLEU.

Result: (1) Both paradigms achieve similar throughput (~50K tokens/second), with masked diffusion only 4.7% slower in wall-clock time. (2) The autoregressive model converges faster but starts overfitting around step 14,000, whereas the masked diffusion model converges more slowly and continues improving through step 20,000. (3) AR generations are more fluent but highly repetitive (99.8% of samples share the same first word), while the masked diffusion model yields structurally more diverse text (93.4% unique 5-word openings, higher Distinct-n, lower Self-BLEU) at the expense of some grammatical inconsistencies.

Conclusion: Autoregressive and masked diffusion language models have comparable raw training efficiency, but differ in training dynamics and generation characteristics. AR models are compute-efficient and quickly reach high fluency but tend to overfit and produce repetitive text, whereas masked diffusion models train more slowly yet keep improving and produce more structurally diverse, though sometimes less grammatical, outputs. These differences imply distinct compute-optimal regimes and suggest a fundamental diversity-fluency trade-off between the paradigms. The released code and checkpoints enable further, reproducible investigation.

Abstract: We present a controlled empirical comparison between autoregressive (AR) and masked diffusion (MDLM) language models. Both models are trained on identical data (50M tokens from TinyStories), identical compute budget (20,000 steps, batch size 32, sequence length 512), and identical hardware (NVIDIA H100 80GB), isolating the generation paradigm as the sole variable. We report three findings. First, both paradigms achieve comparable training throughput (~50K tokens/second), with MDLM requiring only 4.7% more wall-clock time. Second, AR converges faster and begins overfitting by step 14,000, while MDLM converges more slowly and is still improving at step 20,000, suggesting different compute-optimal training regimes. Third, quantitative diversity analysis over 1,000 generated samples reveals a structural diversity-fluency trade-off: AR produces fluent but repetitive outputs (99.8% begin with the same word), while MDLM generates more diverse narratives (93.4% unique 5-word openings, higher Distinct-n, lower Self-BLEU), at the cost of occasional grammatical inconsistencies. All code, trained checkpoints, and data pipelines are released for reproducibility.

</details>


### [94] [Multiperspectivity as a Resource for Narrative Similarity Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.22103)
*Max Upravitelev,Veronika Solopova,Jing Yang,Charlott Jakob,Premtim Sahitaj,Ariana Sahitaj,Vera Schmitt*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes using multiple LLM personas to model inherently interpretive narrative similarity judgments, showing that an ensemble of diverse personas improves accuracy and highlights limitations of single-ground-truth evaluation frameworks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Narrative similarity is interpretive and subjective, so different readers can disagree while all being reasonable. Standard benchmarks assume a single ground truth, which clashes with this multiperspectivity and limits both model training and evaluation. The authors are motivated to design systems and evaluation schemes that acknowledge and leverage interpretive plurality instead of treating it as noise.

Method: The authors construct an ensemble of 31 distinct LLM personas, including expert practitioners grounded in explicit interpretive frameworks and more intuitive lay-style personas. Each persona independently predicts narrative similarity labels for pairs in the SemEval-2026 Task 4 dataset. The final system aggregates these predictions via majority voting. They systematically vary ensemble size to analyze accuracy trends and error correlations, and perform error analysis focusing on interpretive vocabularies, including gender-focused language.

Result: On SemEval-2026 Task 4, the persona ensemble reaches an accuracy of 0.705. Accuracy increases as more personas are added, in line with Condorcet Jury Theorem intuition despite weakened independence among members. Individual practitioner personas perform worse than lay personas but have less correlated errors, so they contribute disproportionately to ensemble gains. Error analysis shows a robust negative correlation between the use of gender-focused interpretive vocabulary and accuracy across persona types.

Conclusion: Modeling interpretive plurality via multiple LLM personas and aggregating their decisions yields better predictive performance on narrative similarity than relying on single, monolithic models. Diverse interpretive frameworks, even if weaker individually, can strengthen ensemble decisions due to error diversity. The observed tension between gender-focused interpretations and benchmark accuracy suggests that current single-ground-truth evaluation frameworks fail to capture all valid readings, underlining the need for pluralistic evaluation designs that accommodate multiple legitimate interpretations.

Abstract: Predicting narrative similarity can be understood as an inherently interpretive task: different, equally valid readings of the same text can produce divergent interpretations and thus different similarity judgments, posing a fundamental challenge for semantic evaluation benchmarks that encode a single ground truth. Rather than treating this multiperspectivity as a challenge to overcome, we propose to incorporate it in the decision making process of predictive systems. To explore this strategy, we created an ensemble of 31 LLM personas. These range from practitioners following interpretive frameworks to more intuitive, lay-style characters. Our experiments were conducted on the SemEval-2026 Task 4 dataset, where the system achieved an accuracy score of 0.705. Accuracy improves with ensemble size, consistent with Condorcet Jury Theorem-like dynamics under weakened independence. Practitioner personas perform worse individually but produce less correlated errors, yielding larger ensemble gains under majority voting. Our error analysis reveals a consistent negative association between gender-focused interpretive vocabulary and accuracy across all persona categories, suggesting either attention to dimensions not relevant for the benchmark or valid interpretations absent from the ground truth. This finding underscores the need for evaluation frameworks that account for interpretive plurality.

TL;DR: The paper proposes the “Semantic Ladder,” a framework for progressively turning natural language content into machine-readable semantic models while keeping everything linked and traceable.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design an architectural framework called the Semantic Ladder. The Semantic Ladder demonstrates mechanisms for semantic enrichment, structuring, and modelling that maintain links between representations at different levels and support integration of heterogeneous forms such as text, ontologies, and embeddings. It provides a blueprint for building semantic knowledge spaces incrementally and at scale.

Conclusion: The Semantic Ladder offers a principled way to gradually move from natural language to highly formal semantic representations while maintaining semantic continuity and traceability. This progressive approach reduces the burden of semantic parsing at data entry, enables incremental construction of rich semantic knowledge spaces, and facilitates scalable, interoperable, and AI-ready data and knowledge infrastructures that can integrate diverse representational forms.

Abstract: Semantic data and knowledge infrastructures must reconcile two fundamentally different forms of representation: natural language, in which most knowledge is created and communicated, and formal semantic models, which enable machine-actionable integration, interoperability, and reasoning. Bridging this gap remains a central challenge, particularly when full semantic formalization is required at the point of data entry. Here, we introduce the Semantic Ladder, an architectural framework that enables the progressive formalization of data and knowledge. Building on the concept of modular semantic units as identifiable carriers of meaning, the framework organizes representations across levels of increasing semantic explicitness, ranging from natural language text snippets to ontology-based and higher-order logical models. Transformations between levels support semantic enrichment, statement structuring, and logical modelling while preserving semantic continuity and traceability. This approach enables the incremental construction of semantic knowledge spaces, reduces the semantic parsing burden, and supports the integration of heterogeneous representations, including natural language, structured semantic models, and vector-based embeddings. The Semantic Ladder thereby provides a foundation for scalable, interoperable, and AI-ready data and knowledge infrastructures.

Conclusion: By augmenting limited document-level MT data with LLM-generated parallel documents and applying strong quality filtering, then using a two-stage fine-tuning regimen (sentence-level followed by document-level), LLMs can be better adapted for document-level machine translation, addressing data scarcity and reducing problematic generation behavior.

Abstract: In Machine Translation, Large Language Models (LLMs) have generally underperformed compared to conventional encoder-decoder systems and thus see limited adoption. However, LLMs excel at modeling contextual information, making them a natural fit for document-level translation tasks where coherence across sentences is crucial. Despite this potential, document-level MT with LLMs faces two key challenges: (1) the scarcity of large-scale, high-quality document-level parallel data; and (2) the propensity of LLMs to introduce hallucinations and omissions during generation. To address these challenges, we propose a two-stage fine-tuning strategy leveraging LLM-augmented document-level data. First, we augment data by converting summarization data into document-level parallel data using a LLM, and then filter it using multiple metrics, leveraging sacreBLEU, COMET, and LaBSE-based cosine similarity-to improve data quality. Finally, we employ a two-stage fine-tuning strategy: first fine-tuning on the abundant sentence-level MT resources, and then on the filtered document-level corpus.

### [97] [Gumbel Distillation for Parallel Text Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.22216)
*Chi Zhang,Xixi Hu,Bo Liu,Qiang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces Gumbel Distillation, a technique that improves the quality of parallel (non-autoregressive) language model decoding by better matching the token distribution of a strong autoregressive teacher using the Gumbel-Max trick.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autoregressive language models generate tokens sequentially, which is slow at inference time. Parallel (non-autoregressive) decoding methods are faster but usually produce lower-quality text because they struggle to capture the complex joint distribution over token sequences. There is a need for a method that allows fast parallel decoders to approach the quality of strong autoregressive models.

Method: Use the Gumbel-Max trick to define a deterministic mapping from samples in a latent Gumbel noise space to the output tokens generated by a strong autoregressive teacher model. This mapping is then distilled into a parallel decoder so that it learns to approximate the teacher’s induced token distribution. The technique is model-agnostic and can be applied to different parallel decoding architectures such as MDLM and BD3-LM.

Result: On the LM1B and OpenWebText benchmarks, applying Gumbel Distillation to parallel language models significantly improves their generation quality. Specifically, for MDLM on OpenWebText, the method yields a 30.0% improvement in MAUVE score and a 10.5% improvement in generative perplexity compared with a baseline MDLM trained without this technique.

Conclusion: Gumbel Distillation effectively narrows the performance gap between fast parallel decoders and high-quality autoregressive teachers by enabling non-AR models to better approximate the teacher’s token distribution. Because it is model-agnostic and improves multiple architectures, it represents a practical way to obtain higher-quality yet efficient parallel language models.

Abstract: The slow, sequential nature of autoregressive (AR) language models has driven the adoption of parallel decoding methods. However, these non-AR models often sacrifice generation quality as they struggle to model the complex joint distribution of token sequences. To narrow this performance gap, we introduce Gumbel Distillation, a novel distillation technique that enables parallel decoders to learn this distribution effectively. Our method leverages the Gumbel-Max trick to create a deterministic mapping from a latent Gumbel noise space to the output tokens of a high-performing AR teacher. As a model-agnostic technique, Gumbel Distillation seamlessly integrates with diverse parallel decoding architectures, including MDLM and BD3-LM. Experiments on LM1B and OpenWebText show that Gumbel Distillation substantially improves the generation quality of parallel language models, achieving a 30.0% improvement in MAUVE score and 10.5% in generative perplexity over MDLM trained on OpenWebText dataset. Code available at https://github.com/hxixixh/gumbel-distill.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This is done using centroid-based vector adaptation estimated from healthy-control speech in both languages: they compute centroids in the embedding space and apply a shift that maps source centroids toward target centroids. This transformation is then applied to source-language representations before training or inference. They test LS using oral diadochokinetic (DDK) recordings from Parkinson’s disease datasets in Czech, German, and Spanish, in cross-lingual (train on one language, test on another) and multilingual (train on combined languages) settings.

In multilingual settings, where multiple languages are used jointly, LS still brings consistent but smaller performance gains. Representation analysis (e.g., language classification from embeddings or clustering) shows that language identity becomes less salient after LS, indicating the language-dependent structure is reduced.

Conclusion: Representation-level language shift using simple centroid-based adaptation can effectively reduce language dependence of self-supervised speech embeddings, enabling better cross-lingual dysarthria detection when labeled data are limited. Even in multilingual training, LS yields modest but consistent improvements, and embedding analyses corroborate that LS removes language-specific variance while preserving clinically relevant information.

Abstract: The limited availability of dysarthric speech data makes cross-lingual detection an important but challenging problem. A key difficulty is that speech representations often encode language-dependent structure that can confound dysarthria detection. We propose a representation-level language shift (LS) that aligns source-language self-supervised speech representations with the target-language distribution using centroid-based vector adaptation estimated from healthy-control speech. We evaluate the approach on oral DDK recordings from Parkinson's disease speech datasets in Czech, German, and Spanish under both cross-lingual and multilingual settings. LS substantially improves sensitivity and F1 in cross-lingual settings, while yielding smaller but consistent gains in multilingual settings. Representation analysis further shows that LS reduces language identity in the embedding space, supporting the interpretation that LS removes language-dependent structure.

### [99] [MemDLM: Memory-Enhanced DLM Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.22241)
*Zehua Pei,Hui-Ling Zhen,Weizhe Lin,Sinno Jialin Pan,Yunhe Wang,Mingxuan Yuan,Bei Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MemDLM, a memory-augmented diffusion language model that aligns training with multi-step denoising-style inference and improves long-context and retrieval performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion Language Models have advantages like parallel decoding and flexible generation but suffer from a mismatch between how they are trained (single-step masked prediction) and how they are used (multi-step denoising). This mismatch slows convergence, limits performance, and particularly hurts long-context understanding and retrieval tasks. The authors aim to reduce this train-inference gap and address attention bottlenecks in long-context settings.

Method: They propose MemDLM, which incorporates a simulated denoising trajectory into training via bi-level optimization. In an inner loop, the model updates a set of fast weights that serve as a parametric memory, capturing sample-specific denoising trajectories. In an outer loop, the base model parameters are updated conditioned on this memory. This shifts part of the learning burden from token representations to fast-weight parameters. The same inner loop mechanism can be optionally activated at inference time as an adaptation step to the current context.

Result: MemDLM shows faster convergence and lower training loss compared to standard DLM training. When the inner-loop memory mechanism is used at inference, the model achieves improved performance on long-context understanding and on Needle-in-a-Haystack retrieval benchmarks, indicating reduced reliance on standard attention and better in-weight retrieval. The memory behaves like an emergent retrieval mechanism that mitigates token-level attention bottlenecks.

Conclusion: By embedding a denoising-like trajectory into training and leveraging fast-weight parametric memory, MemDLM narrows the train-inference mismatch in diffusion language models and enhances their performance, especially for long-context and challenging retrieval tasks. The parametric memory can be reused at inference as an adaptation and retrieval mechanism, making DLMs more effective and efficient in practice.

Abstract: Diffusion Language Models (DLMs) offer attractive advantages over Auto-Regressive (AR) models, such as full-attention parallel decoding and flexible generation. However, they suffer from a notable train-inference mismatch: DLMs are trained with a static, single-step masked prediction objective, but deployed through a multi-step progressive denoising trajectory. We propose MemDLM (Memory-Enhanced DLM), which narrows this gap by embedding a simulated denoising process into training via Bi-level Optimization. An inner loop updates a set of fast weights, forming a Parametric Memory that captures the local trajectory experience of each sample, while an outer loop updates the base model conditioned on this memory. By offloading memorization pressure from token representations to parameters, MemDLM yields faster convergence and lower training loss. Moreover, the inner loop can be re-enabled at inference time as an adaptation step, yielding additional gains on long-context understanding. We find that, when activated at inference time, this Parametric Memory acts as an emergent in-weight retrieval mechanism, helping MemDLM further reduce token-level attention bottlenecks on challenging Needle-in-a-Haystack retrieval tasks. Code: https://github.com/JarvisPei/MemDLM.
