  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large vision-language models still struggle with spatial reasoning tasks, and it is unclear how internal attention mechanisms support such reasoning. The authors aim to open this black box using mechanistic interpretability, to understand which components handle spatial cognition and how to improve it.

Method: They construct CogVSR, a dataset that breaks complex spatial reasoning questions into chain-of-thought style subquestions, each associated with a specific spatial or cognitive function. Using this dataset, they design a probing framework that analyzes attention heads in various VLM families to detect heads specialized in different spatial and cognitive roles, then perform interventions such as ablating or amplifying these heads and activating latent spatial heads.

Result: They find that functionally specialized attention heads are sparse across models and that heads specialized in spatial reasoning are particularly few compared to those for other cognitive functions. Intervention experiments show that ablating identified functional heads harms spatial reasoning performance, while emphasizing or activating them improves spatial understanding and task accuracy.

Conclusion: Attention heads with specific cognitive roles, especially for spatial reasoning, play a critical but sparse role in VLMs. By identifying, characterizing, and intervening on these heads, one can both better understand how models perform spatial reasoning and enhance their spatial capabilities, pointing toward interpretability-guided methods for improving multimodal reasoning.

Abstract: Despite remarkable advances in large Vision-Language Models (VLMs), spatial reasoning remains a persistent challenge. In this work, we investigate how attention heads within VLMs contribute to spatial reasoning by analyzing their functional roles through a mechanistic interpretability lens. We introduce CogVSR, a dataset that decomposes complex spatial reasoning questions into step-by-step subquestions designed to simulate human-like reasoning via a chain-of-thought paradigm, with each subquestion linked to specific cognitive functions such as spatial perception or relational reasoning. Building on CogVSR, we develop a probing framework to identify and characterize attention heads specialized for these functions. Our analysis across diverse VLM families reveals that these functional heads are universally sparse, vary in number and distribution across functions. Notably, spatially specialized heads are fewer than those for other cognitive functions, highlighting their scarcity. We propose methods to activate latent spatial heads, improving spatial understanding. Intervention experiments further demonstrate their critical role in spatial reasoning: removing functional heads leads to performance degradation, while emphasizing them enhances accuracy. This study provides new interpretability driven insights into how VLMs attend to space and paves the way for enhancing complex spatial reasoning in multimodal models.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Train 12 Random Forest models on concatenated molecular fingerprints (FCFP, ECFP, MACCS, atom pairs; 4,263 dimensions). Use rank-averaged ensembling across these RFs. Crucial hyperparameters include setting max_features to 0.20 rather than sqrt(d).

Tuning max_features from sqrt(d) to 0.20 yields about +0.008 ROC-AUC, and averaging GNN predictions across seeds removes GNN seed variance, reducing the final ensemble's standard deviation from 0.0008 to 0.0002.

Conclusion: Carefully optimized ensembles of classical Random Forests on diverse fingerprints, combined with variance-reduced GNN ensembles at small weight, can set new state of the art on molecular benchmarks. Hyperparameter choices (like max_features) and variance reduction by pre-averaging GNN seeds are as important as architecture complexity, and strong baselines without pretraining or external data can be highly competitive.

Abstract: Multi-RF Fusion achieves a test ROC-AUC of 0.8476 +/- 0.0002 on ogbg-molhiv (10 seeds), placing #1 on the OGB leaderboard ahead of HyperFusion (0.8475 +/- 0.0003). The core of the method is a rank-averaged ensemble of 12 Random Forest models trained on concatenated molecular fingerprints (FCFP, ECFP, MACCS, atom pairs -- 4,263 dimensions total), blended with deep-ensembled GNN predictions at 12% weight. Two findings drive the result: (1) setting max_features to 0.20 instead of the default sqrt(d) gives a +0.008 AUC gain on this scaffold split, and (2) averaging GNN predictions across 10 seeds before blending with the RF eliminates GNN seed variance entirely, dropping the final standard deviation from 0.0008 to 0.0002. No external data or pre-training is used.

*Jinming Yang,Xinyu Jiang,Xinshan Jiao,Xinping Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes an LLM-based multi-agent probabilistic framework to model how students develop different subjective social perceptions in classrooms using only local information and limited data, and shows it can reproduce realistic collective dynamics without assuming global knowledge.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real classrooms, students do not have complete or equally accurate information about the social network or peers’ academic performance. Yet, they still form beliefs about who is competent, popular, or central, and these beliefs evolve through local interactions. Existing models often assume a global, objective view of the social structure, which misses how subjective perceptions and local uncertainty shape group-level outcomes. The authors aim to capture how individual differences in accessible information, social anxiety, and trust propagate through peer communication and lead to stable, heterogeneous perceptions consistent with what is observed in education settings.

Method: The authors build a multi-agent simulation where each student is represented as an agent controlled by a large language model. Rather than using a single global social graph, they assign each student a subjective graph encoding which ties they can perceive and how far information can travel from their perspective. All reasoning and communication are constrained to this local, subjective view, implemented via retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) that only exposes local graph information to each agent. Agents produce narrative evaluations of classmates’ academic and social standing, annotated with uncertainty, and exchange these narratives over time. The model incorporates structural perturbations interpreted as social-anxiety effects that systemically reduce the accuracy of some agents’ perceptions. Belief updates about peers’ competence and status are done probabilistically, with LLM-based trust scores modulating how much an agent integrates received information. Real exam-score time series (six exams) are used as an exogenous ground truth to drive or benchmark the simulations, and multi-step simulations explore how epistemic uncertainty propagates through local peer interactions.

Result: The simulations show that the proposed framework, despite never accessing a global, objective social network, can generate collective patterns similar to those observed in real educational contexts. Specifically, heterogeneous and stable group-level differences in perceived competence and social standing emerge from local interactions and subjective views. The system captures how incomplete information, varying perceptual accuracy, and differential trust yield diverse, persistent social perceptions among students. The experiments demonstrate that epistemic uncertainty about peers spreads and stabilizes in structured ways through the network, matching qualitative expectations from classroom dynamics, and that structural perturbations such as social-anxiety-like distortions systematically shape these outcomes.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that an LLM-driven, multi-agent probabilistic modeling approach with subjective local graphs and constrained information access can realistically capture how students’ differing social perceptions form and evolve without assuming a global perspective. By integrating RAG, trust-weighted probabilistic belief updating, and structural perturbations representing social-anxiety, the framework provides a flexible tool for studying epistemic uncertainty and social perception in classrooms. This suggests broader applicability of LLM-based multi-agent models for understanding emergent collective beliefs and misperceptions in real-world social systems, where only partial and biased information is available to individuals.

Abstract: We present an LLM-driven multi-agent probabilistic modeling framework that demonstrates how differences in students' subjective social perceptions arise and evolve in real-world classroom settings, under constraints from an observed social network and limited questionnaire data. When social information is incomplete and the accuracy of perception differs between students, they can form different views of the same group structure from local cues they can access. Repeated peer communication and belief updates can gradually change these views and, over time, lead to stable group-level differences. To avoid assuming a global "god's-eye view," we assign each student an individualized subjective graph that shows which social ties they can perceive and how far information is reachable from their perspective. All judgments and interactions are restricted to this subjective graph: agents use retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) to access only local information and then form evaluations of peers' competence and social standing. We also add structural perturbations related to social-anxiety to represent consistent individual differences in the accuracy of social perception. During peer exchanges, agents share narrative assessments of classmates' academic performance and social position with uncertainty tags, and update beliefs probabilistically using LLM-based trust scores. Using the time series of six real exam scores as an exogenous reference, we run multi-step simulations to examine how epistemic uncertainty spreads through local interactions. Experiments show that, without relying on global information, the framework reproduces several collective dynamics consistent with real-world educational settings. The code is released at https://anonymous.4open.science/r/Rashomonomon-0126.

Result: The simulated agents’ engagement levels systematically change with information load and descriptive norms. Moreover, the influence of popularity cues (likes and reshares) depends on the context, showing conditional sensitivity instead of rigidly following the initial prompt instructions. This suggests that the model-based agents can exhibit structured, contingent behavior responsive to experimental conditions.

Conclusion: LLM-based simulations can support theory-relevant insights if they are designed and evaluated carefully. The authors argue for methodological practices such as multi-condition “stress tests” of behavior, explicit no-norm baseline conditions (because a default prompt is not a true neutral control), and preserving endogenous feedback loops—especially when studying phenomena like bandwagon effects. These practices help distinguish genuinely theory-consistent behavioral patterns from merely plausible language output.

Abstract: Large language models make agent-based simulation more behaviorally expressive, but they also sharpen a basic methodological tension: fluent, human-like output is not, by itself, evidence for theory. We evaluate what an LLM-driven simulation can credibly support using information engagement on social media as a test case. In a Weibo-like environment, we manipulate information load and descriptive norms, while allowing popularity cues (cumulative likes and Sina Weibo-style cumulative reshares) to evolve endogenously. We then ask whether simulated behavior changes in theoretically interpretable ways under these controlled variations, rather than merely producing plausible-looking traces. Engagement responds systematically to information load and descriptive norms, and sensitivity to popularity cues varies across contexts, indicating conditionality rather than rigid prompt compliance. We discuss methodological implications for simulation-based communication research, including multi-condition stress tests, explicit no-norm baselines because default prompts are not blank controls, and design choices that preserve endogenous feedback loops when studying bandwagon dynamics.

### [130] [Profit is the Red Team: Stress-Testing Agents in Strategic Economic Interactions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20925)
*Shouqiao Wang,Marcello Politi,Samuele Marro,Davide Crapis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces profit-driven red teaming, where an adaptive, learning adversary strategically manipulates inputs to test and improve the robustness of agentic systems in structured, auditable environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing red teaming often relies on static, handcrafted prompt attacks that fail to capture adaptive, strategic adversaries who can shape external inputs (retrieved content, tool outputs, other actors’ messages) in real-world deployments. As agents increasingly operate in economically relevant, multi-actor settings, there is a need for a systematic way to assess and harden them against profit-motivated manipulation and exploitation based on outcome feedback, not manual labels or taxonomies.

Method: The authors propose a stress-testing protocol called profit-driven red teaming. They train a learned opponent whose objective is to maximize its profit against the target agent, using only scalar outcome feedback, without LLM-as-judge scoring or curated attack labels. The protocol is instantiated in a lean arena consisting of four canonical economic interaction games that form a controlled testbed. The adversarial policy learns strategies (e.g., probing, anchoring, deceptive commitments) through interaction, and successful exploit traces are then distilled into concise prompt rules that are fed back into the target agent as defenses.

Result: In controlled experiments, agents that appear robust against standard static red-team baselines are nonetheless consistently exploitable when faced with a profit-optimized opponent. The learned adversary autonomously discovers sophisticated manipulative tactics such as probing, anchoring, and deceptive commitments, despite not being explicitly taught them. When the researchers convert these exploit episodes into explicit prompt rules or guidelines for the agent, most previously observed failures disappear and the target agents’ performance improves substantially across the considered economic interactions.

Conclusion: Profit-driven red teaming, using a learning adversary motivated by profit in structured, auditable settings, reveals vulnerabilities that static red teaming misses and yields concrete defensive data. Distilling learned exploits into prompt-level rules significantly boosts robustness, suggesting this protocol is a practical path for hardening agentic systems operating in economic or other structured environments where outcomes are observable and can be used as feedback.

Abstract: As agentic systems move into real-world deployments, their decisions increasingly depend on external inputs such as retrieved content, tool outputs, and information provided by other actors. When these inputs can be strategically shaped by adversaries, the relevant security risk extends beyond a fixed library of prompt attacks to adaptive strategies that steer agents toward unfavorable outcomes. We propose profit-driven red teaming, a stress-testing protocol that replaces handcrafted attacks with a learned opponent trained to maximize its profit using only scalar outcome feedback. The protocol requires no LLM-as-judge scoring, attack labels, or attack taxonomy, and is designed for structured settings with auditable outcomes. We instantiate it in a lean arena of four canonical economic interactions, which provide a controlled testbed for adaptive exploitability. In controlled experiments, agents that appear strong against static baselines become consistently exploitable under profit-optimized pressure, and the learned opponent discovers probing, anchoring, and deceptive commitments without explicit instruction. We then distill exploit episodes into concise prompt rules for the agent, which make most previously observed failures ineffective and substantially improve target performance. These results suggest that profit-driven red-team data can provide a practical route to improving robustness in structured agent settings with auditable outcomes.

</details>

The authors aim to fill this gap by providing an implementation of UFO that is usable, standardized, and addresses recurring modeling problems.

Method: The authors design and formalize gUFO as an OWL 2 DL ontology derived from the original UFO, selecting and adapting categories, relations, and constraints to fit OWL’s expressivity and Semantic Web usage. They introduce specific modeling patterns (for type typology, reification of intrinsic and relational aspects, situations, and higher-order types), analyze how gUFO differs from the full UFO reference ontology, and position gUFO through a systematic comparison with OWL implementations of other foundational ontologies such as BFO and DOLCE. They also report on evidence of community adoption.

Result: The outcome is gUFO, a lightweight yet well-founded OWL 2 DL implementation of UFO that: (1) operationalizes OntoClean through an explicit typology of types; (2) provides reification patterns for intrinsic and relational aspects; (3) supports modeling of situations and higher-order types; and (4) offers reusable patterns that tackle recurrent Semantic Web knowledge graph modeling issues. The paper documents gUFO’s structure, its differences from the original UFO, and its positioning relative to other foundational ontology implementations.

Conclusion: gUFO successfully brings the theoretical strengths of the Unified Foundational Ontology into a practical OWL 2 DL form appropriate for Semantic Web applications. By supporting an advanced typology of types, aspect reification, and situations/higher-order types, it fills limitations of existing foundational ontology implementations and offers robust, reusable modeling patterns for knowledge graphs. UFO is a mature foundational ontology with a rich axiomatization and that has been employed in a significant number of projects in research and industry. Moreover, it is currently in the process of standardization by the International Organization for Standardization as the ISO/IEC CD 21838-5. gUFO stands out from other foundational ontology implementations (such as those provided for BFO and DOLCE) given its unique support for a typology of types (operationalizing OntoClean guidelines), its reification patterns for intrinsic and relational aspects, and its support for situations and high-order types. gUFO provides well-founded patterns to address recurrent problems in Semantic Web knowledge graphs. In this paper, we present gUFO with its constituting categories, relations and constraints, discuss how it differs from the original UFO reference ontology, elaborate on its community adoption, and systematically position it in relation to existing OWL-based implementations of popular alternative foundational ontologies.

### [132] [AutoMOOSE: An Agentic AI for Autonomous Phase-Field Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20986)
*Sukriti Manna,Henry Chan,Subramanian K. R. S. Sankaranarayanan*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Even though multiphysics frameworks like MOOSE are powerful for phase-field materials modeling, they are difficult to use: constructing correct input files, doing parameter sweeps, debugging runs, and extracting quantitative results all require deep expert knowledge and manual effort. This limits broader adoption and slows down research workflows. The authors aim to reduce this barrier and move toward AI-assisted, possibly autonomous, simulation-driven materials discovery.

Method: The authors design AutoMOOSE, an agentic orchestration layer around MOOSE. It uses a five-agent pipeline, where an Input Writer agent coordinates six specialized sub-agents to generate full MOOSE input files from natural-language prompts, and a Reviewer agent automatically inspects and corrects runtime failures. The framework uses a modular plugin architecture so that new phase-field models can be added without touching the core system. They wrap the workflow in an MCP (Model Context Protocol) server that exposes ten structured tools, enabling integration with any MCP-compatible client. The system is evaluated on a copper grain-growth benchmark at multiple temperatures, comparing AI-generated inputs and outcomes to human-expert baselines.

Result: On a four-temperature copper grain growth benchmark, AutoMOOSE produces MOOSE input files where 6 of 12 structural blocks exactly match a human-expert reference and 4 more are functionally equivalent. It recovers grain coarsening kinetics with R² = 0.90–0.95 for temperatures ≥600 K, and an activation energy Q_fit = 0.296 eV, close to the human-reference value 0.267 eV under the same setup. It also identifies and fixes three classes of runtime failures within a single correction iteration, and generates FAIR-compliant provenance records for every run.

Conclusion: A relatively lightweight multi-agent orchestration layer can meaningfully lower the expertise and effort required to run validated phase-field simulations in MOOSE. By automating input construction, execution management, error correction, and physical validation from natural-language specifications, AutoMOOSE narrows the gap between conceptual physics understanding and operational simulation campaigns. This is presented as a step toward AI-driven materials discovery workflows and ultimately self-driving laboratories, while preserving rigor via provenance and consistency checks.

Abstract: Multiphysics simulation frameworks such as MOOSE provide rigorous engines for phase-field materials modeling, yet adoption is constrained by the expertise required to construct valid input files, coordinate parameter sweeps, diagnose failures, and extract quantitative results. We introduce AutoMOOSE, an open-source agentic framework that orchestrates the full simulation lifecycle from a single natural-language prompt. AutoMOOSE deploys a five-agent pipeline in which the Input Writer coordinates six sub-agents and the Reviewer autonomously corrects runtime failures without user intervention. A modular plugin architecture enables new phase-field formulations without modifying the core framework, and a Model Context Protocol (MCP) server exposes the workflow as ten structured tools for interoperability with any MCP-compatible client. Validated on a four-temperature copper grain growth benchmark, AutoMOOSE generates MOOSE input files with 6 of 12 structural blocks matching a human expert reference exactly and 4 functionally equivalent, executes all runs in parallel with a 1.8x speedup, and performs an end-to-end physical consistency check spanning intent, finite-element execution, and Arrhenius kinetics with no human verification. Three runtime failure classes were diagnosed and resolved autonomously within a single correction cycle, and every run produces a provenance record satisfying FAIR data principles. ### [133] [Can we automatize scientific discovery in the cognitive sciences?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20988)
*Akshay K. Jagadish,Milena Rmus,Kristin Witte,Marvin Mathony,Marcel Binz,Eric Schulz*

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current cognitive science relies on a slow, human-driven cycle: designing experiments, collecting data, and testing a limited set of pre-specified model classes. This process is constrained by human time, creativity, and existing theoretical biases, making it hard to explore the huge space of possible cognitive mechanisms and experimental paradigms. The authors want a more scalable, systematic way to discover and refine theories of mind.

Method: They design an in-silico discovery loop where: (1) Large Language Models (LLMs) propose experimental paradigms that embody conceptually meaningful task structures; (2) foundation models of cognition generate high-fidelity synthetic behavioral data for these tasks; (3) LLM-based program synthesis automatically constructs candidate cognitive models (algorithms) to explain the data, performing a high-throughput search over many hypotheses; and (4) an LLM-critic evaluates the conceptual yield or ‘interestingness’ of the resulting models and experiments, feeding this back to guide the next iteration of the loop.

Result: The abstract does not report detailed empirical results, but it claims that the proposed automated loop can rapidly surface informative experiments and plausible cognitive mechanisms by continuously generating tasks, simulating behavior, proposing models, and filtering them via an LLM-based notion of interestingness.

Conclusion: A fully automated, LLM-centered discovery engine can serve as a scalable, high-throughput in-silico method for developing cognitive theories. By automating each stage of the traditional experimental cycle and using LLMs both as generators and critics, cognitive science can more efficiently identify promising experiments and mechanisms for later validation with real human participants.

Abstract: The cognitive sciences aim to understand intelligence by formalizing underlying operations as computational models. Traditionally, this follows a cycle of discovery where researchers develop paradigms, collect data, and test predefined model classes. However, this manual pipeline is fundamentally constrained by the slow pace of human intervention and a search space limited by researchers' background and intuition. Here, we propose a paradigm shift toward a fully automated, in silico science of the mind that implements every stage of the discovery cycle using Large Language Models (LLMs). In this framework, experimental paradigms exploring conceptually meaningful task structures are directly sampled from an LLM. High-fidelity behavioral data are then simulated using foundation models of cognition. The tedious step of handcrafting cognitive models is replaced by LLM-based program synthesis, which performs a high-throughput search over a vast landscape of algorithmic hypotheses. Finally, the discovery loop is closed by optimizing for ''interestingness'', a metric of conceptual yield evaluated by an LLM-critic. By enabling a fast and scalable approach to theory development, this automated loop functions as a high-throughput in-silico discovery engine, surfacing informative experiments and mechanisms for subsequent validation in real human populations.

### [134] [The Intelligent Disobedience Game: Formulating Disobedience in Stackelberg Games and Markov Decision Processes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.20994)
*Benedikt Hornig,Reuth Mirsky*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces the Intelligent Disobedience Game (IDG), a game-theoretic framework to model when and how an automated assistant should override human instructions for safety, and provides a Markov decision process formulation to train and study such agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In shared autonomy settings (e.g., assistive robots, automated driving, medical decision support), AI systems sometimes must choose between following human commands and overriding them to prevent harm. While this "intelligent disobedience" is safety-critical, there is a lack of formal mathematical models capturing the strategic interaction, information asymmetry, and multi-step nature of these decisions. Without such foundations, it is hard to design principled algorithms that disobey safely and to systematically study human trust in such systems.

Method: The authors define the Intelligent Disobedience Game (IDG) as a sequential Stackelberg game between a human leader and an assistive follower with asymmetric information about safety and goals. They analyze this game to derive optimal strategies for both agents over multi-step interactions, identifying conditions leading to phenomena like safety traps, where the system perpetually avoids harm but also perpetually fails to achieve the user’s higher-level goal. They then construct an equivalent shared-control Multi-Agent Markov Decision Process (MA-MDP) representation of the IDG to serve as a computational environment. This MA-MDP can be used with reinforcement learning algorithms to train assistive agents that appropriately learn when to comply or disobey.

Result: The analysis characterizes optimal policies for human and assistive agents within the IDG framework and reveals strategic pathologies such as safety traps arising from overly conservative disobedience. The translation of the game into a shared-control MA-MDP yields a compact, simulation-friendly testbed suitable for training and evaluating reinforcement learning agents that must balance safety against goal achievement. This provides concrete settings in which to study learned intelligent disobedience behaviors and their effects on performance and safety metrics.

Conclusion: The paper establishes a formal game-theoretic model for intelligent disobedience in shared autonomy, demonstrating how optimal strategies can both ensure safety and, under certain conditions, unintentionally block goal achievement. By offering both the IDG and its MA-MDP instantiation, the work lays a mathematical and computational foundation for designing, training, and empirically evaluating AI systems that can safely decide when to override human instructions and for studying human trust and perception of such disobedient behavior.

Abstract: In shared autonomy, a critical tension arises when an automated assistant must choose between obeying a human's instruction and deliberately overriding it to prevent harm. This safety-critical behavior is known as intelligent disobedience. To formalize this dynamic, this paper introduces the Intelligent Disobedience Game (IDG), a sequential game-theoretic framework based on Stackelberg games that models the interaction between a human leader and an assistive follower operating under asymmetric information. It characterizes optimal strategies for both agents across multi-step scenarios, identifying strategic phenomena such as ``safety traps,'' where the system indefinitely avoids harm but fails to achieve the human's goal. The IDG provides a needed mathematical foundation that enables both the algorithmic development of agents that can learn safe non-compliance and the empirical study of how humans perceive and trust disobedient AI. The paper further translates the IDG into a shared control Multi-Agent Markov Decision Process representation, forming a compact computational testbed for training reinforcement learning agents.

Second, we implement extensive Function Calling capabilities that elevate the LLM to an agentic planner, orchestrating robot actions (navigation, gaze control, tablet interaction) and integrating diverse multimodal feedback (vision, touch, system state). The framework runs on the robot's tablet but can also be built to run on regular Android smartphones or tablets, decoupling development from robot hardware. This work provides the HRI community with a practical, extensible platform for exploring advanced LLM-driven embodied interaction.

### [136] [Knowledge Boundary Discovery for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21022)
*Ziquan Wang,Zhongqi Lu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They introduce Knowledge Boundary Discovery (KBD), a reinforcement-learning framework that automatically finds what an LLM does and does not know by generating questions at its knowledge limits.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM evaluations rely heavily on static, human-crafted benchmarks that may not reveal where models start to hallucinate or move from confident knowledge to uncertainty. There is a need for an automatic, model-centric method that probes and maps out these knowledge boundaries without full prior knowledge of the test space.

Method: They model interaction with an LLM as a partially observable environment and use a reinforcement learning agent to iteratively generate questions. Each action is a new, progressively harder or more specific question. The LLM’s response is treated as an observation, and the agent maintains and updates belief states about the model’s knowledge. The reward is based on entropy reduction, encouraging questions that most effectively clarify whether the LLM is within or beyond its knowledge boundary, resulting in two classes of questions: answerable (within-boundary) and unanswerable (beyond-boundary).

Result: KBD is able to automatically construct sets of non-trivial questions that the target LLM can answer confidently and questions it cannot answer reliably. When they compare these automatically generated question sets to established, manually designed LLM benchmarks, they find that KBD’s generated boundaries are comparable in quality and coverage to human-crafted datasets.

Conclusion: KBD provides an automated, reinforcement-learning-based way to discover and characterize LLM knowledge boundaries by generating targeted questions and interpreting model responses. This approach yields question sets similar to human benchmarks and offers a new, adaptive direction for evaluating and stress-testing LLM capabilities and hallucination behavior.

Abstract: We propose Knowledge Boundary Discovery (KBD), a reinforcement learning based framework to explore the knowledge boundaries of the Large Language Models (LLMs). We define the knowledge boundary by automatically generating two types of questions: (i) those the LLM can confidently answer (within-knowledge boundary) and (ii) those it cannot (beyond-knowledge boundary). Iteratively exploring and exploiting the LLM's responses to find its knowledge boundaries is challenging because of the hallucination phenomenon. To find the knowledge boundaries of an LLM, the agent interacts with the LLM under the modeling of exploring a partially observable environment. The agent generates a progressive question as the action, adopts an entropy reduction as the reward, receives the LLM's response as the observation and updates its belief states. We demonstrate that the KBD detects knowledge boundaries of LLMs by automatically finding a set of non-trivial answerable and unanswerable questions. We validate the KBD by comparing its generated knowledge boundaries with manually crafted LLM benchmark datasets. Experiments show that our KBD-generated question set is comparable to the human-generated datasets. Our approach paves a new way to evaluate LLMs.

Method: KLDrive introduces two coupled components: (1) an energy-based scene fact construction module that fuses and reconciles multi-source evidence (e.g., sensors, perception outputs) into a coherent, reliable scene knowledge graph; and (2) an LLM agent that performs fact-grounded reasoning over this graph using a constrained action space and explicit structural constraints, guided by structured prompting and few-shot in-context examples, to adapt to varied reasoning tasks without task-specific fine-tuning.

We present KLDrive, the first knowledge-graph-augmented LLM reasoning framework for fine-grained question answering in autonomous driving. Experiments on two large-scale autonomous-driving QA benchmarks show that KLDrive outperforms prior state-of-the-art methods, achieving the best overall accuracy of 65.04% on NuScenes-QA and the best SPICE score of 42.45 on GVQA. HisPO uses gradient masking that accounts for policy staleness and discrepancies between training and inference engines at both sequence and token levels. The proposed LongCat-Flash-Prover sets a new state-of-the-art for open-weight models on several theorem-proving benchmarks, showing that carefully designed agentic RL, stability mechanisms for MoE, and consistency checks can significantly advance automated formal mathematics with open-source systems.

Abstract: We introduce LongCat-Flash-Prover, a flagship 560-billion-parameter open-source Mixture-of- Experts (MoE) model that advances Native Formal Reasoning in Lean4 through agentic tool-integrated reasoning (TIR). We decompose the native formal reasoning task into three independent formal capabilities, i.e., auto-formalization, sketching, and proving. To facilitate these capabilities, we propose a Hybrid-Experts Iteration Framework to expand high-quality task trajectories, including generating a formal statement based on a given informal problem, producing a whole-proof directly from the statement, or a lemma-style sketch. During agentic RL, we present a Hierarchical Importance Sampling Policy Optimization (HisPO) algorithm, which aims to stabilize the MoE model training on such long-horizon tasks. It employs a gradient masking strategy that accounts for the policy staleness and the inherent train-inference engine discrepancies at both sequence and token levels. Additionally, we also incorporate theorem consistency and legality detection mechanisms to eliminate reward hacking issues. Extensive evaluations show that our LongCat-Flash-Prover sets a new state-of-the-art for open-weights models in both auto-formalization and theorem proving. Demonstrating remarkable sample efficiency, it achieves a 97.1% pass rate on MiniF2F-Test using only 72 inference budget per problem. On more challenging benchmarks, it solves 70.8% of ProverBench and 41.5% of PutnamBench with no more than 220 attempts per problem, significantly outperforming existing open-weights baselines.

### [139] [ORACLE: Optimizing Reasoning Abilities of Large Language Models via Constraint-Led Synthetic Data Elicitation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21140)
*Zhuojie Yang,Wentao Wan,Keze Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes ORACLE, a framework that uses structured, syllogism-inspired reasoning chains plus symbolic verification of each step to generate higher-quality synthetic multi-step reasoning data for LLM training, improving performance on diverse reasoning benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Synthetic reasoning data is widely used to improve LLM reasoning, but current methods mostly filter by correctness of the final answer and ignore errors in intermediate steps. Existing step-level checking approaches rely on code execution or symbolic engines that require precise, complete contexts, so they don’t work well for natural language reasoning with ambiguity or incomplete information. There is a need for a framework that can reliably validate intermediate reasoning steps in open-ended natural language tasks.

Method: ORACLE combines LLM-based generation with symbolic supervision in a structured, syllogistic style. The LLM is prompted with a unified template to produce modular, step-by-step reasoning chains. These steps are then checked by a symbolic reasoning engine that verifies the logical validity of each intermediate inference rather than only the final answer. Invalid steps are filtered or corrected, yielding a curated set of high-quality multi-step reasoning traces that can be used as training data. The design focuses on making reasoning steps structured enough for symbolic checking while still being generated in natural language contexts.

Result: When used to generate training data and train LLMs, ORACLE-based data leads to consistent improvements over strong baseline synthetic-data methods. Across six benchmarks spanning logical, factual, and commonsense reasoning, and evaluated on multiple model backbones, models trained with ORACLE data achieve higher performance, indicating better reasoning capabilities and generalization.

Conclusion: Incorporating symbolic step-level validation into LLM-generated reasoning chains yields higher-quality synthetic reasoning data than filtering only by final correctness. ORACLE’s syllogism-inspired, modular reasoning format allows symbolic engines to operate even in natural language settings, enabling fine-grained verification. This structured data generation approach improves LLM reasoning across diverse benchmarks, suggesting that hybrid generative-symbolic supervision is a promising direction for future reasoning-focused LLM training.

Abstract: Training large language models (LLMs) with synthetic reasoning data has become a popular approach to enhancing their reasoning capabilities, while a key factor influencing the effectiveness of this paradigm is the quality of the generated multi-step reasoning data. To generate high-quality reasoning data, many recent methods generate synthetic reasoning paths and filter them based on final answer correctness, often overlooking flaws in intermediate reasoning steps. To enhance the verification of intermediate reasoning steps, prior work primarily resorts to code execution or symbolic reasoning engines. However, code-based validation is restricted to code or mathematical tasks, and reasoning engines require a well-structured and complete context. As a result, existing methods fail to function effectively in natural language reasoning tasks that involve ambiguous or incomplete contexts. In these tasks, synthetic data still lack reliable checks for verifying each reasoning step. To address this challenge, we introduce ORACLE, a structured data generation framework inspired by syllogistic reasoning. ORACLE integrates the generative strengths of LLMs with symbolic supervision: the LLM produces step-wise reasoning contexts, while a symbolic reasoning engine verifies the validity of each intermediate step. By employing a unified prompting template to elicit modular reasoning chains, ORACLE enables fine-grained, step-level validation, facilitating the construction of high-quality multi-step reasoning data. Across six logical, factual, and commonsense reasoning benchmarks, our ORACLE consistently outperforms strong baselines on multiple models.

### [140] [Can LLMs Fool Graph Learning? Exploring Universal Adversarial Attacks on Text-Attributed Graphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21155)
*Zihui Chen,Yuling Wang,Pengfei Jiao,Kai Wu,Xiao Wang,Xiang Ao,Dalin Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces BadGraph, a universal adversarial attack framework for text-attributed graphs that jointly perturbs graph topology and node texts using LLM reasoning, significantly degrading performance of both GNN- and LLM-based TAG models while remaining stealthy and interpretable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing text-attributed graph models leverage both graph structure and node text via diverse backbones (GNNs, PLMs, LLMs), but this diversity creates a fragmented threat landscape. Current attacks are usually architecture-specific and often assume white-box access, neglecting API-based black-box PLMs. There is a need for a universal, architecture-agnostic attack that can evaluate and stress-test the security of TAG models in realistic settings where models perceive and encode graph patterns very differently.

Method: The authors propose BadGraph, an attack framework that uses large language models as reasoning engines to craft perturbations on both graph topology and node texts. They first design a target influencer retrieval module that exploits graph priors to find influential nodes and construct cross-modally aligned attack shortcuts, i.e., structural and textual perturbation opportunities that are mutually reinforcing. Using these identified targets, BadGraph queries LLMs (even via black-box APIs) to generate semantically plausible yet adversarial text modifications, and coordinates them with topological changes to fool a wide range of TAG models, including GNN-based and LLM/PLM-based reasoners.

Result: Across multiple TAG benchmarks and diverse model architectures, BadGraph causes large drops in performance—up to 76.3%—demonstrating that its perturbations effectively transfer between GNN-based and LLM-based models. The framework works in black-box scenarios and maintains a high degree of stealth (small, natural-looking text and structural changes) while still being potent. Theoretical analysis supports why the cross-modal alignment and influencer retrieval increase attack transferability, and empirical case studies show that the attacks remain interpretable to humans.

Conclusion: BadGraph provides a universal, transferable adversarial attack mechanism for text-attributed graphs by jointly exploiting structural and textual vulnerabilities using LLM reasoning. This exposes substantial security risks in current TAG models, including those accessed only via APIs, and highlights the need for more robust defenses that account for both graph and language modalities. The framework also offers interpretable perturbations that can help researchers understand and mitigate vulnerabilities in future TAG architectures.

Abstract: Text-attributed graphs (TAGs) enhance graph learning by integrating rich textual semantics and topological context for each node. While boosting expressiveness, they also expose new vulnerabilities in graph learning through text-based adversarial surfaces. Recent advances leverage diverse backbones, such as graph neural networks (GNNs) and pre-trained language models (PLMs), to capture both structural and textual information in TAGs. This diversity raises a key question: How can we design universal adversarial attacks that generalize across architectures to assess the security of TAG models? The challenge arises from the stark contrast in how different backbones-GNNs and PLMs-perceive and encode graph patterns, coupled with the fact that many PLMs are only accessible via APIs, limiting attacks to black-box settings. To address this, we propose BadGraph, a novel attack framework that deeply elicits large language models (LLMs) understanding of general graph knowledge to jointly perturb both node topology and textual semantics. Specifically, we design a target influencer retrieval module that leverages graph priors to construct cross-modally aligned attack shortcuts, thereby enabling efficient LLM-based perturbation reasoning. Experiments show that BadGraph achieves universal and effective attacks across GNN- and LLM-based reasoners, with up to a 76.3% performance drop, while theoretical and empirical analyses confirm its stealthy yet interpretable nature.

### [141] [Does AI Homogenize Student Thinking? A Multi-Dimensional Analysis of Structural Convergence in AI-Augmented Essays](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21228)
*Keito Inoshita,Michiaki Omura,Tsukasa Yamanaka,Go Maeda,Kentaro Tsuji*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper empirically shows that AI-assisted writing increases essay quality but makes certain aspects of students’ writing structures more similar to each other, revealing a tradeoff between quality and structural diversity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI writing tools are widely adopted in education and are known to improve essay quality, but it is unclear how they affect the diversity of students’ thinking as reflected in essay structure. The authors want to understand whether AI support makes students’ writing more homogeneous and under what conditions this occurs or can be avoided.

Method: The authors analyzed 6,875 essays produced under five conditions: Human-only, AI-only, and three different Human+AI prompt strategies. They measured multiple structural dimensions of the essays, such as cohesion architecture and perspective plurality, and compared variance and convergence patterns across conditions. They also conducted convergence target analysis to examine how human–AI interaction shifts essay structures relative to purely human and purely AI baselines, and tested how varying prompt specificity alters these effects.

Result: They identified a Quality-Homogenization Tradeoff: AI-augmented essays showed substantial quality improvements but also a marked reduction in structural diversity on some dimensions. Specifically, variance in cohesion architecture dropped by 70–78%, indicating strong homogenization, whereas perspective plurality actually became more diverse. Convergence analysis showed that AI-augmented essays moved toward AI-like structural patterns but also diverged from a simple Human–AI axis, suggesting partial replacement of human structures by AI patterns along with the emergence of novel hybrid structures. Importantly, when prompts were made more specific, homogenization on argument depth flipped into diversification.

Conclusion: AI assistance improves essay quality but can compress the diversity of structural patterns in student writing, particularly in cohesion. However, this homogenization is not inherent to AI itself; it depends on how human–AI interaction is designed. By adjusting prompt specificity and interaction strategies, educators and system designers can encourage diversification on key dimensions such as argument depth, mitigating the loss of structural diversity while retaining quality gains.

Abstract: While AI-assisted writing has been widely reported to improve essay quality, its impact on the structural diversity of student thinking remains unexplored. Analyzing 6,875 essays across five conditions (Human-only, AI-only, and three Human+AI prompt strategies), we provide the first empirical evidence of a Quality-Homogenization Tradeoff, in which substantial quality gains co-occur with significant homogenization. The effect is dimension-specific: cohesion architecture lost 70-78% of its variance, whereas perspective plurality was diversified. Convergence target analysis further revealed that AI-augmented essays were pulled toward AI structural patterns yet deviated significantly from the Human-AI axis, indicating simultaneous partial replacement and partial emergence. Crucially, prompt specificity reversed homogenization into diversification on argument depth, demonstrating that homogenization is not an intrinsic property of AI but a function of interaction design.

### [142] [ConsRoute:Consistency-Aware Adaptive Query Routing for Cloud-Edge-Device Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21237)
*Haoyu Qiao,Hao Zhang,Shanwen Mao,Siyao Cheng,Jie Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ConsRoute is a lightweight semantic-aware routing framework for cloud–edge–device LLM inference that uses reranking-based semantic consistency and hidden-state reuse to cut latency and cost by ~40% while preserving ≥95% of cloud-only quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are powerful but slow and expensive to run, especially in latency-sensitive and resource-limited environments. Cloud–edge–device collaborative inference can help by routing each query to an appropriate model tier, but existing routing methods use coarse predictors of quality, missing fine-grained trade-offs and incurring extra overhead. There is a need for a routing framework that is more accurate, semantic-aware, and low-overhead so that overall latency and cost are reduced without noticeably hurting response quality.

Method: The paper introduces ConsRoute, a routing framework for multi-tier LLM deployments. It uses a reranker to estimate semantic consistency between responses from different model tiers, providing fine-grained soft supervision labels for training the router. On-device, ConsRoute avoids additional encoders or passes by reusing hidden states from the LLM prefilling stage as compact query representations. These representations are clustered, and for each cluster, Bayesian optimization is used to learn specialized routing thresholds that trade off quality, latency, and cost under varying query distributions. At inference, a query is represented via its reused hidden state, mapped to a cluster, and then routed based on the learned cluster-specific thresholds.

Result: Experiments on collaborative cloud–edge–device LLM setups show that ConsRoute maintains at least 95% of the performance of a cloud-only deployment while cutting end-to-end latency and inference cost by about 40%. Across benchmarks, it outperforms existing routing baselines on both final response quality and system efficiency metrics.

Conclusion: ConsRoute demonstrates that semantic-consistency-based supervision and hidden-state reuse enable accurate, low-overhead routing in hierarchical LLM deployments. By combining reranker-guided signals, representation clustering, and Bayesian-optimized thresholds, it delivers near-cloud quality with substantially reduced latency and cost, offering a practical solution for deploying LLMs in latency- and resource-constrained scenarios.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) deliver impressive capabilities but incur substantial inference latency and cost, which hinders their deployment in latency-sensitive and resource-constrained scenarios. Cloud-edge-device collaborative inference has emerged as a promising paradigm by dynamically routing queries to models of different capacities across tiers. In this paper, we propose ConsRoute, a lightweight, semantic-aware, and adaptive routing framework that significantly improves inference efficiency while minimizing impact on response quality. Unlike prior routing methods that rely on predicting coarse-grained output quality gaps, ConsRoute leverages a reranker to directly assess the semantic consistency between responses generated by models at different tiers, yielding fine-grained soft supervision signals for routing. To minimize device-side overhead, ConsRoute reuses hidden states from the LLM prefilling stage as compact query representations, avoiding additional encoders or inference passes. Furthermore, these representations are clustered, and Bayesian optimization is employed to learn cluster-specific routing thresholds that dynamically balance quality, latency, and cost under heterogeneous query distributions. Extensive experiments demonstrate that ConsRoute achieves near-cloud performance (>=95%) while reducing end-to-end latency and inference cost by nearly 40%, consistently outperforming existing routing baselines in both response quality and system efficiency.

### [143] [Graph of States: Solving Abductive Tasks with Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21250)
*Yu Luo,Rongchen Gao,Lu Teng,Xidao Wen,Jiamin Jiang,Qingliang Zhang,Yongqian Sun,Shenglin Zhang,Jiasong Feng,Tong Liu,Wenjie Zhang,Dan Pei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Graph of States (GoS), a neuro-symbolic framework using structured belief states, causal graphs, and state machines to enable reliable abductive reasoning in large language model systems, significantly outperforming existing methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs perform well on deductive and inductive reasoning, they struggle with abductive reasoning, which involves inferring the best explanation for observations. Existing reasoning frameworks are mostly built for static deductive tasks, with unstructured state representations and no explicit control over reasoning states. This leads to issues such as fabricating evidence, drifting away from the original context, failing to revise incorrect paths, and stopping prematurely. There is a need for a framework that can structure and control the reasoning process specifically for abductive tasks.

Method: The authors propose Graph of States (GoS), a general-purpose neuro-symbolic framework for abductive reasoning. GoS represents reasoning as transitions over structured belief states. It uses: (1) a causal graph to explicitly encode logical dependencies among hypotheses, evidence, and conclusions; and (2) a state machine to constrain and control valid reasoning transitions (e.g., proposing, revising, or discarding hypotheses). Multi-agent collaboration among LLM-based agents is grounded in this symbolic structure, ensuring that the reasoning steps adhere to the causal graph and state machine, turning open-ended exploration into a directed search process.

Result: On two real-world abductive reasoning datasets, GoS is empirically shown to substantially outperform existing baseline methods designed primarily for deductive tasks. The framework reduces common pathologies in LLM reasoning such as Evidence Fabrication, Context Drift, Failed Backtracking, and Early Stopping, leading to more accurate and reliable abductive inferences.

Conclusion: GoS provides an effective and general-purpose solution for complex abductive reasoning tasks by combining LLMs with structured symbolic control via causal graphs and state machines. By grounding multi-agent reasoning in explicit belief states and constrained transitions, it converts unconstrained reasoning into a convergent search process and achieves significant performance gains over prior methods. The approach suggests a promising direction for integrating neuro-symbolic techniques to address higher-level reasoning challenges in LLMs.

Abstract: Logical reasoning encompasses deduction, induction, and abduction. However, while Large Language Models (LLMs) have effectively mastered the former two, abductive reasoning remains significantly underexplored. Existing frameworks, predominantly designed for static deductive tasks, fail to generalize to abductive reasoning due to unstructured state representation and lack of explicit state control. Consequently, they are inevitably prone to Evidence Fabrication, Context Drift, Failed Backtracking, and Early Stopping. To bridge this gap, we introduce Graph of States (GoS), a general-purpose neuro-symbolic framework tailored for abductive tasks. GoS grounds multi-agent collaboration in a structured belief states, utilizing a causal graph to explicitly encode logical dependencies and a state machine to govern the valid transitions of the reasoning process. By dynamically aligning the reasoning focus with these symbolic constraints, our approach transforms aimless, unconstrained exploration into a convergent, directed search. Extensive evaluations on two real-world datasets demonstrate that GoS significantly outperforms all baselines, providing a robust solution for complex abductive tasks. Code repo and all prompts: https://anonymous.4open.science/r/Graph-of-States-5B4E.

### [144] [The Library Theorem: How External Organization Governs Agentic Reasoning Capacity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21272)
*Zachary F. Mainen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that transformer-based agents gain major efficiency by using indexed external memory for retrieval instead of sequentially scanning their own chain-of-thought, both theoretically and empirically.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While chain-of-thought lets transformer agents externalize reasoning, they still typically scan this growing context sequentially, which becomes inefficient as reasoning length increases. The authors aim to understand and improve how agents retrieve information from their own externalized reasoning, treating the context window as a paged I/O system and asking whether index-like structures can offer asymptotic gains, and how current language models behave when given such tools.

Method: The authors formalize the transformer context window as an I/O page abstraction and analytically compare retrieval costs for two regimes: sequential scan versus indexed access via external tools, deriving asymptotic bounds on page reads per query and cumulative cost over many reasoning steps. They then design a controlled lookup benchmark across three content types (random hashes, ordered integers, and encyclopedia entries) and across memory sizes from 50 to 5,000 items, implementing agents with and without indexed retrieval tools. They run experiments on two model versions (GPT-4o-mini and GPT-5.4), measuring page reads and token usage, as well as observing behavioral patterns such as adherence to or deviation from the retrieval protocol.

Result: Theoretically, indexed external memory yields exponentially better retrieval asymptotics than sequential scanning: O(log_b N) versus Ω(N) per query and O(T log_b T) versus Θ(T^2) over T steps, with indexed agents approaching O(1) reads in some abstract settings. Empirically, on abstract content (like random hashes), the indexed agent achieves a median of one page read across all store sizes, validating the predicted constant or logarithmic scaling. Attempts to simulate indexing using sorted pages without a true index do not close the efficiency gap: the weaker model cannot reliably execute binary search at scale, and the stronger model performs close to ideal log_2 N search yet still uses roughly five times more reads than the indexed agent. On familiar, semantically rich content (encyclopedia entries), models frequently ignore the retrieval protocol, relying on their internal parametric memory, which causes very high token usage even when the external index is correctly configured.

Conclusion: Indexed external memory tools can dramatically reduce retrieval costs compared to naive sequential scanning of the context window, but end-to-end performance depends on the interaction between a model’s semantic competence and its capacity to follow procedural protocols. The findings suggest that language models should be primarily used for constructing semantic indexes—where their understanding is beneficial—while deterministic, non-ML algorithms should handle traversing these indexes—where model understanding can actually be detrimental by encouraging protocol-violating shortcuts. This supports a design principle of separating content understanding from navigation and control in tool-augmented language model systems.

Abstract: Externalized reasoning is already exploited by transformer-based agents through chain-of-thought, but structured retrieval -- indexing over one's own reasoning state -- remains underexplored. We formalize the transformer context window as an I/O page and prove that tool-augmented agents with indexed external memory achieve exponentially lower retrieval cost than agents restricted to sequential scanning: $O(\log_b N)$ versus $Ω(N)$ page reads per query, and $O(T \log_b T)$ versus $Θ(T^2)$ cumulative cost over $T$ reasoning steps -- a gap that widens as deliberation deepens. We test these predictions on a controlled lookup benchmark across three content types -- random hashes, ordered integers, and encyclopedia entries -- varying store size from 50 to 5,000 items, and replicate key conditions across two model generations (GPT-4o-mini and GPT-5.4). On abstract content, the indexed agent achieves median 1 page read regardless of store size, confirming the $O(1)$ prediction. Sorted pages without an index fail to close the gap: the weaker model cannot sustain binary search at scale, and the stronger model achieves near-optimal $\log_2 N$ search but still loses to the index by $5\times$. On familiar content (encyclopedia entries), a competing failure mode emerges: the model recognizes the domain, bypasses the retrieval protocol, and generates answers from parametric memory, producing catastrophic token expenditure even when the index is sound. This parametric memory competition dissociates the two cognitive operations that indexing combines: understanding content (where language models excel) and following navigational protocols (where they fail when understanding tempts them to shortcut). The result argues for a separation of concerns: use language models for index construction, where semantic understanding helps, and deterministic algorithms for index traversal, where it hurts.

*Pantea Karimi,Kimia Noorbakhsh,Mohammad Alizadeh,Hari Balakrishnan*

TL;DR: The paper introduces Engram, an agentic researcher architecture that overcomes LLM limitations in complex system heuristic design by using persistent archives and distilled research digests to enable long-horizon, multi-step exploration beyond a single context window.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Designing high-performance system heuristics requires creative, multi-step reasoning and coordinated changes, which traditional evolutionary methods and existing LLM agent frameworks handle poorly. Method: Engram structures exploration as a sequence of LLM-based agents that iteratively design, test, and analyze mechanisms. After each run, agents store code, logs, and results in a persistent Archive and distill key modeling insights into a concise Research Digest. New agents start with a fresh context window but load and build upon the Research Digest, effectively decoupling long-horizon reasoning from a single context window limitation.

Result: Engram outperforms baseline approaches across several domains, including multi-cloud multicast, LLM inference request routing, and optimization of KV cache reuse in databases handling natural language queries, indicating better heuristic design and system performance.

Conclusion: By separating long-horizon exploration from context window constraints through persistent Archives and Research Digests, Engram mitigates evolutionary neighborhood bias and coherence ceiling issues, enabling LLM-based agents to more effectively design complex system heuristics across diverse application domains.

Abstract: Designing high-performance system heuristics is a creative, iterative process requiring experts to form hypotheses and execute multi-step conceptual shifts. While Large Language Models (LLMs) show promise in automating this loop, they struggle with complex system problems due to two critical failure modes: evolutionary neighborhood bias and the coherence ceiling. Evolutionary methods often remain trapped in local optima by relying on scalar benchmark scores, failing when coordinated multi-step changes are required. Conversely, existing agentic frameworks suffer from context degradation over long horizons or fail to accumulate knowledge across independent runs.
  We present Engram, an agentic researcher architecture that addresses these limitations by decoupling long-horizon exploration from the constraints of a single context window. Engram organizes exploration into a sequence of agents that iteratively design, test, and analyze mechanisms. At the conclusion of each run, an agent stores code snapshots, logs, and results in a persistent Archive and distills high-level modeling insights into a compact, persistent Research Digest. Subsequent agents then begin with a fresh context window, reading the Research Digest to build on prior discoveries.
  We find that Engram exhibits superior performance across diverse domains including multi-cloud multicast, LLM inference request routing, and optimizing KV cache reuse in databases with natural language queries.

The nano model architecture provides linear scaling, sparse activation, selective untraining, and sub-20-second training cycles, resolving the traditional tension between capability and computational efficiency. A central contribution is the Unfireable Safety Kernel: an architecturally immutable safety boundary that cannot be disabled or circumvented by any system component, including its own self-improvement engine. This is not a social or ethical alignment statement; it is a technical framework ensuring human control persists as autonomy increases. Safety is an architectural constraint governing every operation, not a policy layer applied after the fact. We present formal alignment between ARYA's architecture and canonical world model requirements, and report summarizing its state-of-the-art performance across 6 of 9 competitive benchmarks head-to-head with GPT-5.2, Opus 4.6, and V-JEPA-2. The authors want a principled way for MLLMs to translate their multimodal understanding into accurate, low-level control actions in robotic benchmarks.

Method: The authors propose RoboAlign, a training framework for MLLMs targeted at VLA tasks. First, the model produces candidate action tokens using zero-shot natural-language-style reasoning. These action sequences are then refined using reinforcement learning, where the objective directly optimizes action accuracy and task success. Conclusion: RoboAlign provides a systematic way to align multimodal LLMs with low-level robot actions by refining zero-shot action reasoning using reinforcement learning. This bridges the gap between language understanding and control and leads to reliable, sizable improvements in VLA performance on both simulated and real-world tasks, even with very limited RL data. The framework suggests that RL-based alignment is a powerful complement to traditional supervised training for embodied AI based on MLLMs.

However, these approaches have been reported to result in unstable VLA performance, often yielding only marginal or even negative gains. In this paper, we propose a more systematic MLLM training framework RoboAlign that reliably improves VLA performance. Our key idea is to sample action tokens via zero-shot natural language reasoning and refines this reasoning using reinforcement learning (RL) to improve action accuracy. As a result, RoboAlign bridges the modality gap between language and low-level actions in MLLMs, and facilitate knowledge transfer from MLLM to VLA. To validate the effectiveness of RoboAlign, we train VLAs by adding a diffusion-based action head on top of an MLLM backbone and evaluate them on major robotics benchmarks. Remarkably, by performing RL-based alignment after SFT using less than 1\% of the data, RoboAlign achieves performance improvements of 17.5\%, 18.9\%, and 106.6\% over SFT baselines on LIBERO, CALVIN, and real-world environments, respectively.

### [149] [AgentHER: Hindsight Experience Replay for LLM Agent Trajectory Relabeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21357)
*Liang Ding*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgentHER repurposes failed LLM-agent trajectories by relabeling them with alternative goals, turning discarded failures into effective training data for agent finetuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM agents perform poorly on realistic tool-use and web navigation benchmarks, and most collected trajectories are failures that are thrown away. This wastes the majority of available experience and limits data efficiency for supervised fine-tuning and preference optimization. The authors are motivated to exploit these failed trajectories as an additional supervision signal, akin to how HER in reinforcement learning turns failed episodes into successes for alternative goals.

Method: They adapt Hindsight Experience Replay (HER) from RL to language-agent settings. AgentHER is a four-stage pipeline: (1) failure classification identifies unsuccessful trajectories; (2) outcome extraction summarizes what the agent actually achieved; (3) LLM-guided prompt relabeling with confidence gating rewrites the original instruction/goal into a new, achievable goal that matches the observed outcome, possibly using either rule-based heuristics or an LLM judge; and (4) data packaging converts these relabeled trajectories into clean training examples for SFT, DPO, and ShareGPT-style datasets. This converts many failed attempts on the original goal into successful demonstrations for alternative goals.

Result: On WebArena and ToolBench benchmarks, augmenting training with AgentHER-generated data yields 7.1–11.7 percentage point improvements over training only on successful trajectories, across four model families (GPT-4o, Qwen2.5-72B/7B, LLaMA-3.1-8B). AgentHER doubles data efficiency by matching the baseline’s performance with only 50% of the successful demos. The performance gains hold consistently across model sizes from 1.5B to 72B parameters (+5.8–9.2 points) and further improve under iterative deployment (+2.1 points with additional redeployment rounds). Human evaluation with multiple judges reports 97.7% precision in the relabeled trajectories, suggesting high quality of the augmented data.

Conclusion: Failed trajectories of LLM agents can be systematically recycled into high-quality training examples by relabeling them with alternative, actually achieved goals. AgentHER operationalizes this idea for language-based agents and demonstrates substantial performance and data-efficiency gains on web navigation and tool-use benchmarks across diverse model families and sizes. The work suggests that large repositories of agent failures are an underexploited supervision source and that HER-like ideas transfer effectively from RL to natural language and tool-using LLM agents.

Abstract: LLM agents fail on the majority of real-world tasks -- GPT-4o succeeds on fewer than 15% of WebArena navigation tasks and below 55% pass@1 on ToolBench (Zhou et al., 2024; Qin et al., 2024) -- yet every failed trajectory is routinely discarded, wasting the dominant source of collected experience. We introduce AgentHER, a framework that recovers this lost training signal by adapting the Hindsight Experience Replay (HER; Andrychowicz et al., 2017) principle to natural-language agent trajectories for offline data augmentation. The key insight is simple: a trajectory that fails goal A is often a correct demonstration for some achievable alternative goal B. AgentHER realises this idea through a four-stage pipeline -- failure classification, outcome extraction, LLM-guided prompt relabeling with confidence gating, and data packaging -- that converts discarded failures into high-quality SFT, DPO, and ShareGPT training data, with both zero-cost rule-based and LLM-judge implementations. On WebArena (Zhou et al., 2024) and ToolBench (Qin et al., 2024), AgentHER improves over success-only SFT by +7.1-11.7 pp across four model families (GPT-4o, Qwen2.5-72B/7B, LLaMA-3.1-8B), while achieving 2x data efficiency -- matching baseline performance with only 50% of successful demonstrations. Gains are consistent from 1.5B to 72B parameters (+5.8-9.2 pp) and compound under iterative redeployment (+2.1 pp over additional rounds). Human evaluation confirms 97.7% relabeling precision under multi-judge verification.

</details>


### [150] [AdaRubric: Task-Adaptive Rubrics for LLM Agent Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21362)
*Liang Ding*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces ADARUBRIC, a system that dynamically generates task-specific rubrics and dimension-aware preference data for training and evaluating LLM-based agents, achieving substantially better alignment with human judgments and improved agent performance without manual rubric engineering.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-as-Judge setups use static, hand-crafted rubrics that do not generalize across diverse agent tasks (e.g., code debugging vs. web navigation). These fixed rubrics miss task-critical dimensions, allow high scores in some areas to hide failures in others, and result in poor correlation with human judgments. There is a need for an evaluation and preference-generation framework that adapts to the task, reasons step-by-step, and preserves dimension-level failures when constructing training signals.

Method: ADARUBRIC automatically generates task-specific rubrics from task descriptions, decomposing evaluation into relevant dimensions (e.g., correctness, error handling, goal alignment, efficiency). It scores agent trajectories step-by-step, providing per-dimension feedback with associated confidence scores. The system then constructs preference pairs using a new DimensionAwareFilter that enforces a necessary condition: a trajectory cannot be preferred overall if it exhibits clear failure on any critical dimension, preventing strong performance on other dimensions from masking that failure. These rubric-driven preference pairs are used to train agents via methods like DPO and PPO, without human-crafted rubrics.

Result: On WebArena and ToolBench benchmarks, ADARUBRIC’s automatic rubrics achieve a Pearson correlation of 0.79 with human ratings, which is a +0.16 improvement over the best static rubric baseline, and reach deployment-grade reliability with Krippendorff’s alpha of 0.83. Agents trained via DPO on ADARUBRIC-generated preference pairs outperform those trained with Prometheus-generated preferences by 6.8 to 8.5 percentage points in task success across three benchmarks. The benefits transfer to SWE-bench code repair, where task success improves by 4.9 percentage points, and PPO training converges faster, gaining 6.6 percentage points at 5K steps, all without manual rubric engineering.

Conclusion: Dynamic, task-specific rubrics combined with dimension-aware preference filtering substantially improve the reliability of LLM-as-Judge evaluations and yield better, more sample-efficient training signals for agentic LLMs. ADARUBRIC demonstrates that evaluation rubrics can be automatically induced from task descriptions and used to train stronger, more general agents without bespoke, hand-engineered evaluation criteria.

Abstract: LLM-as-Judge evaluation fails agent tasks because a fixed rubric cannot capture what matters for this task: code debugging demands Correctness and Error Handling; web navigation demands Goal Alignment and Action Efficiency. We present ADARUBRIC, which closes this gap by generating task-specific evaluation rubrics on the fly from task descriptions, scoring trajectories step-by-step with confidence-weighted per-dimension feedback, and filtering preference pairs with the novel DimensionAwareFilter - a provably necessary condition for preventing high-scoring dimensions from masking dimension-level failures. On WebArena and ToolBench, ADARUBRIC achieves Pearson r=0.79 human correlation (+0.16 over the best static baseline) with deployment-grade reliability (Krippendorff's $α$=0.83). DPO agents trained on ADARUBRIC preference pairs gain +6.8 to +8.5 pp task success over Prometheus across three benchmarks; gains transfer to SWE-bench code repair (+4.9 pp) and accelerate PPO convergence by +6.6 pp at 5K steps - both without any rubric engineering. Code: https://github.com/alphadl/AdaRubrics.
