### [151] [A transformer architecture alteration to incentivise externalised reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21376)
*Elizabeth Pavlova,Mariia Koroliuk,Karthik Viswanathan,Cameron Tice,Edward James Young,Puria Radmard*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They add early-exit mechanisms to transformers and use RL so the model learns to stop computation early on easy tokens, cutting unnecessary compute while preserving reasoning quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning-oriented LLMs often run their full depth for every token, even when many tokens are easy to predict. This wastes computation and may give the model more internal computation than needed, which can enable complex long-horizon internal planning. The authors want a way to adaptively allocate depth—using full computation only where it is actually needed—both to save compute and potentially limit excess internal reasoning capacity on simple steps.

Method: They modify a standard transformer by inserting early-exit heads at intermediate layers. During training, the model is taught to sometimes stop (exit) at a shallow layer if the next-token prediction is already good enough. After standard training plus a calibration phase, they apply a reinforcement learning procedure that rewards exiting as early as possible while keeping task performance high. The objective balances token-level accuracy with computational cost, training the model to choose, per token, how many layers to use.

Result: On small reasoning-focused models, they show preliminary empirical evidence that the model learns to vary the computation per token. Easy tokens tend to exit at earlier layers, while harder tokens still use more depth. Overall, this adaptivity reduces average per-token compute without significantly degrading performance on the evaluated reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: Early-exit architectures combined with RL-based incentives can train LLMs to be more compute-efficient, token-adaptive reasoners. If scaled up, this approach could reduce unnecessary internal computation that might otherwise enable complex non-myopic planning, concentrating deep computation only on genuinely hard prediction steps while saving compute on easy ones.

Abstract: We propose a new architectural change, and post-training pipeline, for making LLMs more verbose reasoners by teaching a model to truncate forward passes early. We augment an existing transformer architecture with an early-exit mechanism at intermediate layers and train the model to exit at shallower layers when the next token can be predicted without deep computation. After a calibration stage, we incentivise the model to exit as early as possible while maintaining task performance using reinforcement learning. We provide preliminary results to this effect for small reasoning models, showing that they learn to adaptively reduce computations across tokens. We predict that, applied at the right scale, our approach can minimise the amount of excess computation that reasoning models have at their disposal to perform non-myopic planning using their internal activations, reserving this only for difficult-to-predict tokens.

### [152] [PivotRL: High Accuracy Agentic Post-Training at Low Compute Cost](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21383)
*Junkeun Yi,Damon Mosk-Aoyama,Baihe Huang,Ritu Gala,Charles Wang,Sugam Dipak Devare,Khushi Bhardwaj,Abhibha Gupta,Oleksii Kuchaiev,Jiantao Jiao,Jian Zhang,Venkat Srinivasan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PivotRL improves long-horizon agent post-training by combining the compute efficiency of supervised fine-tuning with the out-of-domain robustness of end-to-end RL, using local rollouts around SFT data and relaxed reward matching.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing post-training methods for long-horizon agents face a tradeoff: supervised fine-tuning is cheap but leads to poor out-of-domain generalization, while end-to-end reinforcement learning generalizes better but is very expensive due to many on-policy rollouts. There is a need for a method that keeps compute low but preserves OOD capabilities.

Method: PivotRL operates on existing SFT trajectories. It performs local on-policy rollouts around these trajectories to discover pivot states—intermediate turns where sampled actions differ significantly in their outcomes—and uses them for learning. Instead of requiring exact string matches to SFT demonstrations, it assigns rewards to functionally equivalent actions, thereby broadening the supervision signal. Theoretical analysis shows these mechanisms create strong learning signals with high natural gradient norm while minimally disturbing the policy’s relative probabilities on actions not related to the training tasks.

Result: Across four agentic domains, PivotRL improves in-domain accuracy by 4.17 percentage points over standard SFT on the same data and improves OOD accuracy in non-agentic tasks by 10.04 percentage points. On agentic coding tasks, it reaches performance comparable to full end-to-end RL while using four times fewer rollout turns. The technique has been adopted in production for NVIDIA’s Nemotron-3-Super-120B-A12B model.

Conclusion: PivotRL offers a practical, compute-efficient alternative to end-to-end RL for post-training long-horizon agents, retaining or improving in-domain performance while significantly enhancing out-of-domain robustness. Its theoretical grounding and empirical results, along with industrial adoption, suggest it is a strong candidate for scalable agentic post-training.

Abstract: Post-training for long-horizon agentic tasks has a tension between compute efficiency and generalization. While supervised fine-tuning (SFT) is compute efficient, it often suffers from out-of-domain (OOD) degradation. Conversely, end-to-end reinforcement learning (E2E RL) preserves OOD capabilities, but incurs high compute costs due to many turns of on-policy rollout. We introduce PivotRL, a novel framework that operates on existing SFT trajectories to combine the compute efficiency of SFT with the OOD accuracy of E2E RL. PivotRL relies on two key mechanisms: first, it executes local, on-policy rollouts and filters for pivots: informative intermediate turns where sampled actions exhibit high variance in outcomes; second, it utilizes rewards for functional-equivalent actions rather than demanding strict string matching with the SFT data demonstration. We theoretically show that these mechanisms incentivize strong learning signals with high natural gradient norm, while maximally preserving policy probability ordering on actions unrelated to training tasks. In comparison to standard SFT on identical data, we demonstrate that PivotRL achieves +4.17% higher in-domain accuracy on average across four agentic domains, and +10.04% higher OOD accuracy in non-agentic tasks. Notably, on agentic coding tasks, PivotRL achieves competitive accuracy with E2E RL with 4x fewer rollout turns. PivotRL is adopted by NVIDIA's Nemotron-3-Super-120B-A12B, acting as the workhorse in production-scale agentic post-training.

*Johnathan Sun,Andrew Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper uses activation steering in large language models to construct and manipulate high-level behavioral personas (like altruism and forgiveness) in game-theoretic settings, showing these personas systematically alter strategic choices and explanations.

  <summary>Details</summary>

These vectors are then injected into the model’s activations during play in canonical game-theoretic tasks, and both the resulting strategic choices and natural-language justifications are evaluated to see how the persona steering changes behavior.

Result: Activation steering via persona vectors systematically shifts models’ strategic choices in canonical games as well as the language used to justify those choices. They also find that steered rhetoric and actual strategic behavior can diverge, and that vectors affecting the model’s own behavior differ (at least partially) from vectors that control its expectations about other agents’ behavior.

Conclusion: Persona vectors obtained through activation steering provide a promising mechanistic tool for probing and controlling high-level behavioral traits of LLMs in strategic environments, though the observed divergence between rhetoric and strategy and the partial separation between self-behavior and expectation vectors highlight that these traits are multifaceted and not captured by a single dimension.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed as autonomous decision-makers in strategic settings, yet we have limited tools for understanding their high-level behavioral traits. We use activation steering methods in game-theoretic settings, constructing persona vectors for altruism, forgiveness, and expectations of others by contrastive activation addition. Evaluating on canonical games, we find that activation steering systematically shifts both quantitative strategic choices and natural-language justifications. However, we also observe that rhetoric and strategy can diverge under steering. In addition, vectors for self-behavior and expectations of others are partially distinct. Our results suggest that persona vectors offer a promising mechanistic handle on high-level traits in strategic environments.

### [154] [The Myhill-Nerode Theorem for Bounded Interaction: Canonical Abstractions via Agent-Bounded Indistinguishability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21399)
*Anthony T. Nixon*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper defines a canonical way to ‘quotient’ or merge histories in a POMDP based on what a bounded (finite-state) agent can actually distinguish, yielding a decision-sufficient compressed representation of experience with theoretical guarantees and empirical validation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In partially observable environments (POMDPs), the full history of observations and actions is often intractably large, but real agents are capacity-limited and cannot distinguish all possible histories. We need a principled, canonical way to compress or quotients histories according to what bounded agents can actually tell apart, while preserving decision quality for relevant objectives. Existing notions like belief states or bisimulation either assume unbounded reasoning, focus on latent states, or lack a canonical minimal form tied to a bounded controller class.

Method: The authors formalise capacity limits via a fixed family of finite-state controllers (probes) in finite POMDPs. They define a closed-loop Wasserstein pseudometric on observation histories induced by the distinguishability of histories under these probes, and then construct a probe-exact quotient that merges any two histories that no controller in the family can distinguish. They prove this quotient is canonical, minimal, and unique in an analogue of the Myhill-Nerode theorem, and they analyse both fully clock-aware probes and a tractable coarsening. Theoretical results include decision-sufficiency for observation/action-based objectives and Lipschitz-type approximation bounds for latent-state rewards. They then run deterministic-stationary experiments and case studies on benchmark POMDPs (Tiger, GridWorld, RockSample) to study approximation behaviour and runtime, using exact small cases to quantify gaps and larger cases for empirical exploration.

Result: They obtain a canonical, minimal, and unique quotient over observation histories for a given family of finite-state controllers, characterized via a Wasserstein pseudometric. For clock-aware probes, this quotient is exactly sufficient for optimal decision-making on objectives that depend only on observations and actions. For objectives involving latent-state rewards, they show an observation-Lipschitz approximation bound that quantifies the error from using the quotient. Experimentally, they demonstrate that a scalable, coarser version of the quotient tracks the exact construction closely on small POMDPs, and they provide empirical evidence on larger problems (Tiger, GridWorld, RockSample) about approximation quality and computational costs, supported by additional stress tests in supplementary material.

Conclusion: The paper introduces a bounded-interaction analogue of the Myhill-Nerode theorem for finite POMDPs, showing that capacity-limited agents naturally induce a canonical quotient over histories that captures exactly what such agents can distinguish. This quotient serves as a decision-sufficient compressed representation for a broad class of objectives and admits quantitative approximation guarantees for latent-state rewards. Empirical studies on standard benchmarks indicate that a practical coarsening of the quotient is tractable and closely aligned with the exact construction on smaller problems, suggesting that the framework can function both as a theoretical tool and as a practical diagnostic for designing and evaluating bounded agents in partially observable environments.

Abstract: Any capacity-limited observer induces a canonical quotient on its environment: two situations that no bounded agent can distinguish are, for that agent, the same. We formalise this for finite POMDPs. A fixed probe family of finite-state controllers induces a closed-loop Wasserstein pseudometric on observation histories and a probe-exact quotient merging histories that no controller in the family can distinguish. The quotient is canonical, minimal, and unique-a bounded-interaction analogue of the Myhill-Nerode theorem. For clock-aware probes, it is exactly decision-sufficient for objectives that depend only on the agent's observations and actions; for latent-state rewards, we use an observation-Lipschitz approximation bound. The main theorem object is the clock-aware quotient; scalable deterministic-stationary experiments study a tractable coarsening with gap measured on small exact cases and explored empirically at larger scale. We validate theorem-level claims on Tiger and GridWorld. We also report operational case studies on Tiger, GridWorld, and RockSample as exploratory diagnostics of approximation behavior and runtime, not as theorem-facing evidence when no exact cross-family certificate is available; heavier stress tests are archived in the appendix and artifact package.

</details>


### [155] [Silent Commitment Failure in Instruction-Tuned Language Models: Evidence of Governability Divergence Across Architectures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21415)
*Gregory M. Ruddell*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that many large language models make confident but undetectable mistakes as agents, and introduces a framework (governability and a Detection–Correction matrix) to categorize and measure how detectable and correctable their errors are.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used as autonomous agents with tools and real-world impact, existing safety setups assume that the system (or a wrapper) can notice when the model is likely wrong and intervene. The authors suspect this assumption is flawed: some models might produce incorrect but confident answers with no internal indications of conflict, making them hard to monitor or correct in time. They want to empirically test how often such failures occur, how they differ across models, and whether standard metrics like benchmark accuracy predict how governable a model is.

Method: The authors define a new concept, governability, capturing how easily a model’s errors can be detected pre‑commitment and corrected once detected. They study six models across twelve reasoning-heavy domains, focusing on instruction-following models that can be probed for internal conflict signals. They measure: (1) conflict detection—whether the model emits any signal (e.g., inconsistencies, spikes in alternative answers) before its final answer; (2) correction capacity—whether scaffolds or interventions can steer the model to a correct answer once a conflict is detected; and (3) how these behaviors change (or not) across architectures and fine-tuning setups. They run a 2×2 experiment varying architecture and fine-tuning, analyze spike ratios as a proxy for detection signal strength, and evaluate the impact of identical governance scaffolds across models. They then organize model-task pairs into a Detection and Correction Matrix with four regimes based on whether detection and correction are possible or not.

Result: Across the six tested models and twelve reasoning domains, two of the three instruction-following models frequently produced "silent commitment failures": wrong but fluent and confident answers with no detectable conflict signal before the answer was committed. The third instruction-following model did emit a detectable conflict signal on average 57 tokens before final output when decoded greedily. Benchmark accuracy did not correlate with governability—models with similar or better accuracy could still be much less governable. Correction capacity turned out to be largely independent of detection capacity, and the same governance scaffolds had opposite effects on different models. In a 2×2 experiment, architectural differences produced a 52× difference in spike ratio (governability signal), while fine-tuning only changed this ratio by about ±0.32×, suggesting that governability is strongly determined during pretraining rather than fine-tuning.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the standard assumption that model errors are reliably detectable at runtime is invalid for many current LLMs, especially some instruction-following variants. Governability is a distinct property not captured by benchmark accuracy and is largely set by pretraining, with fine-tuning having limited effect. Because error detection and correction are partially independent, safety and control tools cannot be assumed to transfer across models, and identical governance scaffolds may help some models while harming others. The authors propose the Detection and Correction Matrix to classify model-task pairs into four regimes—Governable (detectable & correctable), Monitor Only (detectable but not correctable), Steer Blind (not detectable but correctable given external signal), and Ungovernable (neither detectable nor correctable)—to guide deployment and monitoring strategies for LLM agents.

Abstract: As large language models are deployed as autonomous agents with tool execution privileges, a critical assumption underpins their security architecture: that model errors are detectable at runtime. We present empirical evidence that this assumption fails for two of three instruction-following models evaluable for conflict detection. We introduce governability -- the degree to which a model's errors are detectable before output commitment and correctable once detected -- and demonstrate it varies dramatically across models. In six models across twelve reasoning domains, two of three instruction-following models exhibited silent commitment failure: confident, fluent, incorrect output with zero warning signal. The remaining model produced a detectable conflict signal 57 tokens before commitment under greedy decoding. We show benchmark accuracy does not predict governability, correction capacity varies independently of detection, and identical governance scaffolds produce opposite effects across models. A 2x2 experiment shows a 52x difference in spike ratio between architectures but only +/-0.32x variation from fine-tuning, suggesting governability is fixed at pretraining. We propose a Detection and Correction Matrix classifying model-task combinations into four regimes: Governable, Monitor Only, Steer Blind, and Ungovernable.

This is a theoretical/analytical approach rather than an empirical measurement study.

Result: Using the A-U diffusion framework, the paper forecasts likely trajectories for the structure of the GAI industry—such as phases of experimentation, consolidation, and optimization—and links these phases to corresponding changes in environmental impact. Conclusion: The authors conclude that generative AI will not become environmentally harmless, but its long‑term impact is likely more moderate than some current alarming predictions, provided that efficiency‑oriented innovation and certain business models prevail. They emphasize that the dominant business model (e.g., centralized large models vs. more distributed or specialized solutions) will be a key determinant of the overall environmental footprint, and that policy and market choices shaping industry structure will therefore strongly influence GAI’s eventual sustainability profile.

Abstract: The rise of generative artificial intelligence (GAI) has led to alarming predictions about its environmental impact. However, these predictions often overlook the fact that the diffusion of innovation is accompanied by the evolution of products and the optimization of their performance, primarily for economic reasons. This can also reduce their environmental impact. By analyzing the GAI ecosystem using the classic A-U innovation diffusion model, we can forecast this industry's structure and how its environmental impact will evolve. While GAI will never be green, its impact may not be as problematic as is sometimes claimed. However, this depends on which business model becomes dominant.

However, because most LLMs are trained on public domain corpora, directly applying them to real-world software development often yields low success rates, as these scenarios frequently require domain-specific knowledge. In particular, domain-specific tasks usually demand highly specialized solutions, which are often underrepresented or entirely absent in the training data of generic LLMs. To address this challenge, we propose DomAgent, an autonomous coding agent that bridges this gap by enabling LLMs to generate domain-adapted code through structured reasoning and targeted retrieval. A core component of DomAgent is DomRetriever, a novel retrieval module that emulates how humans learn domain-specific knowledge, by combining conceptual understanding with experiential examples. It dynamically integrates top-down knowledge-graph reasoning with bottom-up case-based reasoning, enabling iterative retrieval and synthesis of structured knowledge and representative cases to ensure contextual relevance and broad task coverage. ### [158] [Behavioural feasible set: Value alignment constraints on AI decision support](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21435)
*Taejin Park*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that when organisations use commercial AI decision-support systems, they silently inherit the vendor’s embedded value judgements, which strongly limit what recommendations the AI can produce and what trade-offs remain negotiable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Organisations increasingly rely on commercial AI systems for high-stakes decision support (e.g., hiring, lending, resource allocation). These systems arrive pre-aligned and fine-tuned by vendors according to their own safety, brand, and normative assumptions. Existing governance discussions focus on whether AI can assist decisions and how to prompt or oversee it, but pay less attention to the structural constraints imposed by vendor alignment. The author aims to clarify how these embedded constraints shape the space of possible recommendations and thereby constrain organisational autonomy and stakeholder governance, even when users attempt to prompt the system differently.

Method: The paper introduces the concept of a “behavioural feasible set”: the set of all recommendations an AI system can produce under vendor-imposed alignment constraints. It then analytically characterises thresholds where organisational requirements and preferences exceed the flexibility of this feasible set. Empirically, the author runs scenario-based experiments using (1) binary decision scenarios and (2) multi-stakeholder ranking tasks. They compare pre-alignment and post-alignment versions of an open-weight model to isolate the effect of alignment. They also benchmark leading commercial models to assess whether these exhibit similar or greater rigidity. Across these tasks, the experiments measure how much the system’s recommendations can be shifted by changing prompts to reflect different contextual or stakeholder priorities.

Result: The analysis shows that vendor alignment materially shrinks the behavioural feasible set: after alignment, the AI’s recommendations become substantially less adjustable in response to legitimate contextual changes or stakeholder preferences. Compared to its pre-alignment variant, the aligned open-weight model exhibits greater rigidity in its outputs. Leading commercial models demonstrate similar or even stronger rigidity. In multi-stakeholder ranking tasks, alignment does not neutralise stakeholder value conflicts; instead, it implicitly reweights stakeholder priorities in a direction determined by the vendor. As a result, organisational attempts to prompt for different value trade-offs are systematically constrained by these upstream alignment decisions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that organisational governance challenges with AI are not primarily about crafting better prompts or interfaces, but about the structural limits imposed by vendor alignment. By choosing a particular commercial AI vendor, organisations effectively adopt a pre-packaged set of value orientations and trade-off priorities that may not be transparent or renegotiable. This means that some stakeholder priorities become structurally embedded while others are rendered non-negotiable, regardless of downstream use or oversight. Consequently, responsible AI governance must treat vendor selection and alignment policies as core normative choices, not mere technical details, and should develop mechanisms to surface, audit, and potentially contest these embedded value constraints.

Abstract: When organisations adopt commercial AI systems for decision support, they inherit value judgements embedded by vendors that are neither transparent nor renegotiable. The governance puzzle is not whether AI can support decisions but which recommendations the system can actually produce given how its vendor has configured it. I formalise this as a behavioural feasible set, the range of recommendations reachable under vendor-imposed alignment constraints, and characterise diagnostic thresholds for when organisational requirements exceed the system's flexibility. In scenario-based experiments using binary decision scenarios and multi-stakeholder ranking tasks, I show that alignment materially compresses this set. Comparing pre- and post-alignment variants of an open-weight model isolates the mechanism: alignment makes the system substantially less able to shift its recommendation even under legitimate contextual pressure. Leading commercial models exhibit comparable or greater rigidity. In multi-stakeholder tasks, alignment shifts implied stakeholder priorities rather than neutralising them, meaning organisations adopt embedded value orientations set upstream by the vendor. Organisations thus face a governance problem that better prompting cannot resolve: selecting a vendor partially determines which trade-offs remain negotiable and which stakeholder priorities are structurally embedded.

</details>


### [159] [Safety as Computation: Certified Answer Reuse via Capability Closure in Task-Oriented Dialogue](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21448)
*Cosimo Spera*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a new way to speed up and reuse answers in task-oriented dialogue systems by turning safety certification into a core computation that pre-computes all safe follow-up answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current dialogue systems repeatedly recompute answers using retrieval or generation at every turn, even when responses are logically implied by previous state. This is inefficient and misses opportunities to reuse already safe and derivable answers.

Method: Reframe capability-based task-oriented dialogue as computing a fixed-point closure cl(At) during the safety certification phase. Implement a Certified Answer Store (CAS) with Pre-Answer Blocks (PAB) that, at each certified turn, materializes all derivable follow-up answers along with minimal provenance witnesses. Later queries are answered by fast formal containment checks against this store instead of new retrieval/generation.

Result: The system can answer subsequent user queries in sub-millisecond time by reusing pre-certified answers from CAS, effectively avoiding redundant computation while maintaining safety guarantees.

Conclusion: By making safety certification a computational primitive and caching its closure as certified answers, task-oriented dialogue systems can dramatically improve efficiency and latency, answering many future queries via fast reuse instead of repeated retrieval or generation.

Abstract: We introduce a new paradigm for task-oriented dialogue systems: safety certification as a computational primitive for answer reuse. Current systems treat each turn independently, recomputing answers via retrieval or generation even when they are already derivable from prior state. We show that in capability-based systems, the safety certification step computes a fixed-point closure cl(At) that already contains every answer reachable from the current configuration. We operationalize this insight with a Certified Answer Store (CAS) augmented by Pre-Answer Blocks (PAB): at each certified turn, the system materializes all derivable follow-up answers together with minimal provenance witnesses. Subsequent queries are answered in sub-millisecond time via formal containment checks, eliminating redundant retrieval and generation.

Across six energy tickers, only a few associations survive all checks, while renewables show aspect and horizon specific responses. While not establishing causality, the framework provides statistically robust, directionally interpretable signals, with limited sample size (six stocks, one quarter) constraining generalizability and framing this work as a methodological proof of concept.

They either use generic, static reasoning nodes that lack domain expertise or generate nodes on-the-fly, forcing a single orchestrator to both invent domain-specific logic and design the overall agent topology. This coupling limits performance because LLMs have internal knowledge constraints and face a difficult joint optimization problem.

Method: Unified-MAS decouples node implementation from system orchestration through offline node synthesis. It has two main stages: (1) Search-Based Node Generation, which retrieves external open-world knowledge to build specialized node blueprints beyond the LLM’s internal knowledge; and (2) Reward-Based Node Optimization, which iteratively refines the internal logic of bottleneck nodes using a perplexity-guided reward signal. These optimized nodes are then plugged into existing automatic-MAS frameworks.

Result: Across four specialized domains, plugging Unified-MAS into four different Automatic-MAS baselines improves the performance–cost trade-off, with performance gains up to 14.2% while also significantly reducing computation or API costs. Additional experiments show that Unified-MAS is robust to the choice of designer LLM and also improves performance on more standard tasks like mathematical reasoning.

Conclusion: By separating node design from topology control and leveraging external knowledge plus reward-based refinement, Unified-MAS overcomes key limitations of existing automatic MAS frameworks in knowledge-intensive domains. It yields more specialized and efficient agent nodes, improves accuracy and cost-effectiveness across multiple domains and tasks, and generalizes across different underlying LLM designers.

Abstract: Automatic Multi-Agent Systems (MAS) generation has emerged as a promising paradigm for solving complex reasoning tasks. However, existing frameworks are fundamentally bottlenecked when applied to knowledge-intensive domains (e.g., healthcare and law). They either rely on a static library of general nodes like Chain-of-Thought, which lack specialized expertise, or attempt to generate nodes on the fly. In the latter case, the orchestrator is not only bound by its internal knowledge limits but must also simultaneously generate domain-specific logic and optimize high-level topology, leading to a severe architectural coupling that degrades overall system efficacy. To bridge this gap, we propose Unified-MAS that decouples granular node implementation from topological orchestration via offline node synthesis. Unified-MAS operates in two stages: (1) Search-Based Node Generation retrieves external open-world knowledge to synthesize specialized node blueprints, overcoming the internal knowledge limits of LLMs; and (2) Reward-Based Node Optimization utilizes a perplexity-guided reward to iteratively enhance the internal logic of bottleneck nodes. Extensive experiments across four specialized domains demonstrate that integrating Unified-MAS into four Automatic-MAS baselines yields a better performance-cost trade-off, achieving up to a 14.2% gain while significantly reducing costs. Further analysis reveals its robustness across different designer LLMs and its effectiveness on conventional tasks such as mathematical reasoning.

### [162] [Counterfactual Credit Policy Optimization for Multi-Agent Collaboration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21563)
*Zhongyi Li,Wan Tian,Yikun Ban,Jinju Chen,Huiming Zhang,Yang Liu,Fuzhen Zhuang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose CCPO, a counterfactual credit-assignment method for collaborative multi-agent LLMs that improves reinforcement learning stability and performance by estimating each agent’s marginal contribution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In collaborative multi-agent LLM systems, reinforcement learning typically uses a shared global reward, which makes it hard to tell how much each agent contributed to success or failure. This poor credit assignment increases gradient variance, encourages some agents to free-ride on others’ work, and weakens learning, especially on complex reasoning tasks where different roles (e.g., thinking, reasoning, voting) interact.

Method: They introduce Counterfactual Credit Policy Optimization (CCPO), which builds agent-specific learning signals via counterfactual trajectories: for each agent, they simulate what the outcome would have been if that agent’s contribution were removed, and use the difference as that agent’s marginal contribution. This yields role-sensitive advantages for policy gradient optimization. They further add a global-history-aware normalization scheme that uses statistics from global rollouts to normalize and stabilize these advantages under heterogeneous tasks and data distributions. They test CCPO on two collaboration structures: (1) a sequential Think–Reason dyad and (2) multi-agent voting.

Result: On mathematical and logical reasoning benchmarks, CCPO reduces free-riding behavior and outperforms strong existing multi-agent RL baselines in collaborative LLM training, showing more precise and effective credit assignment. The method works across the considered collaboration topologies (sequential dyad and voting).

Conclusion: Counterfactual, agent-specific credit assignment via CCPO leads to more stable and effective reinforcement learning for collaborative multi-agent LLMs than global-reward approaches. By estimating each agent’s marginal contribution and normalizing advantages with global rollout history, CCPO produces finer-grained credit, mitigates free-riding, and improves reasoning performance on challenging benchmarks.

Abstract: Collaborative multi-agent large language models (LLMs) can solve complex reasoning tasks by decomposing roles and aggregating diverse hypotheses. Yet, reinforcement learning (RL) for such systems is often undermined by credit assignment: a shared global reward obscures individual contributions, inflating update variance and encouraging free-riding. We introduce Counterfactual Credit Policy Optimization (CCPO), a framework that assigns agent-specific learning signals by estimating each agent's marginal contribution through counterfactual trajectories. CCPO builds dynamic counterfactual baselines that simulate outcomes with an agent's contribution removed, yielding role-sensitive advantages for policy optimization. To further improve stability under heterogeneous tasks and data distributions, we propose a global-history-aware normalization scheme that calibrates advantages using global rollout statistics. We evaluate CCPO on two collaboration topologies: a sequential Think--Reason dyad and multi-agent voting. Across mathematical and logical reasoning benchmarks, CCPO mitigates free-riding and outperforms strong multi-agent RL baselines, yielding finer-grained and more effective credit assignment for collaborative LLM training. Our code is available at https://github.com/bhai114/ccpo.

It yields better performance and more reliable improvements even when reward signals are noisy, indicating enhanced robustness and stability during training compared with standard optimization procedures.

Conclusion: Structuring multi-agent LLM collaboration into explicit answer, critique, and rewrite phases enables clearer credit assignment between agents, and combining this with an adaptive robust estimator for rewards leads to more stable and effective multi-agent reinforcement learning. The approach improves reasoning performance and training robustness under noisy reward conditions, helping to prevent optimization failures in collaborative reasoning tasks.

Abstract: Multi-agent collaboration has emerged as a powerful paradigm for enhancing the reasoning capabilities of large language models, yet it suffers from interaction-level ambiguity that blurs generation, critique, and revision, making credit assignment across agents difficult. Moreover, policy optimization in this setting is vulnerable to heavy-tailed and noisy rewards, which can bias advantage estimation and trigger unstable or even divergent training. To address both issues, we propose a robust multi-agent reinforcement learning framework for collaborative reasoning, consisting of two components: Dual-Agent Answer-Critique-Rewrite (DACR) and an Adaptive Robust Estimator (ARE). DACR decomposes reasoning into a structured three-stage pipeline: answer, critique, and rewrite, while enabling explicit attribution of each agent's marginal contribution to its partner's performance. ARE provides robust estimation of batch experience means during multi-agent policy optimization. Across mathematical reasoning and embodied intelligence benchmarks, even under noisy rewards, our method consistently outperforms the baseline in both homogeneous and heterogeneous settings. These results indicate stronger robustness to reward noise and more stable training dynamics, effectively preventing optimization failures caused by noisy reward signals.

Our benchmarking results reveal that mental navigation capability does not naturally emerge from standard pre-training. Frontier MLLMs struggle profoundly with zero-shot structured spatial representation, and their planning accuracy decays precipitously over extended horizons. To overcome this, we propose \textbf{NavMind}, a reasoning model that internalizes mental navigation using explicit, fine-grained cognitive maps as learnable intermediate representations. Through a difficulty-stratified progressive supervised fine-tuning paradigm, NavMind effectively bridges the gap between raw perception and structured planning. Experiments demonstrate that NavMind achieves superior mental navigation capabilities, significantly outperforming frontier commercial and spatial MLLMs.

### [166] [INTRYGUE: Induction-Aware Entropy Gating for Reliable RAG Uncertainty Estimation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21607)

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that standard entropy-based uncertainty measures can be misleading in retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) because of internal model mechanisms, and introduces INTRYGUE, a gating method that uses induction-head activity to correct entropy-based uncertainty for better hallucination detection.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Retrieval-augmented generation improves factuality but still suffers from hallucinations, so accurate uncertainty quantification is important for knowing when to trust model outputs. However, existing entropy-based UQ methods often misfire in RAG, flagging correct, context-grounded answers as uncertain. The authors aim to understand the mechanistic reason for this failure and design a UQ method that accounts for how models internally use retrieved context.

Method: The authors perform a mechanistic analysis of transformer internals in RAG settings, focusing on induction heads and "entropy neurons" that drive predictive entropy. They identify a paradoxical interaction where induction heads that correctly copy answers also activate entropy neurons, spuriously increasing entropy. Based on this insight, they propose INTRYGUE, which monitors induction-head activation patterns and gates or adjusts the predictive entropy signal accordingly. They then evaluate INTRYGUE against standard UQ baselines across four RAG benchmarks and six open-source LLMs ranging from 4B to 13B parameters.

Result: INTRYGUE consistently matches or outperforms a variety of existing uncertainty quantification baselines for hallucination detection in RAG across the tested benchmarks and model sizes. It reduces cases where the model reports high uncertainty on correct, context-grounded answers, improving the correlation between uncertainty estimates and actual errors.

Conclusion: The work concludes that hallucination detection in retrieval-augmented generation benefits from leveraging mechanistic interpretability signals—specifically, induction-head activity—alongside traditional predictive uncertainty. Standard entropy-based UQ can be systematically biased in RAG because of internal feature interactions, and mechanistically-informed methods like INTRYGUE offer more reliable uncertainty estimates for deciding when to trust LLM outputs.

Abstract: While retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) significantly improves the factual reliability of LLMs, it does not eliminate hallucinations, so robust uncertainty quantification (UQ) remains essential. In this paper, we reveal that standard entropy-based UQ methods often fail in RAG settings due to a mechanistic paradox. An internal "tug-of-war" inherent to context utilization appears: while induction heads promote grounded responses by copying the correct answer, they collaterally trigger the previously established "entropy neurons". This interaction inflates predictive entropy, causing the model to signal false uncertainty on accurate outputs. To address this, we propose INTRYGUE (Induction-Aware Entropy Gating for Uncertainty Estimation), a mechanistically grounded method that gates predictive entropy based on the activation patterns of induction heads. Evaluated across four RAG benchmarks and six open-source LLMs (4B to 13B parameters), INTRYGUE consistently matches or outperforms a wide range of UQ baselines. Our findings demonstrate that hallucination detection in RAG benefits from combining predictive uncertainty with interpretable, internal signals of context utilization.

### [168] [Silicon Bureaucracy and AI Test-Oriented Education: Contamination Sensitivity and Score Confidence in LLM Benchmarks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21636)
*Yiliang Song,Hongjun An,Jiangan Chen,Xuanchen Yan,Huan Song,Jiawei Shao,Xuelong Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that current LLM benchmark scores can be misleading due to training data contamination and proposes an audit framework that tests how sensitive benchmark performance is to noisy perturbations, revealing that similar scores may imply very different levels of true generalization and confidence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Public benchmarks determine how LLMs are ranked and deployed, but they assume that benchmark scores reflect genuine generalization. With modern large-scale training, it is increasingly hard to guarantee that benchmark items have not appeared in training data or in semantically similar forms. This raises the concern that models may exhibit exam-oriented, contamination-driven competence rather than robust, principled capabilities. The authors want a way to distinguish models that truly generalize from those that exploit memorized benchmark artifacts, and to quantify how trustworthy benchmark scores really are.

Method: The authors design an audit framework that measures contamination sensitivity and score confidence for LLM benchmarks. They use a router–worker setup: a router model receives benchmark items that have been subjected to different conditions and then forwards them to a worker model for solving. The key manipulation is to compare a clean-control condition (original benchmark questions) with several noisy conditions in which benchmark problems are systematically deleted, rewritten, or otherwise perturbed before being sent downstream. By checking whether noisy variants systematically outperform the clean-control baseline, they infer whether models are relying on contamination-related cues that can be reassembled even after perturbations.

Result: Across multiple LLMs, the authors find that noisy conditions often yield performance above the clean-control baseline, but the extent and pattern of gains vary by model. This indicates that even when benchmark questions are perturbed, models can still pick up and recombine benchmark-related cues, reactivating contamination-related memory. The observed above-baseline gains under noisy perturbations suggest that benchmark performance is not purely reflecting generalization to unseen problems but is, at least partly, influenced by exposure-related artifacts in training data.

Conclusion: The study concludes that many benchmark scores for LLMs are more fragile and ambiguous than they appear: similar headline scores may correspond to very different levels of genuine generalization and confidence. Instead of discarding benchmarks, the authors recommend augmenting benchmark-based evaluation with explicit audits of contamination sensitivity and score confidence. Such audits can help distinguish exam-oriented, contamination-driven competence from deeper, principled capability and provide a more nuanced, trustworthy interpretation of benchmark results.

Abstract: Public benchmarks increasingly govern how large language models (LLMs) are ranked, selected, and deployed. We frame this benchmark-centered regime as Silicon Bureaucracy and AI Test-Oriented Education, and argue that it rests on a fragile assumption: that benchmark scores directly reflect genuine generalization. In practice, however, such scores may conflate exam-oriented competence with principled capability, especially when contamination and semantic leakage are difficult to exclude from modern training pipelines. We therefore propose an audit framework for analyzing contamination sensitivity and score confidence in LLM benchmarks. Using a router-worker setup, we compare a clean-control condition with noisy conditions in which benchmark problems are systematically deleted, rewritten, and perturbed before being passed downstream. For a genuinely clean benchmark, noisy conditions should not consistently outperform the clean-control baseline. Yet across multiple models, we find widespread but heterogeneous above-baseline gains under noisy conditions, indicating that benchmark-related cues may be reassembled and can reactivate contamination-related memory. These results suggest that similar benchmark scores may carry substantially different levels of confidence. Rather than rejecting benchmarks altogether, we argue that benchmark-based evaluation should be supplemented with explicit audits of contamination sensitivity and score confidence.

</details>


### [169] [Mirage The Illusion of Visual Understanding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21687)
*Mohammad Asadi,Jack W. O'Sullivan,Fang Cao,Tahoura Nedaee,Kamyar Fardi,Fei-Fei Li,Ehsan Adeli,Euan Ashley*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper reveals that multimodal AI models can perform unexpectedly well on vision-language tasks even without images, due to exploiting textual priors and dataset biases, and proposes a new benchmark, B-Clean, to enforce genuinely vision-grounded evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although multimodal AI models appear to reason effectively over images and text, it is unclear whether their performance truly depends on visual understanding or if they are leveraging spurious textual correlations and priors, especially in critical domains like medical imaging.

Method: The authors empirically probe frontier multimodal models under three conditions: (1) normal use where the model is implicitly led to assume images are present, (2) evaluation on standard multimodal benchmarks with no image input at all, and (3) settings where the model is explicitly told it has no image and must guess. They analyze model outputs, scoring on general and medical benchmarks, identify the phenomenon of “mirage reasoning” where models fabricate image-grounded descriptions, and study performance differences between mirage-style prompting and explicit-guess prompting. They then design and introduce B-Clean, a benchmark construction method that removes textual cues enabling non-visual inference to ensure that success requires genuine image grounding.

Result: They find that: (1) Models often produce detailed, image-specific reasoning and even pathology-biased clinical descriptions for images that were never provided, a behavior they call mirage reasoning. (2) Models can still reach high, and in some cases state-of-the-art, scores on established multimodal benchmarks without any image input, including achieving top rank on a chest X-ray QA benchmark. (3) When explicitly instructed that no image is available and that they must guess, model performance drops significantly, indicating that implicit assumptions of image presence trigger a different, overconfident reasoning regime. These results reveal that current benchmarks allow non-visual shortcuts and do not reliably test true visual reasoning.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current visual-language model evaluations are fundamentally flawed because models can exploit textual cues and priors instead of genuine visual understanding, leading to overestimated capabilities and dangerous miscalibration, particularly in medicine. To address this, the authors propose B-Clean, a framework for constructing private, text-cleaned benchmarks that remove non-visual cues, thereby enabling fair, vision-grounded assessment of multimodal AI systems.

Abstract: Multimodal AI systems have achieved remarkable performance across a broad range of real-world tasks, yet the mechanisms underlying visual-language reasoning remain surprisingly poorly understood. We report three findings that challenge prevailing assumptions about how these systems process and integrate visual information. First, Frontier models readily generate detailed image descriptions and elaborate reasoning traces, including pathology-biased clinical findings, for images never provided; we term this phenomenon mirage reasoning. Second, without any image input, models also attain strikingly high scores across general and medical multimodal benchmarks, bringing into question their utility and design. In the most extreme case, our model achieved the top rank on a standard chest X-ray question-answering benchmark without access to any images. Third, when models were explicitly instructed to guess answers without image access, rather than being implicitly prompted to assume images were present, performance declined markedly. Explicit guessing appears to engage a more conservative response regime, in contrast to the mirage regime in which models behave as though images have been provided. These findings expose fundamental vulnerabilities in how visual-language models reason and are evaluated, pointing to an urgent need for private benchmarks that eliminate textual cues enabling non-visual inference, particularly in medical contexts where miscalibrated AI carries the greatest consequence. We introduce B-Clean as a principled solution for fair, vision-grounded evaluation of multimodal AI systems.

They aim to fill this gap by conceptualizing tokens as commodities and designing a futures market to stabilize costs and support large-scale AI adoption.

Method: The paper conceptually frames inference tokens as a commodity and compares them with established commodities such as electricity, carbon allowances, and bandwidth. It then constructs a complete futures contract design around a defined Standard Inference Token (SIT), specifying contract terms, settlement, margin, and market-making arrangements. To quantitatively assess effectiveness, the authors build a mean-reverting jump-diffusion model for token prices and run Monte Carlo simulations to test how well SIT futures hedge cost volatility for application-layer firms under different demand scenarios.

Result: Simulation results indicate that, in scenarios where application-layer demand for inference surges, the proposed token futures significantly reduce the volatility of compute costs for firms, by roughly 62%–78%. This suggests that standardized token futures could be an effective hedging tool against spikes and uncertainty in inference prices.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that inference tokens can be meaningfully treated and structured as a financial commodity, and that standardized token futures built on a SIT definition could stabilize AI compute costs and support risk management for AI-dependent enterprises. They further suggest that similar ideas could extend to GPU compute futures. The paper outlines regulatory considerations and argues that such markets provide a viable pathway for the broader financialization and efficient allocation of compute resources.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) and vision-language-action models (VLAs) become widely deployed, the tokens consumed by AI inference are evolving into a new type of commodity. This paper systematically analyzes the commodity attributes of tokens, arguing for their transition from intelligent service outputs to compute infrastructure raw materials, and draws comparisons with established commodities such as electricity, carbon emission allowances, and bandwidth. Building on the historical experience of electricity futures markets and the theory of commodity financialization, we propose a complete design for standardized token futures contracts, including the definition of a Standard Inference Token (SIT), contract specifications, settlement mechanisms, margin systems, and market-maker regimes. By constructing a mean-reverting jump-diffusion stochastic process model and conducting Monte Carlo simulations, we evaluate the hedging efficiency of the proposed futures contracts for application-layer enterprises. Simulation results show that, under an application-layer demand explosion scenario, token futures can reduce enterprise compute cost volatility by 62%-78%. We also explore the feasibility of GPU compute futures and discuss the regulatory framework for token futures markets, providing a theoretical foundation and practical roadmap for the financialization of compute resources.

*Neelmani Vispute*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing tools (checkpointing, observability, telemetry) don’t provide structured, queryable representations of reasoning decisions, evidence use, and strategy evolution, which are necessary for debugging, governance, and performance optimization at scale.

Method: The authors introduce the Agent Execution Record (AER), a schema-level primitive for structured reasoning provenance. It shows that using AERs enables advanced analytics across large populations of agent runs, including mining reasoning patterns, assessing and calibrating confidence behavior, comparing agents, and running counterfactual regression tests via mock replay.

Conclusion: Reasoning provenance is a distinct and essential concern beyond standard state persistence and observability. The proposed AER primitive provides a structured, queryable representation of agent reasoning that current tooling lacks and that cannot be reliably reconstructed from raw execution state. AERs make it possible to perform rich, population-level behavioral analysis of AI agents and support evaluation, governance, and continuous improvement, and the domain-agnostic model plus reference implementation suggest a practical path to adoption in real-world agent platforms.

Abstract: As AI agents transition from human-supervised copilots to autonomous platform infrastructure, the ability to analyze their reasoning behavior across populations of investigations becomes a pressing infrastructure requirement. Existing operational tooling addresses adjacent needs effectively: state checkpoint systems enable fault tolerance; observability platforms provide execution traces for debugging; telemetry standards ensure interoperability. What current systems do not natively provide as a first-class, schema-level primitive is structured reasoning provenance -- normalized, queryable records of why the agent chose each action, what it concluded from each observation, how each conclusion shaped its strategy, and which evidence supports its final verdict. This paper introduces the Agent Execution Record (AER), a structured reasoning provenance primitive that captures intent, observation, and inference as first-class queryable fields on every step, alongside versioned plans with revision rationale, evidence chains, structured verdicts with confidence scores, and delegation authority chains. We formalize the distinction between computational state persistence and reasoning provenance, argue that the latter cannot in general be faithfully reconstructed from the former, and show how AERs enable population-level behavioral analytics: reasoning pattern mining, confidence calibration, cross-agent comparison, and counterfactual regression testing via mock replay. We present a domain-agnostic model with extensible domain profiles, a reference implementation and SDK, and outline an evaluation methodology informed by preliminary deployment on a production platformized root cause analysis agent.

### [172] [Deterministic Hallucination Detection in Medical VQA via Confidence-Evidence Bayesian Gain](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21693)
*Mohammad Asadi,Tahoura Nedaee,Jack W. O'Sullivan,Euan Ashley,Ehsan Adeli*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes CEBaG, a deterministic and efficient method to detect hallucinations in medical multimodal LLMs by analyzing their internal log-probability patterns, outperforming prior entropy-based methods without needing sampling or external models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Medical VQA using multimodal LLMs can support clinical decision-making, but these models often hallucinate—producing answers that contradict the input image—which is dangerous in clinical use. Existing hallucination detectors like Semantic Entropy and VASE are computationally heavy, requiring many stochastic generations and external NLI models, making them impractical for real-world deployment. There is a need for an accurate, lightweight, self-contained hallucination detector that can work reliably in safety-critical medical settings.

Method: The authors empirically analyze the log-probabilities of MLLM outputs and discover that hallucinated answers show inconsistent token-level confidence and weak dependence on visual input. Based on this, they design Confidence-Evidence Bayesian Gain (CEBaG), which is a deterministic scoring method combining two signals: (1) token-level predictive variance, capturing how much the model’s confidence fluctuates across the tokens in an answer; and (2) evidence magnitude, quantifying how strongly the image influences per-token predictions compared with a text-only variant of the model. CEBaG integrates these into a Bayesian-style gain score to flag hallucinated responses, requiring only a single forward pass without sampling, external NLI models, or tuned task-specific thresholds.

Result: Across four different medical MLLMs and three medical VQA benchmarks, spanning 16 total experimental configurations, CEBaG attains the best hallucination detection AUC in 13 out of 16 settings. On average, it improves AUC by about 8 points relative to the strongest baseline, VASE, while remaining deterministic and more computationally efficient because it avoids multiple stochastic generations and external semantic clustering components.

Conclusion: Hallucinations in medical MLLMs can be effectively detected using signals available directly from the model’s own log-probabilities, without expensive sampling or auxiliary models. CEBaG provides a practical, self-contained, and more accurate hallucination detector for medical VQA, making safety checks more feasible for clinical applications and offering a general template for log-probability-based reliability estimation in multimodal LLMs.

Abstract: Multimodal large language models (MLLMs) have shown strong potential for medical Visual Question Answering (VQA), yet they remain prone to hallucinations, defined as generating responses that contradict the input image, posing serious risks in clinical settings. Current hallucination detection methods, such as Semantic Entropy (SE) and Vision-Amplified Semantic Entropy (VASE), require 10 to 20 stochastic generations per sample together with an external natural language inference model for semantic clustering, making them computationally expensive and difficult to deploy in practice. We observe that hallucinated responses exhibit a distinctive signature directly in the model's own log-probabilities: inconsistent token-level confidence and weak sensitivity to visual evidence. Based on this observation, we propose Confidence-Evidence Bayesian Gain (CEBaG), a deterministic hallucination detection method that requires no stochastic sampling, no external models, and no task-specific hyperparameters. CEBaG combines two complementary signals: token-level predictive variance, which captures inconsistent confidence across response tokens, and evidence magnitude, which measures how much the image shifts per-token predictions relative to text-only inference. Evaluated across four medical MLLMs and three VQA benchmarks (16 experimental settings), CEBaG achieves the highest AUC in 13 of 16 settings and improves over VASE by 8 AUC points on average, while being fully deterministic and self-contained. The code will be made available upon acceptance.

The workflow is designed for a screen-and-escalate deployment model: learned surrogates handle large-scale design ranking, while uncertain or out-of-distribution cases are routed to full CFD. This yields faster aerodynamic design iteration without sacrificing decision-grade reliability, governance traceability, or safety boundaries, and the blueprint is presented as a reusable, auditable pattern for similar industrial modeling tasks.

The method formulates surrogate discovery as constrained optimization over programs, not static model instances, and combines Famou-Agent-style evaluator feedback with population-based island evolution, structured mutations (data, model, loss, and split policies), and multi-objective selection balancing ranking quality, stability, and cost. Across eight anonymized evolutionary operators, the best system reaches a Combined Score of 0.9335 with sign-accuracy 0.9180, while trajectory and ablation analyses show that adaptive sampling and island migration are primary drivers of convergence quality. The deployment model is explicitly ``screen-and-escalate'': surrogates provide high-throughput ranking for design exploration, but low-confidence or out-of-distribution cases are automatically escalated to high-fidelity CFD. ### [175] [Compensating Visual Insufficiency with Stratified Language Guidance for Long-Tail Class Incremental Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21708)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing visual-only methods struggle to robustly represent and preserve tail-class knowledge under these conditions. The authors are motivated to exploit rich, scalable language knowledge as an additional supervisory signal to better structure, guide, and stabilize learning in this challenging setting.

Method: They first analyze the long-tail class incremental (LT CIL) data distribution and use it to prompt large language models (LLMs) to generate a stratified language tree: a hierarchical structure that organizes class semantics from coarse to fine granularity. On top of this tree, they propose two forms of guidance. (1) Stratified adaptive language guidance: learnable weights combine multi-level semantic representations from the tree, allowing the supervision strength and granularity to be dynamically adjusted, especially to better support tail classes and mitigate imbalance. (2) Stratified alignment language guidance: they use the tree’s stable semantic structure as a constraint during optimization to better align visual and language embeddings across levels, which regularizes training and reduces catastrophic forgetting during incremental updates.

Result: On multiple standard benchmarks for long-tail class incremental learning, the proposed method consistently outperforms prior approaches, achieving new state-of-the-art performance. The gains are particularly pronounced on tail classes and in later incremental steps where catastrophic forgetting is typically most severe.

Conclusion: Incorporating structured language knowledge via a stratified semantic tree offers an effective way to tackle long-tail class incremental learning. By adaptively fusing multi-scale language guidance and enforcing stable visual-language alignment over time, the method both strengthens learning of underrepresented tail classes and alleviates catastrophic forgetting. This demonstrates that language-driven hierarchical supervision is a powerful complement to traditional visual learning in dynamic, imbalanced scenarios.

Abstract: Long-tail class incremental learning (LT CIL) remains highly challenging because the scarcity of samples in tail classes not only hampers their learning but also exacerbates catastrophic forgetting under continuously evolving and imbalanced data distributions. To tackle these issues, we exploit the informativeness and scalability of language knowledge. Specifically, we analyze the LT CIL data distribution to guide large language models (LLMs) in generating a stratified language tree that hierarchically organizes semantic information from coarse to fine grained granularity. Building upon this structure, we introduce stratified adaptive language guidance, which leverages learnable weights to merge multi-scale semantic representations, thereby enabling dynamic supervisory adjustment for tail classes and alleviating the impact of data imbalance. Furthermore, we introduce stratified alignment language guidance, which exploits the structural stability of the language tree to constrain optimization and reinforce semantic visual alignment, thereby alleviating catastrophic forgetting. Extensive experiments on multiple benchmarks demonstrate that our method achieves state of the art performance.

### [176] [CurvZO: Adaptive Curvature-Guided Sparse Zeroth-Order Optimization for Efficient LLM Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21725)
*Shuo Wang,Ziyu Chen,Ming Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CurvZO is a memory-efficient, sparse zeroth-order fine-tuning method for LLMs that uses online curvature estimates from scalar feedback to adaptively choose which parameters to perturb and how much to perturb them, improving accuracy and speed over prior ZO baselines while keeping memory low.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Backpropagation-based fine-tuning of LLMs achieves strong performance but requires storing activations and gradients, creating large memory overhead that limits scalability on resource-constrained hardware. Zeroth-order optimization avoids backpropagation and uses only forward passes, making it memory-efficient, but it suffers from high-variance gradient estimates that lead to slow or unstable convergence. Sparse ZO methods help by perturbing only a subset of parameters, but their success depends critically on choosing informative parameters, which is difficult because each ZO query provides only scalar feedback and thus limited signal about which coordinates matter. There is therefore a need for a ZO fine-tuning method that remains memory-efficient while using more informative, adaptive parameter selection to improve convergence and performance.

Method: The authors introduce Adaptive Curvature-Guided Sparse Zeroth-Order Optimization (CurvZO). CurvZO extracts curvature-related signals online from scalar zeroth-order feedback obtained during optimization. Using these signals, it builds a parameter-wise sampling distribution that determines which coordinates (parameters) to perturb in each sparse ZO update, prioritizing those expected to be most informative. This reduces the variance of the sparse ZO gradient estimator. Additionally, CurvZO adaptively adjusts the perturbation budget over time, based on the evolving distribution of curvature signals, so that updates remain targeted on important parameters while retaining enough exploration to avoid getting stuck in suboptimal regions. The overall method is implemented for fine-tuning large language models with ZO optimization while maintaining memory efficiency.

Result: In experiments on OPT and LLaMA models across a range of NLP tasks, CurvZO consistently outperforms existing ZO optimization baselines for LLM fine-tuning. It yields accuracy improvements of up to 4.4 percentage points and reduces training time by up to a factor of 2, while retaining the memory-efficiency benefits of zeroth-order methods (since it still relies only on forward passes and sparse parameter perturbations).

Conclusion: CurvZO demonstrates that curvature-guided, adaptively sparse zeroth-order optimization can significantly improve the practical viability of memory-efficient fine-tuning for large language models. By leveraging curvature information inferred from scalar feedback to guide parameter selection and perturbation budgets, CurvZO reduces gradient estimator variance, leading to better accuracy and faster convergence than prior ZO approaches without sacrificing memory savings. This makes ZO-based fine-tuning more competitive with backpropagation in resource-constrained settings.

Abstract: Fine-tuning large language models (LLMs) with backpropagation achieves high performance but incurs substantial memory overhead, limiting scalability on resource-constrained hardware. Zeroth-order (ZO) optimization provides a memory-efficient alternative by relying solely on forward passes, yet it typically suffers from slow or unstable convergence due to high-variance gradient estimates. Sparse ZO updates partially address this issue by perturbing only a subset of parameters, but their effectiveness hinges on selecting informative parameters, which is challenging in ZO optimization because each query yields only scalar feedback. We propose \textbf{Adaptive Curvature-Guided Sparse Zeroth-Order Optimization (CurvZO)}, which tracks curvature signals online from scalar ZO feedback and leverages these signals to construct a parameter-wise sampling distribution for selecting coordinates at each update, reducing the variance of the sparse ZO gradient estimator. Moreover, CurvZO dynamically adapts the perturbation budget to the evolving curvature signal distribution, yielding sparse ZO updates that remain both focused and sufficiently exploratory. Extensive experiments on OPT and Llama across diverse NLP tasks show that CurvZO consistently improves fine-tuning performance and reduces training time over ZO baselines. It improves accuracy by up to 4.4 points and achieves up to a $2\times$ speedup, while preserving memory efficiency.

Abstract: Scientific idea generation is a cornerstone of autonomous knowledge discovery, yet the iterative evolution required to transform initial concepts into high-quality research proposals remains a formidable challenge for Large Language Models (LLMs). Conversely, language-based refinement methods are typically confined to inference-time prompting, targeting models that are not explicitly optimized to internalize such critiques. By integrating these signals into the RL loop, we condition the policy to systematically utilize precise feedback during both optimization and inference. Extensive experiments demonstrate that EvoIdeator, built on Qwen3-4B, significantly outperforms much larger frontier models across key scientific metrics.
