### [178] [The Reasoning Error About Reasoning: Why Different Types of Reasoning Require Different Representational Structures](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21736)
*Yiling Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a unified structural framework for understanding what kinds of internal representations are required for different types of reasoning, showing a sharp boundary between associative/probabilistic reasoning and fully deductive reasoning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Across psychology, AI, and philosophy of mind, there is no systematic, cross-disciplinary account of what structural properties a representational system must have to support different reasoning abilities. Debates over symbolic vs. neural, probabilistic vs. logical systems often talk past each other because they lack a shared structural vocabulary. The author wants a principled way to say which kinds of representations can, even in principle, support which kinds of reasoning, and why scaling up statistical learning may not suffice for some reasoning types.

Method: The author develops a conceptual framework that characterizes representational systems in terms of four structural properties: (1) operability (the system supports the necessary operations on representations), (2) consistency (it avoids certain incompatible states), (3) structural preservation (it respects relevant structural relationships across transformations), and (4) compositionality (complex representations can be systematically built from simpler parts). The paper analyzes how different kinds of reasoning—induction, analogy, causal reasoning, deduction, formal logic—differ in the degree to which they require each property, and which reasoning failures result when a property is not met. The framework is then qualitatively compared with evidence from AI benchmarks, developmental studies, and cognitive neuroscience to see whether observed capabilities and deficits align with the proposed structural demands.

Result: The analysis identifies a key structural boundary in reasoning types. Below this boundary, forms of reasoning (e.g., many inductive and analogical inferences) can be implemented with associative, probabilistic representations that need not fully satisfy all four properties. Above the boundary, especially for deductive and formal logical reasoning, a system must satisfy operability, consistency, structural preservation, and compositionality to a strong degree. The paper argues that no amount of scaling a purely statistical/associative system, without reorganizing its representational structure, can deliver the guarantees required for these higher forms of reasoning. Evidence from AI (e.g., scaling behavior of large models), developmental trajectories in children, and neural data is interpreted as broadly consistent with the proposed distinctions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that certain structural properties of internal representations are necessary conditions for different reasoning abilities, with deductive and logical reasoning requiring a full package of operability, consistency, structural preservation, and compositionality. These properties mark a principled boundary that associative, probabilistic systems alone cannot cross merely by scale. The framework is deliberately neutral about whether representations are symbolic, neural, or hybrid, and it is offered as a way to reorganize and clarify ongoing debates rather than to settle them. It yields concrete, testable predictions—such as compounding degradation of performance when multiple structural properties are weakened, selective sensitivity to targeted disruptions of specific properties, and the inability of mere scaling to reproduce reasoning types that require strong structural guarantees.

Abstract: Different types of reasoning impose different structural demands on representational systems, yet no systematic account of these demands exists across psychology, AI, and philosophy of mind. I propose a framework identifying four structural properties of representational systems: operability, consistency, structural preservation, and compositionality. These properties are demanded to different degrees by different forms of reasoning, from induction through analogy and causal inference to deduction and formal logic. Each property excludes a distinct class of reasoning failure. The analysis reveals a principal structural boundary: reasoning types below it can operate on associative, probabilistic representations, while those above it require all four properties to be fully satisfied. Scaling statistical learning without structural reorganization is insufficient to cross this boundary, because the structural guarantees required by deductive reasoning cannot be approximated through probabilistic means. Converging evidence from AI evaluation, developmental psychology, and cognitive neuroscience supports the framework at different levels of directness. Three testable predictions are derived, including compounding degradation, selective vulnerability to targeted structural disruption, and irreducibility under scaling. The framework is a necessary-condition account, agnostic about representational format, that aims to reorganize existing debates rather than close them.

</details>


### [179] [The Presupposition Problem in Representation Genesis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21745)
*Yiling Wu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that existing philosophical theories cannot adequately explain how representation first arises in cognitive systems, a problem made urgent by large language models (LLMs).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address the foundational question of how non-representational physical systems become representational—i.e., how representation genesis occurs—and to examine why current philosophical frameworks fail to explain this transition, especially in light of LLMs that show high performance without a clear representational genesis story.

Method: Conceptual and structural analysis of major frameworks in philosophy of mind (Language of Thought, teleosemantics, predictive processing, enactivism, genetic phenomenology), identifying a shared structural feature—the Representation Presupposition—and deriving its consequences for explanatory projects about the origin of representation.

Result: The paper finds that all surveyed frameworks, when applied to representation genesis, at some point rely on notions that already presuppose a system organized as a representer, leading to what the authors call the Representation Presupposition and a consequent Representation Regress that blocks a non-circular account of the first emergence of contentful representation.

Conclusion: No existing major framework in philosophy of mind currently provides a non-circular explanation of how representation first arises; they all defer explanation by importing representational notions into the very account that is supposed to explain their origin. The authors articulate this structural problem and propose two minimal adequacy conditions that any future account of representation genesis must meet, noting that LLMs make solving this problem practically significant rather than purely theoretical.

Abstract: Large language models are the first systems to achieve high cognitive performance without clearly undergoing representation genesis: the transition from a non-representing physical system to one whose states guide behavior in a content-sensitive way. Prior cognitive systems had already made this transition before we could examine it, and philosophy of mind treated genesis as a background condition rather than an explanatory target. LLMs provide a case that does not clearly involve this transition, making the genesis question newly urgent: if genesis did not occur, which cognitive capacities are affected, and why? We currently lack the conceptual resources to answer this. The reason, this paper argues, is structural. Major frameworks in philosophy of mind, including the Language of Thought hypothesis, teleosemantics, predictive processing, enactivism, and genetic phenomenology, share a common feature when applied to the genesis question: at some explanatory step, each deploys concepts whose explanatory purchase depends on the system already being organized as a representer. This pattern, which we call the Representation Presupposition structure, generates systematic explanatory deferral. Attempts to explain the first acquisition of content-manipulable representation within the existing categorical vocabulary import resources from the representational side of the transition itself. We call this the Representation Regress. The paper offers a conceptual diagnosis rather than a new theory, establishing the structure of the problem and deriving two minimum adequacy conditions for any account that avoids this pattern. LLMs make the absence of such a theory consequential rather than merely theoretical.

  <summary>Details</summary>

They implement and train persona-conditioned explanation policies in a drug discovery setting, using limited expert feedback to instantiate and refine two distinct personas and then using RL to optimize explanations under each persona.

Additionally, persona-based training cut the amount of required expert feedback by roughly two orders of magnitude while still producing high-quality, expert-aligned explanations.

Conclusion: Agentic personas—explicit models of expert reasoning styles—enable scalable, adaptive explainability for AI systems in complex, high-stakes domains. By conditioning a reinforcement learning-based explanation agent on these personas, the system can match expert epistemic preferences, maintain strong predictive performance, and drastically reduce the need for expensive expert feedback. This approach demonstrates a practical path toward personalized, context-sensitive explanations for scientific AI tools, with potential for broader application across other complex decision-making domains.

Abstract: AI explanation methods often assume a static user model, producing non-adaptive explanations regardless of expert goals, reasoning strategies, or decision contexts. Knowledge graph-based explanations, despite their capacity for grounded, path-based reasoning, inherit this limitation. In complex domains such as scientific discovery, this assumption fails to capture the diversity of cognitive strategies and epistemic stances among experts, preventing explanations that foster deeper understanding and informed decision-making. However, the scarcity of human experts limits the use of direct human feedback to produce adaptive explanations.
  We present a reinforcement learning approach for scientific explanation generation that incorporates agentic personas, structured representations of expert reasoning strategies, that guide the explanation agent towards specific epistemic preferences. In an evaluation of knowledge graph-based explanations for drug discovery, we tested two personas that capture distinct epistemic stances derived from expert feedback.
  Results show that persona-driven explanations match state-of-the-art predictive performance while persona preferences closely align with those of their corresponding experts. Adaptive explanations were consistently preferred over non-adaptive baselines (n = 22), and persona-based training reduces feedback requirements by two orders of magnitude. These findings demonstrate how agentic personas enable scalable adaptive explainability for AI systems in complex and high-stakes domains.

### [181] [Reasoning or Rhetoric? An Empirical Analysis of Moral Reasoning Explanations in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.21854)
*Aryan Kasat,Smriti Singh,Aman Chadha,Vinija Jain*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper examines whether large language models truly engage in moral reasoning or just mimic it, finding that they mainly reproduce advanced moral rhetoric without showing human-like developmental progression and often display internal incoherence between moral principles and choices.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To test the widespread assumption that LLMs can reason about morality in a human-like way, and to determine whether their apparent moral sophistication reflects genuine underlying reasoning structures (e.g., Kohlberg’s stages) or surface-level alignment to human-like moral language.

Method: The authors apply an LLM-as-judge scoring pipeline (validated across three different judge models) to classify over 600 responses generated by 13 LLMs of varying architectures, sizes, and training regimes. The models respond to six classical moral dilemmas, and their outputs are scored according to Kohlberg’s stages of moral development. Ten complementary analyses are conducted to examine stage distributions, consistency across dilemmas, relationships with scale and training type, and coherence between stated justifications and chosen actions.

Result: Most responses are classified as post-conventional (Kohlberg Stages 5–6) irrespective of scale, architecture, or prompting, inverting typical human patterns where Stage 4 is dominant. Some models show “moral decoupling”: their stated moral justifications do not align with their chosen actions, indicating logical incoherence. Model size has a statistically significant but small effect, training regime has no independent main effect, and models show highly uniform, nearly “robotic” consistency across different dilemmas, often producing logically indistinguishable answers to distinct moral problems.

Conclusion: The observed patterns suggest that current LLMs do not exhibit human-like moral developmental trajectories but instead display what the authors call “moral ventriloquism”: alignment training teaches them to reproduce the language and structure of mature moral reasoning without the underlying developmental basis or fully coherent reasoning processes. This challenges interpretations of LLM moral outputs as evidence of genuine moral cognition.

Abstract: Do large language models reason morally, or do they merely sound like they do? We investigate whether LLM responses to moral dilemmas exhibit genuine developmental progression through Kohlberg's stages of moral development, or whether alignment training instead produces reasoning-like outputs that superficially resemble mature moral judgment without the underlying developmental trajectory. Using an LLM-as-judge scoring pipeline validated across three judge models, we classify more than 600 responses from 13 LLMs spanning a range of architectures, parameter scales, and training regimes across six classical moral dilemmas, and conduct ten complementary analyses to characterize the nature and internal coherence of the resulting patterns. Our results reveal a striking inversion: responses overwhelmingly correspond to post-conventional reasoning (Stages 5-6) regardless of model size, architecture, or prompting strategy, the effective inverse of human developmental norms, where Stage 4 dominates. Most strikingly, a subset of models exhibit moral decoupling: systematic inconsistency between stated moral justification and action choice, a form of logical incoherence that persists across scale and prompting strategy and represents a direct reasoning consistency failure independent of rhetorical sophistication. Model scale carries a statistically significant but practically small effect; training type has no significant independent main effect; and models exhibit near-robotic cross-dilemma consistency producing logically indistinguishable responses across semantically distinct moral problems. We posit that these patterns constitute evidence for moral ventriloquism: the acquisition, through alignment training, of the rhetorical conventions of mature moral reasoning without the underlying developmental trajectory those conventions are meant to represent.

### [184] [Future-Interactions-Aware Trajectory Prediction via Braid Theory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.22035)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous vehicles must anticipate the future motions of many interacting agents (cars, pedestrians, cyclists) simultaneously. Existing multi-agent prediction models either become computationally heavy when modeling interactions or use hand-crafted heuristics to categorize social behaviors, which can be brittle and non-generalizable. There is a need for a compact, principled representation of multi-agent coordination that improves joint prediction quality without significantly increasing complexity.

Method: They leverage braid theory, which represents multi-agent interactions as braids capturing how agent trajectories cross over time. Instead of using braids only to focus attention or as a weak prior, they introduce a new auxiliary task: braid prediction. In parallel with standard trajectory prediction, the model predicts the crossing type (edge labels) between agent pairs in the braid representation. This auxiliary classification task is integrated into training but keeps the base trajectory prediction architecture largely unchanged, adding negligible computational overhead. The braid labels encode coordination modes and future crossing structure, encouraging the learned representations to capture social and intention-aware structure in the data.

Result: Models augmented with the braid prediction auxiliary task achieve notable improvements on joint prediction metrics across three different datasets, indicating better consistency and realism in the multi-agent forecasts. The added braid head does not significantly increase training or inference time, showing that the gains come with minimal complexity overhead. Qualitative analysis suggests the model better respects coordinated behaviors and future crossing patterns between agents.

Conclusion: Explicitly predicting braid structures—how agents will cross each other—serves as an effective auxiliary signal that injects social and intention awareness into multi-agent trajectory prediction models. This braid-based conditioning improves joint prediction accuracy without substantial computational cost, demonstrating braid theory as a practical, powerful descriptor for multi-agent behavior in autonomous driving contexts. The approach is simple to adopt and generalizes across multiple datasets, suggesting it can be a useful addition to existing prediction architectures.

Abstract: To safely operate, an autonomous vehicle must know the future behavior of a potentially high number of interacting agents around it, a task often posed as multi-agent trajectory prediction. Many previous attempts to model social interactions and solve the joint prediction task either add extensive computational requirements or rely on heuristics to label multi-agent behavior types. Braid theory, in contrast, provides a powerful exact descriptor of multi-agent behavior by projecting future trajectories into braids that express how trajectories cross with each other over time; a braid then corresponds to a specific mode of coordination between the multiple agents in the future. In past work, braids have been used lightly to reason about interacting agents and restrict the attention window of predicted agents. We show that leveraging more fully the expressivity of the braid representation and using it to condition the trajectories themselves leads to even further gains in joint prediction performance, with negligible added complexity either in training or at inference time. We do so by proposing a novel auxiliary task, braid prediction, done in parallel with the trajectory prediction task. By classifying edges between agents into their correct crossing types in the braid representation, the braid prediction task is able to imbue the model with improved social awareness, which is reflected in joint predictions that more closely adhere to the actual multi-agent behavior. This simple auxiliary task allowed us to obtain significant improvements in joint metrics on three separate datasets. We show how the braid prediction task infuses the model with future intention awareness, leading to more accurate joint predictions. Code is available at github.com/caiocj1/traj-pred-braid-theory.

Result: On an IT automation task—representative of enterprise use cases—the framework yields consistent and substantial performance improvements over baseline LLM agents across many evaluation scenarios, indicating effective policy improvement from offline logs and robustness to data quality issues.

By abstracting agent reasoning into a DT-MDP, learning rewards and policies from mixed-quality logs, and using those to engineer better contexts, it achieves significant empirical gains and is expected to transfer to other similar enterprise agent settings.

To address these challenges, we propose a lightweight, model-agnostic framework for improving LLM-based enterprise agents via offline reinforcement learning (RL). The proposed Context Engineering via DT-MDP (DT-MDP-CE) framework comprises three key components: (1) A Digital-Twin Markov Decision Process (DT-MDP), which abstracts the agent's reasoning behavior as a finite MDP; (2) A robust contrastive inverse RL, which, armed with the DT-MDP, to efficiently estimate a well-founded reward function and induces policies from mixed-quality offline trajectories; and (3) RL-guided context engineering, which uses the policy obtained from the integrated process of (1) and (2), to improve the agent's decision-making behavior. As a case study, we apply the framework to a representative task in the enterprise-oriented domain of IT automation. Extensive experimental results demonstrate consistent and significant improvements over baseline agents across a wide range of evaluation settings, suggesting that the framework can generalize to other agents sharing similar characteristics in enterprise environments.

Motivation: Human interpretation of complex cardiac tests is a bottleneck in cardiovascular care, and existing AI vision-language models are limited to single modalities, static inputs, and are prone to spurious or hallucinated reasoning. There is a need for an interactive, multimodal system that can jointly interpret multiple cardiac imaging and signal modalities with high accuracy and reliability.

Method: The authors design MARCUS, a hierarchical agentic architecture with modality-specific vision-language expert models for ECG, echocardiography, and CMR. Each expert combines domain-trained visual encoders with multi-stage language model optimization. A multimodal orchestrator coordinates these experts to handle single-modality and multimodal inputs, enabling autonomous reasoning and interactive chat. The system is trained on 13.5M images and an expert-curated dataset of 1.6M QA pairs.

Result: MARCUS achieves state-of-the-art accuracies on internal (Stanford) and external (UCSF) cohorts: 87–91% for ECG, 67–86% for echocardiography, and 85–88% for CMR, outperforming leading frontier models by 34–45% with statistically significant improvements (P<0.001). For multimodal cases, MARCUS reaches 70% accuracy versus 22–28% for frontier models, and delivers 1.7–3.0x higher free-text report quality scores. The architecture also reduces mirage reasoning, where models rely on unintended textual cues or hallucinated visual content.

Conclusion: A hierarchical, agentic vision-language architecture with domain-specific visual encoders and a multimodal orchestrator can deliver robust, state-of-the-art multimodal cardiac test interpretation, substantially surpassing general-purpose frontier models. Open-sourcing the models, code, and benchmarks enables broader validation, extension, and clinical translation of multimodal AI systems for cardiovascular care.

Abstract: Cardiovascular disease remains the leading cause of global mortality, with progress hindered by human interpretation of complex cardiac tests. Current AI vision-language models are limited to single-modality inputs and are non-interactive. We present MARCUS (Multimodal Autonomous Reasoning and Chat for Ultrasound and Signals), an agentic vision-language system for end-to-end interpretation of electrocardiograms (ECGs), echocardiograms, and cardiac magnetic resonance imaging (CMR) independently and as multimodal input. MARCUS employs a hierarchical agentic architecture comprising modality-specific vision-language expert models, each integrating domain-trained visual encoders with multi-stage language model optimization, coordinated by a multimodal orchestrator. Trained on 13.5 million images (0.25M ECGs, 1.3M echocardiogram images, 12M CMR images) and our novel expert-curated dataset spanning 1.6 million questions, MARCUS achieves state-of-the-art performance surpassing frontier models (GPT-5 Thinking, Gemini 2.5 Pro Deep Think). Across internal (Stanford) and external (UCSF) test cohorts, MARCUS achieves accuracies of 87-91% for ECG, 67-86% for echocardiography, and 85-88% for CMR, outperforming frontier models by 34-45% (P<0.001). On multimodal cases, MARCUS achieved 70% accuracy, nearly triple that of frontier models (22-28%), with 1.7-3.0x higher free-text quality scores. Our agentic architecture also confers resistance to mirage reasoning, whereby vision-language models derive reasoning from unintended textual signals or hallucinated visual content. MARCUS demonstrates that domain-specific visual encoders with an agentic orchestrator enable multimodal cardiac interpretation. We release our models, code, and benchmark open-source.

</details>
